History of CCB

Combined validation with VCS 
Gold Level Criteria 
Gold Level for Climate Change Adaptation Benefits (GL1), Exceptional 
Benefits for Community (GL2) and Biodiversity (GL3) 
GL1: The project will implement activities that increase the resilience of 
local communities and biodiversity to climate change, including activities 
for livelihood diversification and habitat conservation and restoration. 
GL2: A total of 13 villages adjacent to the project area are included as the 
target beneficiaries of this project, including six villages classified as 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
2 
underdeveloped villages. Over 50% of villagers in the project zone are 
below the national poverty line. The project will provide tangible benefits to 
local communities living in the surrounding area by working to improve 
local livelihoods, health, education, and well-being. 
GL3: The project area is home to 31 globally threatened species, including 
the Bornean orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus wurmbii), Proboscis monkey 
(Nasalis larvatus), and the Bornean white-bearded gibbon (Hylobates 
albibarbis). The project activities will contribute to the conservation of 
these species and their habitat, and restore the degraded peatland and 
mangroves to improve the habitat quality and reduce the threats to 
biodiversity. 
Expected Verification 
Schedule  
n/a

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
The PLUM  Peat  and  Mangrove  Conservation  and Restoration  Project  (PLUM  Project) is  a  conservation 
and  restoration  project  of  peat  and  mangrove  forests  located  in  Central  Kalimantan  Province,  Indonesia. 
The  project  is implemented on  a  forest  concession  with  Forest  Utilization  Business  License held by  PT 
Pagatan Usaha Makmur (PT PUM) for 60 years, covering a total area of 23,665 hectares. Prior to PT PUM 
receiving its principal PBPH license in 2022
1
, the project area was planned to be developed as an oil palm 
plantation along with two other companies adjacent to the area. 
The project is expected to have a range of climate, community and biodiversity benefits by preserving and 
restoring the integrity of the Peat Swamp and Mangrove Forest landscape and the rich biodiversity in the 
area while improving the well-being of local communities. In terms of climate benefits, it is estimated the 
PLUM Project will result in net emissions reductions and removals of 69,999,940 tCO
2
e over 60 years of 
crediting  period. This  will  be  achieved  through activities  covered  by  the  VM0007 REDD+  Methodology 
Framework including  (1)  Avoiding  Planned  Deforestation  (APD),  (2)  Afforestation,  Reforestation  and 
Revegetation (ARR), (3) Restoration of Wetlands Ecosystems (RWE) on Peatland and Tidal Wetlands, and 
(4)  Avoiding  Planned  Wetland  Degradation  (APWD)  on  Peatland  and  Tidal  Wetlands. Additionally,  the 
project will deliver Climate Change Adaptation Benefits by increasing the resilience of local communities 
and biodiversity to climate change. 
The project meets  the  CCB  criteria  for  Exceptional  Community  Benefits.  There  are  13  villages  within  the 
project  zone  and over  50%  of  villagers  are  below  the  national  poverty  line  of IDR 2,120,371/month  per 
household. Furthermore, six of the 13 villages in the project zone are underdeveloped villages. These six 
villages  are  the  main  target  of  the  project’s  community  development  programs.  The  project  activities  will 
target  the  most  vulnerable  communities  within  the  project  zone,  improve  access  to  health  services  and 
provide  fair  and  just  employment  opportunities.  The  project  activities  will  provide  a  positive  impact  on 
communities’ livelihoods and well-being.  
The project also meets the CCB criteria for Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits. PT PUM’s ecosystems are 
home  to  31 globally threatened  species with three Critically  Endangered  (CR)  species  (i.e.,  Bornean 
orangutan), eight Endangered (EN) species (i.e., Proboscis monkey, Bornean white-bearded gibbon), and 
20 Vulnerable (VU) species. Furthermore, the project area is home to restricted range species that can only 
be seen on Kalimantan Island including four endemic mammals and one endemic bird. The project activities 
will contribute to  the  conservation  of  these species  and their habitat,  and  restore  the  degraded  peatland 
and mangroves to improve the habitat quality and reduce the threats to biodiversity. 
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
 
Outcome or Impact Estimated by the End of Project Lifetime 
Section 
Reference 
1) A total of 21,010 ha peatland will be conserved and restored through peat rewetting 
(e.g., canal blocking) and fire prevention and management activities. This effort will allow 
degraded areas to recover and hence provide suitable habitats for key species. In 
addition, negative health impacts from fire smoke and haze to local communities will be 
reduced. 
2.1.11, 
3.2.2 
 
1
 Principal license granted to PT PUM by the Ministry of Investment since 1 August 2022 (Decision of the Head of 
Investment Coordination No.01082211116206001), followed by the definitive license on November 27
th
, 2023.

2 GENERAL 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (G1.2) 
The  PLUM  Peat  and  Mangrove  Conservation  and  Restoration  Project  (PLUM  Project)  is  a  conservation 
and  restoration  project of  peat  and  mangrove  forests located  in  Central  Kalimantan  Province,  Indonesia. 
The project is implemented on a 23,665-hectare forest concession with Forest Utilization Business License, 
held by PT Pagatan Usaha Makmur (PT PUM) for 60 years. The project will implement activities to conserve 
and  restore  the  peat  and  mangrove  forests  in  the  project  area  while delivering positive  impact  on  the 
community  and  biodiversity.  Among  others,  the  project  will  implement  forest  patrols,  fire  prevention,  and 
biodiversity  monitoring  activities  to  prevent  forest  loss  and  protect  biodiversity.  Peat  ecosystems  will  be 
restored by blocking canals and rewetting the area to avoid further peat drainage. Approximately 15,088 
ha of degraded land will be restored through reforestation and Assisted Natural Regeneration (ANR). 
The eastern part of the project is a peatland forest area located in Katingan Regency, Katingan Kuala Sub-
district,  adjacent  to  the  Sebangau  National  Park.  The  western  part  of  the  concession  is  located  in  East 
Kotawaringin  Regency,  Pulau  Hanaut  Sub-district,  and  mostly  consists  of  mangrove  forests  (Map 2-1). 
Based  on  the  conducted  biodiversity  assessment,  the  concession provides  important  habitat  for  31 
threatened  species,  including  the  Bornean  orangutan,  the  Proboscis  monkey,  and  the  Bornean  white-
bearded gibbon. The area is also important for at least 17 migratory bird species from three families. 
The project will provide tangible benefits to local communities living in the surrounding area by working to 
improve local livelihoods, health, education, and well-being. For example, the project will assist local coffee 
farmers and honey  producers  to  improve  harvesting,  processing,  and  market  access.  The  project  will 
support the establishment of women’s groups in the target villages to promote economic opportunities for 
women; and implement interventions that improve health and sanitation in the 13 target villages. 
Prior to PT PUM receiving its principal PBPH license in 2022, the project area was planned to be developed 
as an oil palm plantation along with two other companies adjacent to the area. All three companies were 
owned by a company group named PT Agro Inti Semesta (PT AIS). Additionally, in 2022, an approval to 
convert forest land into oil palm plantation was given to another oil palm company located along the western 
boundary of PT PUM. Without  the  PLUM  Project,  it  is  highly  likely  that  PT  PUM’s  area  would  have been 
developed into an oil palm plantation by PT AIS. 
The project will generate GHG emission reductions or removals by implementing activities covered by the 
VM0007  REDD+  Methodology  Framework  including  (1)  Avoiding  Planned  Deforestation  (APD),  (2) 
Afforestation, Reforestation and Revegetation (ARR), (3) Restoration of Wetlands Ecosystems (RWE) on 
Peatland  and  Tidal  Wetlands,  and  (4)  Avoiding  Planned  Wetland  Degradation  (APWD)  on  Peatland  and 
Tidal Wetlands. It is estimated that the project will generate a total net Verified Carbon Unit (VCU) volume 
of 69,999,940 tCO
2
e over 60 years of crediting period or 1,166,666 tCO
2
e per annum (Table 3-44). 
The project’s goal is to conserve the remaining forest, restore ecosystem function, protect biodiversity, and 
improve the well-being of communities to become resilient to climate change. In conclusion, it is expected 
that the PLUM Project will contribute to preserving the integrity of the Peat Swamp and Mangrove Forest 
landscape and biodiversity in the area while improving the well-being of local communities.

2.1.5 Physical Parameters (G1.3) 
The  project  area  is  located  in two  regencies  of Central  Kalimantan  Province,  namely  Katingan  Regency 
and East Kotawaringin Regency. The sub-districts that are the focus of the project activities are Katingan 
Kuala sub-district of Katingan Regency and Pulau Hanaut sub-district of East Kotawaringin Regency. The 
project area lies between 113°9'00'' - 113°10'00'' east longitude and 3°12'00'' - 3°2'00'' south latitude. The 
eastern  part  of  project  area  is  adjacent  to  the  Sebangau  National  Park,  the  third  largest  national  park  in 
Kalimantan
14
. The physical parameters of the project are described below: 
2.1.5.1 Topography and Slope 
The topography of the project area shows that the entire project area is located in the lowlands and flat with 
the dominant altitude class 0-15 m above sea level, with 0-8% slope class, and without steep hilly areas.  
2.1.5.2 Soil 
Soil type in project area is formed by entisol, inceptisol, and histosol
15
. The majority of soil type in the project 
area is histosol. Histosol soil is divided into three ordos namely tropohemist, tropofibrist, and troposaprist. 
Histosol is the most common soil type in the project area (74% of total area). This soil type is found in the 
northern and central parts of the project area (peat swamp areas). The peat in this area is generally quite 
fertile due to mineral enrichment from the overflow of the Katingan River to the west and the Kaki River to 
 
14
 The Sebangau National Park wide area is around 542,141 hectares with rich biodiversity counting 808 species of 
flora, 35 species of mammals, 182 species of birds, and 54 species of snakes. 
15
 Laporan Survey Flora dan Fauna (Flora Fauna Survey Report) - IUPHHK Restorasi Ekosistem PT. Pagatan Usaha 
Makmur. Kabupaten Katingan - Kalimantan Tengah (Remark Asia, 2022). 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
19 
the east. The project area forms part of two Peat Hydrological Units (Kesatuan Hidrologis Gambut – KHG)
16
, 
namely the Kahayan River – Sebangau River (KHG 62.11-14.02) and the Katingan River – Sebangau River 
(KHG 62.02-06.01). 
Meanwhile, the entisol and inceptisol soils are spread across the central and southern parts of the project 
area with a coverage of 25.9% of the total area. Poor drainage conditions and moderate soil solum in this 
soil group association indicate that this area has a low water absorption capacity so that during the rainy 
season this area is often flooded. Finally, approximately 0.1% of the project area is tidal swamp area. The 
soil in this area contains sulphide material (pyrite), which, when oxidized, causes high acidity level (pH 3.5-
4). This condition can damage plants. However, under anaerobic (flooded) conditions, the pyrite content in 
the soil is not harmful. Therefore, managing this area would require sound water management.

2.1.5.3 Climate 
The project area and its surroundings have a tropical rainforest climate. It is usually warm, humid, and rainy 
all year round. The temperature averages are more stable throughout the year, with an average of 26.9°C 
(min 22.5°C, max 33.2°C), and an average humidity of 85.2%. The highest monthly average rainfall occurs 
during the DJF (Dec) period, with rainfall averaging > 400 mm/month. The lowest monthly rainfall occurs 
during the JJA period (Aug), averaging around 100 mm/month. Thus, based on the data, the project area 
is classified as a wet area throughout the year. 
 
Figure 2-1. Monthly precipitation and average temperature in the project area 
Source: BMKG Meteorological Station H. Asan, WMO ID: 96651, from 2012 to 2022` 
2.1.5.4 Hydrology 
River basins are ecological systems that consist of biophysical as well as social components. There are 
two river basins in the project area, namely 1) Katingan and 2) Sungai Kaki. The Katingan river basin is 
located in the western part, while the Sungai Kaki River basin is located in the eastern part of the project 
area. Some of the rivers in the project area flow to the west and east and end in the Katingan River; 
others flow southward and end in the sea.

2.1.5.5 Land Cover and Vegetation Type 
35% of the land in the project area is covered by peat swamp and mangrove forests and the remaining area 
is covered by non-forests with domination of wet shrub (36%) (Table 2-1). The peat swamp forest includes 
primary and secondary forest, part of which has been exploited but is still in good condition and has a dense 
canopy cover. Fallen trees can be found at a number of points therein and a fairly easy-to-pass trail which 
is  suspected  to  be  an  earlier  exploitation. Plant  species  that  dominate  the peat  swamp  ecosystem are 
Malam-malam    (Diospyros areolata),    Pisangpisang    (Mezzetia  parviflora),    Bintangur    (Calophyllum 
inophyllum), Katiau (Madhuca motleyana), and Jambu-jambu (Syzygium sp.). These species are generally 
found in the secondary forest and in abundance.  
In contrast, the mangrove forest is pre-dominantly in a pristine condition. In the western part of the project 
area,  primary  mangrove  forest,  beach  forest  and  peatlands  can  be  found. The Cemeti  mangrove  forest 
landscape  has  reached  a  climax  succession; the  mangrove  stands  are  dominated  by Rhizopora stylosa 
which is even and dense, with trees up to 15 meters high and more than 20 centimeters in diameter. Other 
types  of  mangroves  that  can  be  found  include Sonneratia alba, Avicennia alba and Avicennia marina.  A 
large population of Nipa palm (Nypa fruticans) can be found 500 meters from the estuary mouth where the 
tides  are  not  too  high.  Meanwhile,  other mangrove associate species  such  as Cerbera  odollam and 
Barringtonia asiatica can also be found.

2.1.6 Social Parameters (G1.3) 
The project zone consists of an eastern part and a western part. The eastern part is located in Katingan 
Kuala  sub-district,  part  of  Katingan  Regency,  while the western  part is  located  in  Satiruk village,  part  of 
Pulau Hanaut sub-district, East Kotawaringin Regency. The project area, especially in the eastern part, is 
surrounded by villages. A total of 17 villages
17
 are located in the vicinity of the project area, of which one in 
the western part and the remainder in the eastern part. Out of these 17 villages, the project proponent will 
be  engaging  with 13 of  them  (see Table 2-2).  The  other  four  villages, located to  the  north  of  the  project 
area, based  on  the  Free,  Prior  and  Informed  Consent  (FPIC) process  conducted, have  no significant 
interaction with the project area. In consultation with these villages, it was therefore decided that they would 
not be included. 
In accordance to BPS Statistics Report (2023), the population in the 12 engaged villages of Katingan Kuala 
Sub-district  in 2022 is 17,323 people  consisting of 9,009 (52%)  men  and 8,314 (48%)  women.  The  total 
population in Satiruk Village of Pulau Hanaut Sub-district is 1,340 people consisting of 688 men (52%) and 
652 women (48%). 
The communities in the engaged villages are ethnically diverse, consisting primarily of local residents and 
transmigrants. The transmigrant population is mostly from the Javanese ethnic group (West Java, Central 
Java and East Java), while the local population is from the Banjar ethnic group. 
The primary livelihood in these villages is paddy farming. Other livelihoods found in these villages include 
fishermen,  handymen,  traders,  laborers,  civil  servants,  teachers,  health  workers,  entrepreneurs,  water 
transport taxis, breeders (goats, cows, chickens and ducks) and fish farmers. Based on these livelihoods, 
the  project  team  identified  several  community  groups  in  the  villages  such  as  paddy  farmers,  fishermen, 
coffee farmers, water taxi operators, and others. In Satiruk Village
18
, villagers mostly rely on farming and 
fishing for their livelihood.

2.1.6.1 Land Tenure 
As mentioned in the previous section, there are 13 villages in the project zone. At the project start, various 
physical interactions were identified between villagers and the forest in the project area for livelihoods such 
as removal of timber for house construction, fishing, wild honey harvest, and other activities. There are no 
villages or community’s land within the project area
19
. However, some villagers have informal management 
rights over tatah/canals in the project area.  
Historically
20
, the land in the surroundings of the project area was held/used by Banjarese communities
21
. 
Forest  was  cleared for  agriculture  and  settlements.  Land  ownership  at  this  time was generally  obtained 
through inheritance from generation to generation from their parents or from their ancestors or by buying 
from  other  people.  Evidence  of  ownership  of  community  lands  is  generally  only  based  on  planting  and 
natural  boundaries  in  the  form  of  rivers  and  trees  (rubber,  durian,  etc.).  The  existing  land  is  currently 
cultivated for various types of agricultural commodities such as paddy fields and plantations such as rubber, 
corn,  vegetables,  fruits,  and  others. Over  time,  land  rights  have evolved from  informal  to  formal  legal 
binding.  
 
19
 Note that although the administrative boundaries overlap with the forest area, the villages are located outside of it. 
No settlements are located in the forest concession area. 
20
 Based on direct interview with villagers. Source: FPIC report. 
21
 The Banjarese or commonly called Urang Banjar, originate from the South Kalimantan region. Some of them are 
also in Central Kalimantan and some in East Kalimantan. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
26 
In 1984, Indonesia performed a transmigration program in Katingan Regency. There were 12 transmigration 
villages
22
 at that time, including Bumi Indah, Singam Raya, and Setia Mulya. At present, more than 30 years 
later, the transmigration villages have grown. Eight out of the 17 villages surrounding the project area are 
transmigration  villages
23
.  The  transmigrants had a right to 2.25  hectares of land  (one  hectare  of  cleared 
land, 0.25 hectare for housing, and the remaining area in the form of forest). The majority of the population 
of the transmigration villages came from West Java, Central Java, and East Java. 
In addition to land tenure rights, there are also customary management rights over tatah/canals by villagers, 
especially the Banjarese. Based on the FPIC report, the history of making tatah/canals started since 1938 
when  they  were  used  as  a  source  of  inland  fisheries.  Over  time,  the  tatah/canals  were  also  used  as 
transportation  channels  for  illegal  timber  extraction  especially  after  year  2000.  However,  since  the 
Indonesian  Government  banned  logging  activities,  many  of  the  canals  have  been  neglected  and  are no 
longer used by villagers. The tatah/canals were then returned back to their original function, as a source of 
inland fisheries. Currently, the tatah/canals have grown with many branches or intersections. However, only 
a few tatah/canals are still used for fishery. 
2.1.6.2 Land Use 
The  land  use  in  the  surroundings  of the project  area  is  divided  into  several  types  of  use,  namely  paddy 
fields, plantations, and settlements. 
Most  of  the  land  is  used  for  paddy  fields.  Such  conditions  occur  in  almost  all engaged villages, both 
transmigrant and local. From 2018 to 2023, the Government of Indonesia constructed irrigation canals for 
paddy fields under the SIMURP project. The SIMURP project supports around 13,000 ha of existing paddy 
fields. A 10-km stretch of these irrigation canals is located inside the PUM concession, along its boundary.
24
  
Besides paddy  fields, there  are  plantations  cultivated  by  the  villagers  such  as  rubber  and  coconut 
plantations. The coconut plantations are mainly cultivated by the local community in coastal villages such 
as Selat Baning and Satiruk. Besides, there is also land used for vegetable gardens and fruit trees such as 
durian. Villagers usually plant vegetables and fruit trees in their yard. Yard land is also commonly used to 
raise livestock, especially cows and goats. Another use of land in the villages is for construction of swiftlet 
houses in the residential yards. 
Finally, the settlement areas consist of housing of villagers, public facilities such as schools, health centers, 
economic  facilities,  livelihood  areas  such  as  paddy  fields,  yards/fields,  and  economic  facilities  such  as 
markets, stores, and others.  
2.1.6.3 Local Transportation 
The  project  area  and  surroundings are located  in  a  remote  area,  about  200  km  from  the  capital  of  the 
province,  Palangkaraya,  reached  by  land  and  water  transportation.  Land  transportation  using motor 
 
22
 Manpower Agency of Katingan Regency.

vehicles  takes about  5  hours’  drive  from  Palangkaraya  to  Sampit, followed  by water  transportation  from 
Sampit to the villages which takes about 5-6 hours.  
Transportation is described to obtain a better understanding of the social parameters in the project area. 
The communities use both land and water transportation for their daily activities, such as accessing schools, 
public  health  facilities,  or  markets. The  choice  of  transportation  mode  is  influenced  by  factors  including 
distance and accessibility. 
2.1.6.4 Economic Activities 
The  livelihood  patterns  in  villages  surrounding  the  project  area  are  directed  at  agriculture  and  fisheries, 
especially  plantation  crop  farming.  In  addition  to  agriculture,  the  communities  are  engaged in  the  inland 
fisheries and marine fisheries sectors. Some local farmers also cultivate perennials/plantations. In addition 
to the agricultural sector, the population is engaged in services and home industries, such as making tofu 
and tempeh, home industry, and other micro, small, and medium enterprise (MSME) activities. 
There are various types of economic facilities in the villages especially in the engaged villages of Katingan 
Kuala sub-district. These include, among others, markets, banks, kiosks/shops, restaurants, mini markets, 
mini-ATMs, and Village Unit Cooperatives or Village Unit Non-Cooperatives and village owned enterprises 
(BUMDes). In Satiruk Village, there is one village market in the area.  
The market is a place of economic interactions among communities. It also serves as a place to observe 
the local business such as food crop agricultural products, home industry products (tofu, tempeh, noodles), 
and other home industry products.  
2.1.6.5 Health and Sanitation 
Health facilities in Katingan Kuala sub-district especially in the engaged villages are limited. Based on the 
statistical  data,  in  the  12  villages  of  Katingan  Kuala  sub-district,  a  population  of  17,062  is  served  by  two 
community health centers (Pusat Kesehatan Masyarakat, Puskesmas) and 16 auxiliary community health 
centers (Puskesmas Pembantu, Pustu). The nearest hospitals are located in Banjarmasin and Sampit about 
4-5  hours  away  by  water  taxi.  There  are  36 health  workers  in  Katingan  Kuala  sub-district  (1 doctor,  15 
nurses, and 20 midwives).  
Sanitation issues in the engaged villages include access to clean water and open defecation. The villages’ 
proximity to the peatlands is the main cause of the lack of clean water for consumption due to high acidity 
and high iron content (pyrite, FeS
2
). There are several water sources used by the villagers, among others 
river water, harvested rainwater, and wells. The most common source for washing and bathing purposes is 
river water, either used directly in the river or pumped and filtered. Communities in villages surrounding the 
project area still practice open defecation in the river.

2.1.8 Stakeholder Identification (G1.5) 
Stakeholders  identified for  the  project include the  project’s  communities  and  other  stakeholders.  The 
project’s communities are defined as the communities in the villages of the project zone; other stakeholders 
are  defined  as  the  persons  or  institutions  or  groups  of  communities  that  impact  or  influence the  project 
activities. 
The identification of stakeholders was based on the following criteria: 
1. Economic activities: communities that implement or intend to implement economic activities related 
to the use of land in the project area, including agriculture and fisheries. 
2. Geographic location: communities that live in the surroundings of the project area.  
3. Use  of  natural  resources  within  the  project  area and  customary  use  rights:  communities  who 
depend on forest-related activities, such as fishermen looking for fish in canals located in the project 
site or other extractive livelihoods such as forest honey or rattan seekers. 
4. Other relevant stakeholders with interest in or influence over the project. 
The identification process  was conducted through participatory meetings and interviews with key contact 
persons  in  the  villages,  such  as  heads  of  village  and  local  public  figures.  The  stakeholder  analysis  was 
conducted  through  internal  discussion  and  confirmation  with  key  contact  persons.  The  aim  of  the 
stakeholder  identification  and  analysis  was  to  identify  the  key  stakeholders  in  the  villages,  including  in 
relation  to  the  decision-making  process.  A  detailed  stakeholder  list  and  description  is  provided in  the 
following section.
 
2.1.9 Stakeholder Descriptions (G1.6, G1.13)

other  stakeholders.  The  table  below  shows  the  stakeholders. Stakeholders  were  identified  as  per  the

2.1.10 Sectoral Scope and Project Type 
This  project  belongs  to Sectoral  Scope  14  for  ‘Agriculture,  Forestry,  and  Other  Land-Use  (AFOLU)’.  The 
project  comprises  REDD  (APD)  and  ARR  activities  on  peatland  and  tidal  wetland  as  defined  in  VM0007 
(Version 1.6). PLUM Project activities covered by the VM0007 include (1) Avoiding Planned Deforestation 
(APD), (2) Afforestation, Reforestation and Revegetation (ARR), (3) Restoration of Wetlands Ecosystems 
(RWE)  on  Peatland  and  Tidal  Wetlands,  and  (4)  Avoiding  Planned  Wetland  Degradation  (APWD)  on 
Peatland and Tidal Wetlands.  
The project is not a grouped project. 
2.1.11 Project Activities and Theory of Change (G1.8) 
The  project aims to  reduce  emissions  from  planned  deforestation  and  forest  degradation while  providing 
tangible  climate,  community,  and  biodiversity  benefits.  The  area  is  directly  threatened  by  the  potential 
conversion to commercial oil palm plantation, which was successfully halted by the project. A portion of the 
project area is in a degraded condition due to historical logging, peatland drainage, and forest fires. Low 
community  welfare  surrounding  the  project  area  due  to  various  causes,  such  as  limited  livelihood 
opportunities  and  low  land  productivity,  have  also  indirectly  threatened  the  integrity  of  the area  through 
community agriculture expansion and illegal logging.  
In  the  baseline  scenario,  the  existence  of  commercial  oil  palm  plantation  in  the  area  may  provide  some 
livelihood opportunities for the communities. However, it will not address the other issues currently faced 
by  the  communities,  such  as  the  limited  access  to  health  care  services  and  education,  limited  livelihood 
opportunities  and  marketing  of  agricultural  products, climate  change, land  degradation,  and  low  water 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
36 
quality and sanitation. The project was therefore established with the goal to conserve the remaining forest, 
restore  ecosystem  function,  protect  biodiversity,  and  improve  the  well-being  of  communities  to  become 
resilient  to  climate  change. In  addition  to improving  the  livelihoods,  the project  also  aims  to  improve 
communities’ health and education. 
The problem root analysis in the areas of community, climate and biodiversity is shown below: 
 
Figure 2-2. Schematic diagram of problem root analysis 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
37 
 
Figure 2-3. Theory of Change 
The Theory of Change of the project is illustrated in the figure above. The overarching goal is supported 
through  five  specific  impacts  which  will  generate  net  benefits  in  the  area  of  Climate,  Community  and 
Biodiversity,  namely:  (1)  GHG  emission  reductions,  (2)  Improved  ecosystem  quality and  resilience,  (3) 
Biodiversity conservation and protection, (4) Sustainable, resilient livelihoods, and (5) Improved community 
well-being.  These  impacts  are  expected  to  be  achieved  through  the  outcomes,  outputs  and  activities 
outlined below. It is anticipated that these outputs and outcomes will address the identified problems and 
root causes and lead to reduced GHG emissions, reduced deforestation and forest degradation, reduced 
biodiversity loss, increased resilience to climate change impacts, and improved local livelihoods and well-
being.  Obtaining  the Forest  Utilization  Business  License  (PBPH) for  the  project  area  is  considered  a 
prerequisite for this Theory of Change.
28
 
The project activities will be implemented with the close involvement of local leaders, local communities, 
and  local  government. The  PLUM  Project  will  also  work  closely  with  organizations  such  as  the  Borneo 
Nature  Foundation  and Dompet  Dhuafa in  the  implementation  of  the  activities on  the  ground  (refer  to 
Section 2.4.4). The project is not located within the jurisdiction of a REDD+ programme. 
Outcome 1.1. Transparent GHG emissions accounting and monitoring 
The project will establish a transparent GHG emissions accounting and monitoring system to ensure that 
the GHG emission reductions or removals are accurately quantified. This will enable the project to generate 
 
28
 The Project succeeded in diverting the oil palm plantation license application process into Forest Utilization Business 
License (PBPH). The Project received the PBPH principal license from the Government of Indonesia in August 2022 
and the definitive license in November 2023. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
38 
Verified Carbon Units (VCUs) and leverage financing for the project. Under the GHG emissions accounting 
and monitoring, the project will implement the following outputs. 
Output 1.1.1 Monitoring GHG emissions on peatlands 
GHG  emissions  on  peatlands  will  be  monitored.  GHG  measurement  equipment  will  be  installed, 
and long-term data on GHG flux on peatlands (CO
2
, CH
4
, etc.) be made available. 
Output 1.1.2 Burnt area monitoring 
The  project  will  set  up  a  system  to  monitor  data  on  burnt  area  using  drone  photos  or  satellite 
images, remote sensing analysis and ground truthing. The data will allow the project to evaluate 
the fire prevention and restoration interventions and calculate the biomass and accumulated carbon 
in the area. 
Output 1.1.3 Land cover change monitoring 
In parallel, the project will establish a system for land cover change monitoring. This will enable the 
project  team  to  monitor  total  area  deforested  and  degraded  as  well  as  forest  cover  change,  in 
particular the increase of forest cover from restoration activities. 
Outcome 1.2. Incidence of fires is reduced through enhanced fire prevention and management 
Along with climate change, wildfires are a serious threat to forest conservation efforts. Degraded or drained 
peatlands are more prone to fires and therefore may cause setbacks to restoration. Therefore, the project 
will invest systematic efforts to reduce and prevent forest fires in the project area and its surroundings. The 
development of a fire early warning system will be achieved by implementing a number of processes that 
enable  the  early  detection  of  fires,  including  the  socialization  of  fire  protection,  provision  of  facilities  and 
infrastructure for fire prevention and extinguishment, periodic patrols and monitoring of hot spots. 
Output 1.2.1 Development of Automatic Weather Station (AWS) and Fire Danger Rating System 
(FDRS) 
An Automatic Weather Station (AWS) will be set up to collect weather data such as air temperature, 
precipitation, soil moisture, and water table depth. This data is critical to predicting fire risk, which 
is  imperative  to  effective  fire  management.  A  Fire  Danger  Rating  System  (FDRS)  will  be  put  in 
place to systematically assess current fire danger and evaluate the factors influencing fire danger. 
The  AWS  and  FDRS  are  important  early  warning  tools  in  preventing  peat  fires. The  information 
provided by these tools will be published for the community to increase their awareness of climate 
change issues. 
Output 1.2.2 Establishment of fire patrol team and fire patrol activities 
A fire patrol team will be established in parallel with the forest protection patrol under Output 2.1.1. 
It  will  carry  out  regular  fire  patrol  activities  depending  on  the  fire  danger  rating,  mostly  in  the  dry 
season. The fire patrol will collaborate closely with the community firefighting team under Output 
3.2.1. This effort aims to increase community participation in forest and land fire prevention. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
39 
 
Output 1.2.3 Planting of firebreak along project boundary 
To  prevent  fire  from  spreading  into  the  concession  land,  the project  will  plant  firebreaks  in 
vulnerable areas along the boundary of the concession. With this firebreak, it is expected that the 
risk  of  fire  from  outside  the  concession  can  be  avoided.  The  firebreak  species  will  be  selected 
based on characteristic of their resistance to fire and the availability in the project area, such as but 
not limited to Galam (Melaleuca leucadendron), Tumih (Combretocarpus rotundatus), and Jelutung 
(Dyera costulata). Furthermore, they are adaptive to flood and stagnant water.  
Output 1.2.4 Provision of fire-fighting equipment and fire watch tower infrastructure 
The  project  will  provide  forest  fire  equipment  to  the  fire  patrol  team,  such  as personal  protective 
equipment, water pumps and hoses, as well as drones for monitoring. It will also build two forest 
fire watch towers in the project area, with a height of approximately 15 meters. 
Outcome 1.3. Peat ecosystems are restored and GHG emissions from degraded peat ecosystems reduced 
Output 1.3.1 Canal blocking and peat rewetting 
A number of canals previously used for the transportation of logs and for fishing have resulted in 
the peat swamp forest being partially drained. For this reason, it is necessary to restore the peatland 
ecosystem  by  blocking  canals.  This  will  be  done  with  the  close  involvement  of  the  tatah/canal 
holders.   Canal   blocking   activities   will   also   involve   local   community   as   a   way   to   provide 
alternative/additional income. Canal blocks will be made of wood and built manually. The design 
should allow fishermen with motorized canoe to pass to the fishing ground inside the concession. 
Output 1.3.2 Peat water level and subsidence monitoring 
The  project  will  monitor  the  peat  water  level  (through  installation  of  dip  wells)  and  subsidence 
(through subsidence poles) on a regular basis. Peat water level monitoring will be conducted daily, 
while the subsidence monitoring is scheduled every three months at sampling points. 
Outcome 1.4. Deforested and degraded areas are restored 
Output 1.4.1 Mangrove nursery establishment 
A nursery will be established where seedlings will be raised and handled until they are ready for 
planting. The aim is to produce high-quality seedlings to increase the survival rate. It is anticipated 
that the following mangrove species will be grown in the nursery: Api api (A. marina, A. lanata, and 
A. alba), Bakau (Rhizophora apiculata, Rhizophora stylosa), Tancang (Bruguiera sp.), and Rambay 
(Sonneratia caseolaris). The project will only use native tree species and will prioritize species that 
are adapted to climate change (e.g., less vulnerable to temperature and salinity increase). 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
40 
Output 1.4.2 Mangrove planting and prevention of coastal abrasion 
Mangrove restoration activities will involve both planting and Assisted Natural Regeneration (ANR). 
In  addition  to  planting  activities,  the project  will  ensure  the  maintenance  of  existing  and  newly 
planted mangroves. Maintenance will be carried out based on conditions found in the field, including 
controlling pest and diseases, and protecting the mangroves from attacks by crabs (Crustacea sp.), 
leaf and stem caterpillars, root fungus, barnacles/teritip and weeds (usually mosses). 
Furthermore, the project will establish sediment traps to prevent coastal abrasion in the mangrove 
area. The sediment traps will consist of fences made of wood/bamboo stakes along eroded land. 
Soil will be deposited as sediment and the land will become suitable for mangrove planting. The 
sediment traps will also prevent mangrove seedlings from being washed away by sea waves. 
Output 1.4.3 Peat nursery establishment 
For  the  restoration  activities  in  the  peatland  area,  the  project  will  establish  a  peat  plant  nursery. 
Seeds  will  be  obtained  in  two  ways:  1)  seedlings;  and  2)  wildings.  The  species  will  be  selected 
based on the adaptability to flood, their uses for the community and growth rate. Only local species 
will be used. The selected species include but are not limited to: Balangeran (Shorea balangeran), 
Jelutung  (Dyera  costulata),  Ramin  (Gonystilus  bancanus),  Bintaro  (Cerbera  manghas),  Punak 
(Tetramerysta glabra),  and  Resak  (Cotylelobium lanceolatum). The  project  will  prioritize  species 
that are adapted to climate change (e.g., that are resistant to floods and droughts).
29
 
The  target  density  for  planting  is  500  trees/ha  in  mature  stage  (20 cm  DBH).  Seedlings  will  be 
planted after they reach 30-50 cm in height or 5-6 months old from seeding time. Wildings will be 
collected from the remaining primary forest in the concession by involving the local community.  
Output 1.4.4 Planting and assisted natural regeneration of degraded peatland 
Historical illegal logging activities have caused serious damage to nearly two-thirds of the forest in 
the project area. This situation is exacerbated by fires occurring due to the deterioration of the peat 
ecosystem. The project will carry out reforestation and ANR activities to restore damaged and lost 
forests,  using  native  species  that  are  ecologically  adapted  to  the  conditions  of  the  peat  and 
mangrove ecosystems. Extreme weather events such as heavy rains, floods and droughts will be 
taken into account when determining the timing of the planting. Communities who rely on the forests 
for  wood  will  be  involved,  for  example,  through  planting  schemes  for  alternative,  sustainable 
sources of wood and/or alternative income sources. 
 
29
 For example, the Balangeran tree (Shorea balangeran) has been found to have relatively high survival rates and 
tolerance to saturated conditions, indicating a degree of resilience to environmental stressors such as flooding. Tata 
HL, et al. (2022). Flooding tolerance of four tropical peatland tree species in a nursery trial. PLOS ONE 17(4): e0262375. 
https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0262375

41 
Outcome 2.1. Factors that lead to species extinction and loss of biodiversity are addressed 
Output 2.1.1 Building Patrol Teams and SMART Patrol System 
Forest protection patrol teams will be established, consisting of permanent staff and involving local 
communities. The patrol teams will address preventive issues including illegal logging, forest fire, 
and other threats. 
The project  will  adopt  the  Spatial  Monitoring  and  Reporting  Tool
30
 (SMART)  Patrol  System,  a 
system that allows data to be collected and stored digitally into a centralized database. This system 
will  help  provide  standard,  accountable  and  transparent  data.  The  system  will  serve  as  a  tool  to 
monitor natural and human-induced threats, as well as trigger species, and biodiversity in general. 
Output 2.1.2 Carrying out periodic patrols for the prevention of illegal activities and other threats 
to biodiversity 
The  protection  of  remaining  forests  and  biodiversity  will  be  ensured  through  regular  patrols.  The 
forest  protection  patrol  teams will  regularly  patrol  the  forest  using  the  SMART  Patrol  System  on 
foot  or  using  motorized  canoe  depending  on  the  condition.  Additionally,  the  project  team  will 
conduct regular joint patrols at least once a year together with local stakeholders. 
Output 2.1.3 Regular biodiversity monitoring 
The project  will  regularly  monitor  the  three  trigger  species  as  well  as  other  threatened  species 
(species  listed  as  CR,  EN,  VU  in  the  IUCN  Red  List)  in  the  project  area  using  a  standardized 
monitoring  protocol.  Permanent  transect  plots  for  flora  and  fauna  monitoring  will  be  established 
within  the  project  area  and  field  staff  will  be  trained  for  flora/fauna  identification,  monitoring 
protocols, and data analysis. 
Output 2.1.4 Construction of guard posts for forest security 
Forest  protection  infrastructure  in  the  form  of  guard  stations  and  shelters  will  be  built  to  create 
favorable conditions for forest protection. Sign boards will be installed along the boundary of the 
concession with the surrounding villages. The sign boards will contain information on the project 
area boundary and on fire prevention. 
Outcome 2.2. Project  area  becomes  a  conservation  and  research  location  for  biodiversity  and  peatland 
and mangrove restoration 
Output 2.2.1 Implementation of orangutan conservation measures 
The project will implement orangutan conservation measures, to be identified in collaboration with 
local conservation organizations and relevant government agencies. The project area may serve 
as an orangutan release location for rescued/displaced orangutans. 
 
30
 https://smartconservationtools.org/  

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
42 
Output 2.2.2 Collaboration with universities/ research institutes to conduct research on biodiversity 
and peatland and mangrove restoration in project area.  
The project will develop collaboration with universities/research institutes and students to conduct 
research  on  biodiversity (including climate  change  impacts on  biodiversity), and  peatland  and 
mangrove restoration in the project area. 
Outcome 3.1. Local communities attain improved livelihoods and economic development 
Output 3.1.1 Development of alternative, resilient livelihoods for affected communities 
The low economic and educational level of the community means that people often rely on forest 
resources  for  their  livelihoods.  Therefore,  the  project  aims  to  create  sustainable  alternative 
livelihoods  to  increase  incomes  of  local  communities  and  reduce  the  pressure  on  forests,  while 
increasing adaptive capacity of local communities to climate change. Initial options for sustainable 
livelihood  development  have  been  identified  in  consultation  with  local  communities  and  include 
agroforestry, mangrove honey, local coffee, duck and chicken rearing, and small-scale fishponds. 
These activities are intended for the impacted communities such as fishermen, former loggers and 
others.  The  activities  cover  the  training,  start-up  capital,  and  technical  assistance. As  part  of  the 
project’s  livelihood  activities,  the  project  will  regularly  discuss  climate  change  impacts  with  local 
communities  and  identify  additional  measures  that  can  be integrated  into  the  project  activities  to 
ensure that they are adaptive to climate change. When selecting agroforestry crops in consultation 
with local communities, the project will take into account climate change considerations building on 
both scientific and local knowledge. For example, tree species will be selected that are resistant to 
flood and drought. 
Output 3.1.2 Revolving fund development 
Limited  access  to  financial  infrastructure  makes  it  difficult  for  communities  to  develop  alternative 
livelihoods that have the potential to enhance their well-being. The project will enhance access to 
financial   resources   through   a   revolving   fund   mechanism   that   helps   households,   especially 
vulnerable  groups,  develop  sustainable  alternative  livelihoods  and  income  sources. A  revolving 
fund  will  be  made  available to  local  livelihood  groups,  including  women’s  groups,  and/or  micro, 
small,  and  medium  enterprises  (MSMEs).  The  targeted  groups  of  the  program  are  low-income 
households,  women’s  groups,  and  villagers  in  general. It  is expected that  these  activities  will 
contribute to strengthening the resilience of local communities to climate change, in particular for 
vulnerable groups including women and poorer households. 
Output 3.1.3 Increasing access to marketing of agricultural products 
In parallel with alternative livelihood development and access to financial resources, the project will 
support enhanced marketing and market access for agricultural products such as honey and coffee. 
This will enable local communities to increase and diversify their income sources. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
43 
Outcome 3.2. Local communities participate in efforts for sustainable land management 
Output 3.2.1 Increasing  the  role  of  Fire  Care  Communities  /  Masyarakat  Peduli  Api  (MPA)  in 
preventing and fighting forest fires 
Cooperation with the local community is considered critical to prevent forest fires. The project will 
establish  Fire  Care  Communities (Masyarakat Peduli Api, MPA)  in  the  villages  surrounding  the 
project  area. These community groups will  be  trained on how  to  handle forest  fires. The  project 
also plans to distribute forest fire prevention posters, awareness raising on the negative impacts of 
burning, among others. This effort aims to increase community participation in forest and land fire 
prevention. 
Output 3.2.2 Promoting sustainable, resilient agricultural practices 
Low agricultural land productivity is one of the root causes of low incomes and poverty and can put 
more  pressure  on  the  project  area.  To  increase  the  productivity  of  existing  community  lands, 
address  land  degradation and  increase  resilience  to  climate  change,  the project  will  develop  a 
Sustainable  Agriculture  Program  for  farmers.  This  will  include  training  and  assistance  on  good 
agricultural  practices  (e.g.,  sustainable  agriculture,  organic  farming,  home  gardens,  etc.).  Best 
practices will be introduced such as crop rotation, integrated pest management, agroforestry, and 
tillage  management. In  designing  and  implementing  these  activities,  the  project  will  take  into 
account climate change considerations building on both scientific and local knowledge. 
Outcome 3.3. Local communities benefit from improved health and sanitation 
Output  3.3.1 Improving  and  supporting  public  health  through  health  service  programs  and 
community-based sanitation programs 
Limited access to health services and sanitation facilities is one of the contributing factors to poor 
health outcomes in the project zone. A program of health services and community-based sanitation 
development  will  be  implemented  to  improve  the  quality  of  public  health  in the  project  area.  In 
particular,  the  project  aims  to  reduce  the  prevalence  of  stunting  among  children  in  the  target 
villages. 
The project will provide the local community health centres with needed equipment such as infant 
scales and will cooperate with local NGOs working in the health sector to provide health services 
and health education for the local communities.  
In  addition,  the project  will  implement  activities  to  improve  sanitation  in  the  target  villages.  It  will 
assist  households  in  adopting  Clean  and  Healthy  Behavior  (Perilaku Hidup Bersih dan Sehat or 
PHBS), will develop clean water facilities as well as toilets and septic tanks. 
Outcome 3.4. Local communities’ benefit from enhanced access to education 
Output 3.4.1 Environmental education program development and partial scholarships for students 
Limited education quality and knowledge of the communities on the local environment is one of the 
existing  root  causes  of  low  levels  of  development  and  land  and  forest  degradation.  This  lack  of 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
44 
knowledge has resulted in environmentally damaging activities (opening lands by burning, draining 
peatlands, etc.). To improve this condition, the project will implement an Environmental Education 
Program  for  students  and  teachers.  Furthermore,  the  project  will  provide  partial  scholarships  for 
students and will provide local senior high school students with internship opportunities. 
2.1.12 Sustainable Development  
The  project  activities  are  expected  to  contribute  to  the  United  Nations  Sustainable  Development  Goals 
(SDGs), especially goals number 1, 3, 5, 6, 12, 13, 14, and 15. The relevant SDG targets and indicators 
are included  in Appendix 13:  Monitoring  Plan. These  priorities  are  aligned  with  Indonesia’s  national 
sustainable development indicators as outlined in Indonesia’s Medium Term Development Plan (RPJMN) 
2020-2024
31
,  including  in  the  areas  of  reducing  poverty, ensuring  environmental  sustainability,  gender 
mainstreaming, low-carbon development, and land and forest restoration
32
. 
 
Table 2-8. Project contribution to SDGs 
Sustainable 
Development Goals 
Application in the Project 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 1 on ending poverty. The 
project aims to provide sustainable alternative livelihoods as well as employment 
opportunities to surrounding communities. Initial options for sustainable 
livelihood development have been identified in consultation with local 
communities. For example, the development of agroforestry and ecologically 
compatible agriculture with the peat/mangrove ecosystem will be implemented 
together with the community. In addition, economic development based on 
natural solutions will be achieved based on existing potentials, such as 
mangrove honey, local coffee, and other products. Support will be provided to 
increase market access for these products. A revolving fund will also be 
implemented with local livelihood groups to improve access to financial 
resources. 
 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 3 on good health and well-
being. Sub-districts in Indonesia, specifically in remote area, generally have one 
auxiliary community health center (Pustu), with one nurse and midwife. The 
provided facilities are basic and generally provide the community with limited 
health services. The project will support the Pustu with additional equipment and 
health services for communities, as well as introduce healthier habits related to 
sanitation such as avoiding or reducing open defecation practice. Through these 
interventions, the project aims to reduce the prevalence of stunting among 
children in the target villages.

45 
Sustainable 
Development Goals 
Application in the Project 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 5 on gender equality. All 
project activities will encourage the participation of all residents of the villages, 
especially women, youth, and marginalized people.  
The project is committed to implementing Gender and Social Inclusion (GESI) in 
all its activities. The project will provide equal opportunities to women, 
vulnerable, and other groups to be involved and participate in all activities, 
including the hiring process for employment opportunities in the project. 
Additionally, the project will support the establishment of women’s groups in the 
target villages to promote economic opportunities for women. 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 6 on clean water and 
sanitation. Clean water and sanitation is one of the major issues in the project 
zone. The project will provide sanitation facilities for communities and will 
support the adoption of Clean and Healthy Behavior (Perilaku Hidup Sehat dan 
Bersih or PHBS) in the target villages. It will also help increase access to clean 
water. 
Additionally, through the conservation and rewetting of peatlands, the project 
supports the protection and restoration of wetland ecosystems, contributing to 
securing water quality and availability in the project zone. 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 12 on responsible 
consumption and production. Through actions that encourage the sustainable 
management of natural resources, sustainable agriculture and the recovery of 
degraded areas, the project promotes the conservation of natural resources, 
coupled with socioeconomic development. The project will support the 
development of sustainable value chains for marketing of local agricultural 
products, generating income and well-being for local communities while 
promoting the sustainable use of natural resources. 
Finally, the project will increase awareness for sustainable development by 
implementing an Environmental Education Program. 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 13 on climate action. All 
activities undertaken by the project aim to take action to combat climate change 
and its impacts by reducing deforestation and forest degradation in the project 
area and consequently reducing greenhouse gases emissions. It will also 
address climate change by restoring mangrove and peatland areas. 
Furthermore, the project will contribute to climate change adaptation by 
increasing the resilience and adaptive capacity of local communities and 
ecosystems to climate change. 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 14 on life below water 
through the conservation and restoration of mangrove ecosystems. Mangroves 
serve as a natural filter leading to improved water quality and a reduction in 
erosion, which enhances the health of surrounding ecosystems. Mangroves also 
provide habitat for marine species populations through their contribution as a 
spawning ground and by providing shelter from predators.

Development Goals 
Application in the Project 
 
The project contributes to the achievement of SDG 15 on life on land. It supports 
the conservation and restoration of forests and wetlands by reducing 
deforestation and restoring degraded ecosystems. 
The project also supports the conservation of 31 species that are classified as 
Critically Endangered, Endangered, and Vulnerable in the IUCN Red List in the 
project area. The conservation program aims to protect the diversity of plants 
and animals and their ecosystems.

2.1.18 Risks to the Project (G1.10) 
The  project  used  the  AFOLU  Non-Permanence  Risk  Report  Excel  Calculation  Tool,  v4.2  (Appendix 7: 
AFOLU Non-permanence Risk Report) where the overall risk rating of 17% was determined. The risk factors 
were classified into three categories: internal risks, external risks and natural risks, and into subcategories 
such  as  project  management,  financial  viability  and  community  engagement.  The  project  was  evaluated 
considering each of the risk factors suggested by the tool. A description was made of the applicability of 
the risk factor and a score was assigned to each factor by category and subcategory. A summary of the 
main risks and mitigation actions identified is provided below. A more detailed risk report can be found in 
Appendix 7: AFOLU Non-permanence Risk Report.

2.1.19 Benefit Permanence (G1.11) 
The project is designed to be sustained for at least 60 years as per the license granted. The license can be 
extended  by  up  to 90 years  as  per  Indonesian  regulations
35
 to  enhance  the climate,  community  and 
biodiversity benefits beyond the project’s lifetime.  
The project’s community  development  activities  are  designed  to  increase  the  quality  of  life  of local 
communities and community groups. They are designed to build the capacity of the local communities in 
the long term and benefits are expected to endure beyond the lifetime of the project. The project will support 
alternative  livelihoods by providing  training and initial seed  capital  through  a  revolving  fund,  and by 
enhancing market access. It will also implement activities to improve health and sanitation and will improve 
access to higher education for local youth.  
Regarding maintaining the project’s climate benefits, the project will rewet the peatlands by building canal 
blocks, which is a recognized method of restoring the hydrology of this degraded area. This will lower the 
risk of fire in the landscape and enable the restoration of natural vegetation over the course of the project. 
In addition, the project will also increase the forest cover in the area by protecting remaining forests and 
 
35
 Government Regulation (Peraturan Pemerintah) No. 23/2021 (clause 150) and MoEF Regulation (PermenLHK) No. 
8/2021 (clause 75). 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
54 
performing ARR activities. The restoration and conservation of peat and mangrove forests will increase the 
resilience  of  ecosystems  to  future  climate  impacts. Furthermore,  the  project  also  helps  build  long-term 
adaptive capacity of the local communities. 
In terms of biodiversity benefits, the project will conduct forest protection patrols and biodiversity monitoring. 
Protecting the remaining forest from conversion to oil palm plantation and restoring deforested areas in the 
project area will provide a habitat extension and enhance connectivity to the larger forest areas in the project 
zone (e.g., Sebangau National Park to the north of project area) which will be beneficial for the long-term 
viability of key species population. Adaptive management will be undertaken, and local communities will be 
closely involved to ensure long-term benefits for biodiversity. The project’s activities are described in more 
detail in Section 2.1.11. 
2.1.20 Financial Sustainability (G1.12) 
The project proponent is a visionary conservation company, led by an experienced CEO with support from 
long-term partners  and  investors. The project proponent  has  been  demonstrating  the  commitment  to  the 
project by developing an integrated long-term plan which covers conservation, restoration, and community 
development. The financial projections of the company are classified as commercially sensitive information 
and will be shared with the audit team on a confidential basis. 
2.1.21 Grouped Projects  
Not Applicable 
2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Land Use Scenarios without the Project (G2.1) 
The PLUM Project is located in Katingan and Kotawaringin regencies, Central Kalimantan Province. The 
project area is under the management of PT PUM, who holds the forest utilization business license (PBPH) 
for 60 years in a total area of ±23,665 ha since 27
th
 November 2023 (Decision of the Head of Investment 
Coordination No.02200084122050002). This PBPH is the definitive license to the earlier principal license 
that was granted to PT PUM by the Ministry of Investment since 1 August 2022 (Decision of the Head of 
Investment Coordination No.01082211116206001). 
Prior to PT PUM receiving its principal PBPH license, the project area was designated to become hybrid 
coconut  oil  plantation  in  2009 (Katingan Regent Decree No.335/2009) and  oil  palm  plantation in  2011 
(Katingan Regent Decree No.525/229/KPTS/VIII/2011). An environmental permit for oil palm plantation was 
granted to PT PUM in 2013
36
, followed by other administrative steps and approvals. PT PUM’s area was 
planned to be developed as an oil palm plantation along with two other companies adjacent to the area (PT 
Katingan Mujur Sejahtera or PT KMS to the east and PT Trubus Bumi Sejahtera or PT TBS to the south). 
All three companies were owned by a company group named PT Agro Inti Semesta (PT AIS)
37
. Additionally, 
in 2022, an approval to convert forest land into oil palm plantation was given to another oil palm company 
(PT Katingan Sawit Bersama or PT KSB
38
) not associated with PT AIS located along the western boundary 
 
36
 
Decree of Environmental Permit for Development of Oil Palm Plantation PT PUM (No.525.21/172/KPTS/IV/2013)
 
37
 Previously Agro Investama Group (AIG). 
38
 Not affiliated with PT AIS. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
55 
of PT PUM, reflecting that the project zone was envisioned as an oil palm production area (Map 3-2). These 
oil palm plantations are/were on Convertible Production Forest (Hutan Produksi Konversi, HPK) land
39
. PT 
KMS already started its operation since 2013. Without the PLUM Project, it is highly likely that PT PUM’s 
area would have continued to be developed into an oil palm plantation by PT AIS. 
Prior to the project initiation, the area was already in degraded condition due to historical logging activities 
and forest fires. In 2015, about 1,079 ha of the remaining peat swamp forest was burnt by forest fires which 
was  likely  the  result  of  small-scale communities’ activities  (e.g., illegal  logging,  fishing,  hunting)  and 
exacerbated by the El Niño event. At the time, PT PUM was not yet operating as an oil palm plantation as 
it was still in the process of obtaining all the necessary approvals. This situation demonstrates that, prior to 
the project initiation, activities were not implemented to generate GHG emissions for the purpose of their 
subsequent reduction. 
Hence, in the absence of the project, the land use of the project area would in the most likely scenario have 
become oil palm plantation with no activities to reduce and remove the GHG emissions. Oil palm plantation 
becomes the baseline scenario for the project, and the rate of deforestation is further explained in Section 
3.1.4 with  the  associated  appendix (Appendix 3: Proxy Deforestation  Report). Other alternative land  use 
scenarios  in  the  absence  of  the  project  are described  in Section 3.1.4,  including conservation  project 
(without  being  registered  as  a  VCS  AFOLU  project), industrial  acacia  plantation,  logging  concession, 
smallholder agriculture/farming, unprotected forest, and protected forest. 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (G2.1) 
The most-likely scenario that would happen in the project area is that the area would be converted to oil 
palm plantation. In 2009, the project area was designated to become hybrid coconut oil plantation (Katingan 
Regent Decree No.335/2009). In 2011, the project area was designated to become oil palm plantation and 
had  obtained  a  Plantation  Business  Permit  (Izin Usaha Perkebunan or  IUP) (Katingan Regent Decree 
No.525/229/KPTS/VIII/2011). As  explained  above,  an environmental  permit  for  oil  palm  plantation was 
granted to PT PUM in 2013, followed by other administrative steps and approvals. PT PUM was planned 
to be developed as an oil palm plantation along with two other companies adjacent to the area (PT KMS to 
the east and PT TBS to the south), owned by the same group, PT AIS. In 2013, PT KMS started operating 
at  the east-side  of  PT  PUM’s  concession (Map 3-2). In 2015, PT  TBS obtained  a  Plantation  Business 
Permit. In 2022, an approval to convert forest land into oil palm plantation was given to another oil palm 
company (PT KSB) not associated with PT AIS located to the west of PT PUM. Hence, even though the 
project area is on state-forest land, the government has regulated and allows the conversion of state-forest 
land  into  non-state-forest  land.  Considering that,  prior  to  the  project  initiation, the  project  area had been 
granted an oil palm license and that the adjacent areas to the project area have and will become oil palm 
plantations (i.e., PT KMS, PT TBS, PT KSB), it is very likely that in the absence of the project, the project 
area would have become oil palm plantation. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (G2.2) 
In the absence of the project, the community and biodiversity benefits of the project would not occur. The 
project  benefits for  community include improved access  to  health  services through  village  health  service 
 
39
 Except for PT TBS and partial area of PT KMS, which is on non-state forest land (Areal Penggunaan Lain, APL). For 
PT PUM, 82.56% of the area was on HPK (convertible production forest) and 17.44% on HP (production forest) land. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
56 
improvement   program,   higher   education for   children   through   scholarship   assistance   programs   and 
collaboration with universities, income generation as company’s workers and from livelihood development 
activities, increased access to clean water through village water supply programs (i.e., drilled wells), and 
hydrology system improvement to prevent fires and floods in residential and farm areas (see Section 4.5). 
The project benefits for biodiversity include the conservation of critically endangered species (i.e., Bornean 
orangutan) through habitat patrols and monitoring activities, restoration of peat and mangrove ecosystems 
through replanting of native tree species and water table management and assisting in natural regeneration 
(see Section 5.5). 
Regarding the  community’s  economic situation,  six  villages  that  are  directly adjacent  to PT  PUM’s 
concession are categorized as underdeveloped villages (see Section 4.1.1.3) and over 50% of villagers in 
the project zone are below the national poverty line of IDR 2,120,371/month per household
40
. Based on the 
livelihoods  assessment  conducted  by  the  project,  the  average  income in  the  villages  of  the  project  zone 
ranges   from IDR 266,667/month to IDR 2,900,862/month per   household,   with   an   average   of 
1,886,397/month  per  household
41
. In  terms  of  education, there has  been an  increase in  the number of 
public schools in the villages but access to schools remains a challenge. From the 13 villages in the project 
zone, only four have  a  junior  high  school  and  two  have  a  senior  high  school
42
. Regarding access  to  the 
nearest  healthcare facility, each village  has  at  least  one  healthcare  facility supported  by  healthcare 
professionals. However, this facility is usually not open 24 hours and only two villages (Jaya Makmur and 
Pagatan  Hulu) have a doctor  (general practitioner).
43
 Furthermore,  the  nearest  hospitals  are  in  Sampit, 
Kasongan and Banjarmasin, and it takes 5-7 hours of travel by river to reach them.  
Regarding biodiversity conservation, the project will provide protection to the high biodiversity in the area 
and  improve the  habitat quality by  restoring  deforested  and  degraded  areas. Since  the  project  area is 
situated less than 2 km south of Sebangau National Park, the third largest national park in Kalimantan, the 
project will serve as a habitat extension and thus enhance habitat connectivity to the national park. This will 
be beneficial for the long-term viability of biodiversity within the landscape. The project will also address the 
threats of repeated fires in the project area and high human-disturbance (i.e., canals, logging, poaching) 
that lower the natural habitat quality.  
Under existing laws, regulations, and governance arrangements, the project area would have likely become 
an  oil  palm  plantation  as  demonstrated  by  the  granted  licensed  in  2011  (Katingan Regent Decree 
No.525/229/KPTS/VIII/2011), followed   by   other   administrative   steps   and   approvals,   as   well   as the 
development of the adjacent oil palm concessions. There are no laws or regulations that require the project 
activities to be implemented, nor that prevent the project area from being converted into oil palm plantation.  
In conclusion, the project activities to conserve the critically endangered species and improve community 
livelihoods would  not  occur under  the  without-project  scenario,  as  the  project  area (i.e.,  forest,  peat  and

mangrove  ecosystems) would have been converted to oil palm plantation. If the project activities are not 
implemented, there will be no improvement in economy, education, health, and biodiversity conservation.  
Based on the NPV analysis by PT PUM (Appendix 4: NPV Calculation), the oil palm plantation’s NPV (USD 
72.3 million) is  almost twice as  high  as the  ecosystem  restoration  concession’s  NPV (USD 37.8 million). 
With oil palm plantation being a common business in Indonesia, many financial institutions and government 
policies support the business and provide ease of access to financing. This situation is in contrast with an 
ecosystem  restoration business that  mainly  relies on  carbon  finance  as  the  main  revenue.  With  limited 
access to finance (i.e., investment, loan), it is difficult for the project to access financial support to develop 
and  implement  the  project  activities in  the  absence  of  the  carbon  project. Hence, financial  support  to 
implement the project activities remains a significant barrier under the without-project scenario. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (G2.2) 
This project does not currently intend to use any community and biodiversity benefits described above (see 
Section 2.2.3) as offsets. The project might, in the future, apply for certification under the SD Vista Nature 
or another relevant biodiversity credit framework. The additionality for community and biodiversity benefits 
is demonstrated in the section above.  
2.3 Stakeholder Engagement  
2.3.1 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (G3.1) 
The project provides stakeholders with access to documents and other project information in oral, written, 
and virtual form, as described below: 
1. Regular  coordination.  Regular  coordination  will  be undertaken by  PT  PUM  for  government  at 
different  levels  (i.e.,  national,  provincial, regency,  and  village  governments).  For  community 
stakeholders, the coordination will be undertaken through village meetings. Furthermore, PT PUM 
staff will be permanently based in the field office and at the head office in Jakarta. 
2.
 
Printed version of project documents. The project description document (PD), monitoring reports, 
validation and verification reports and project activities  reports will be available in  English  and  in 
Bahasa Indonesia for consultation both at the project’s head office in Jakarta and in the field office. 
Information  and  news  related  to  the  project  will  also  be  conveyed  through  village  information 
boards.  
3.
 
Online access. Documents related to the project are available through virtual means on Verra and 
the project website (www.plumproject.id).  
2.3.2 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (G3.1) 
There are three types of stakeholders involved in this project, namely government, local communities, and 
business  entities. Summary project  information  has  been  disseminated  to local stakeholders during  the 
introductory meetings and FPIC process as described in Section 2.3.3. For all stakeholders, the summary 
of project documents will be shared and given access to through regular coordination, printed documents 
at the  project offices  and  village  information  boards,  and  online  resources through  Verra  and  the  project 
website (www.plumproject.id). 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
58 
Specifically for communities, the project documents and monitoring reports will be shared through village 
meetings and village information boards. Regular communication will occur during the project lifetime, as 
the project will closely involve the community in the project activities. Furthermore, the project will have a 
dedicated team  to  engage  with  community  to  ensure that the  project  is  benefiting  the  community  and 
obtains consent from community prior to implementing project activities. Hence, the communication will be 
continuous.  Formally,  at  least  two  village  meetings  will  be conducted annually  to  disseminate  project 
activities updates and reports. 
 
2.3.3 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (G3.1) 
Community  meetings  were  conducted  in  all  villages  prior  to  the  implementation  of  project  activities.  The 
meetings were publicized through the village leaders and were attended by the heads of village, community 
group  representatives,  and  the  project  representatives.  The  meetings  had  two  objectives:  first,  as  an 
introductory  meeting  to  the  project,  and  second,  to  gather  initial  opinions  of  the  communities  about  the 
project. These introductory meetings were followed by the FPIC process in each of the villages.  
A total of 13 villages are included as the target for community beneficiaries in this project. Eight out of these 
13 villages have granted their consent and have signed an agreement with the project. The FPIC process 
is still ongoing in the remaining five villages. Main issues highlighted by communities are to make a distinct 
boundary between PT PUM and communities’ lands, and ensure benefits from the project to the community. 
A summary of informational meetings with stakeholders (as part of the FPIC activities) is provided in Table 
2-13 below (refer  to Appendix 5:  FPIC  Consultation  Report for  more  detail  on  FPIC  process including 
evidence of consent). Meetings and consultations with local communities will be continued throughout the 
life of the project.
