2.3.4 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (G3.2) 
One of the initial activities carried out by PT PUM was to conduct socialization and provide explanations to 
the surrounding community about the impacts that may be caused by the project’s ecosystem restoration 
activities, including  the  costs,  risks  and  benefits  from  the  project.  From  the  FPIC  process,  the possible 
negative  impacts from  the  project  to  the  communities relate  to the  rights,  livelihoods  and  cultures  of the 
local communities, such as restrictions on customary land and resource rights, increased centralization of 
forest management and fairness of benefit sharing. 
The project has provided initial information to communities regarding the costs, risks, and benefits of the 
project as reflected in the FPIC report (see Appendix 5: FPIC Consultation Report). As FPIC is an iterative 
and continuous process, the project expects that some adjustments related to communities’ benefits will be 
undertaken as community expectations from the project evolve over the project lifetime. So far, the project 
has learned that communities expect benefits on livelihoods, education, and health (refer to Section 4.2.1). 
The project will maintain active community participation as a prerequisite for the realization of community 
benefits and will regularly discuss expectations from the carbon  project with  the  community.  Along  the 
project  lifetime,  more  detailed and  clear  benefits  will  be  defined  in  the  project. So  far,  however, the 
community  has  understood  and  provided  consent  on  the potential  costs,  risks  and  benefits  that they  will 
receive because of the project.  
2.3.5 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (G3.3) 
Participating communities in the project will be informed about the CCB and VCS validation and verification 
schedule by  the  project’s  site  team.  They  will  also  be  informed if  any  participation is required  from  their 
community and residents. Prior to the validation and verification, the project team will conduct a site visit to 
provide information to the villages through the village leaders.  
2.3.6 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (G3.3) 
The project team will inform the communities in the engaged villages about CCB and VCS validation and 
verification  activities  approximately  three  weeks  prior  to  the site  visit.  The  preparation  process  will  cover 
the following activities: 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
61 
- The project team will prepare validation and verification materials, such as the PD document 
and all supporting documents, as well as the summary validation and verification information 
for the communities. 
- The project team will approach and explain the activities to the engaged villages’ leaders.  
- Meetings with community beneficiaries of project activities will be arranged in consultation with 
the auditor. The arrangement can be adjusted to the needs of the auditor to ensure direct and 
independent communication between the communities and the auditor.  
- The project team will create a visiting route considering the distance, time, and transportation 
between one village to another village, and the forest area within the project area. 
2.3.7 Stakeholder Consultations (G3.4) 
Stakeholder consultations are being conducted as part of the FPIC process. The first phase of consultations 
was undertaken in 2022 to introduce the project in general, consult on the project activities, and obtain the 
communities’ consent, as described in the FPIC report (see Appendix 5: FPIC Consultation Report). Within 
the  communities,  several  community  groups were identified  and  consulted who influenced  the  project 
design, including wood loggers, farmers, fishermen, women’s groups, and village communities in general. 
These groups  provided  input  to  the  project  as  part  of the risk  mitigation  strategy  for  minimizing  potential 
negative impacts on their livelihoods. For community groups who engage in activities that are not legally 
permissible (i.e., wood loggers), the project proposed alternative solutions to mitigate the illegal activities 
while maintaining community participation through livelihood activities (e.g., agroforestry).  
Furthermore, consultations  were held regarding  the SIMURP  project, which constructed  irrigation  canals 
for  paddy  fields  that partially overlap  with  PT PUM  concession. The SIMURP  project  supports around 
13,000  ha  of existing paddy  fields in  the  project  zone. A  10-km  stretch  of the  irrigation  canals is  located 
inside the PUM concession. Based on communities’ input, the irrigation and community farms located inside 
PT PUM have been excluded from the project area. 
2.3.8 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (G3.4) 
The project proponent will maintain regular and frequent communication and consultation with communities. 
The communication and consultation processes are part of the project’s adaptive management, an iterative 
process to identify the most effective management actions in achieving the project’s goal.  
The  project  proponent  will  implement an adaptive  management approach,  which  involves relevant 
communities. The project proponent will use regular meetings as the channel for this communication. The 
meetings  will  provide  an  opportunity  to  gather  inputs,  suggestions,  and  opinions  from  communities.  The 
inputs will be noted and considered in improving the project actions, as described in Figure 2-4 below. 
As part of the adaptive management approach, the project will regularly discuss climate change impacts 
with local communities and identify additional measures that can be integrated into the project activities to 
ensure that they are adaptive to climate change.

arranged  through  the  village  leaders in  line  with  local  customs.  The  project  proponent  will  encourage

2.3.11 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (G3.7) 
The project   operates under   the   constitution,   laws and   regulations of Indonesia. With regard   to 
discrimination, the project will comply with the constitution and relevant laws, among others: 
•
 
Constitution 1945 (UUD 1945) especially section 27 
• Law No. No. 40/2008 on the elimination of racial and ethnic discrimination 
• Law No. 1/2023 concerning the criminal law code 
In addition, the project will comply with any regulations on manpower and safety of workers, among others 
Law  No.  1/1970  on  the  Occupational  Safety  (Occupational  Safety  Law).  All  employees  will  receive  an 
induction on work safety, employment and be provided with an employee pocketbook when they join the 
company. 
The company will implement best practices in recruiting employees, including implementing a policy of zero 
tolerance for sexual harassment and no discrimination to gender, religion, ethnicity, and sexual orientation 
while taking into account job availability, educational background and workforce skills. 
2.3.12 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (G3.8) 
The  project’s  grievance  procedure  includes  grievance  registration,  verification,  identification  of  possible 
solutions with the complainant, taking action to resolve grievances, reporting and monitoring the result. All 
grievances will be processed within a specific timeframe (generally within 15 working days), in a transparent 
and accountable manner. 
The complainant should submit the grievance verbally or in writing to the project office. After receiving the 
grievance, the project will response through formal letter to the complainant. Based on the grievance, the 
project  team  will  develop  a  necessary  action  plan,  implement,  monitor,  evaluate  and  communicate  the 
action and result to the complainant. Figure 2-5 depicts the grievance process involving several parties. 
Moreover, in the FPIC process, there was an agreement between the project proponent and the community 
on a conflict resolution mechanism consisting of: 
1. Project proponent and community will conduct an investigation regarding the related dispute. 
2. Project proponent and community will discuss to reach deliberations and consensus. 
3. If deliberation and consensus do not produce a meeting point, then a third party will be appointed 
as a mediator agreed by both parties. 
4. If all efforts to reach a consensus have not been able to resolve the dispute, then both parties agree 
to bring it to the level of law in force in Indonesia. 
The grievance procedure lays out the ways in which complainants submit or deliver grievances, as well as 
the  ways  in  which  the project  handles  the  grievances.  Unless  the  complainant  requests  anonymity,  the 
complainant should  provide  clear identity  (name,  address,  ID  number,  phone)  and  description  of  the 
grievance as well as the background, expectation, and evidence. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
64 
The project  and  the  project  proponent  have zero  tolerance  for  threats,  intimidation,  violence,  or  reprisals 
towards  any complainants or  their  representative(s). The principles of protection  of  Human  Rights, 
Complainants, and Community Spokespersons will be adhered to, including:  
• Peaceful Resolution of Disputes  
• Confidentiality 
• Anonymity if requested 
• Participation and Informed Consent 
• Conflict of Interest 
• Equality and non-discrimination 
• Prevention 
• Protection

The  feedback  and  grievance  redress  procedure  will  be  communicated  to  the  local  communities  through 
community meetings and  village  boards  and  will  be  accessible  to  all  villagers. Complainants  can  submit 
grievances  verbally  or  in  writing  to  the  project  office. The  grievances  and  project  responses  will  be 
documented and reported every six months by the project team and, unless confidential, made available 
for stakeholders to view at the project office upon request.

2.3.15 Community Employment Opportunities (G3.10) 
All community members will have equal opportunity to apply for employment opportunities offered by the 
project. Workers will be selected based on a competitive recruitment process implemented by the project 
proponent. Women and vulnerable groups will be encouraged and given equal opportunity to apply for all 
positions, including management positions, if the requirements for the vacancy are fulfilled. 
2.3.16 Relevant Laws and Regulations Related to Worker’s Rights (G3.11) 
Indonesian employment law is governed by Law No. 13/2003, dated 15 March 2003, regarding Manpower 
(Labour  Law),  as  amended  by  Law  No.  11/2020,  dated  2  November  2020,  and  as  amended  by  Perpu 
(Government Regulation in lieu of Law) No. 2 year 2022 dated 30 December 2022 regarding Job Creation 
(Job  Creation  Law)  (Labour  Law,  as  amended).  The  government  has  issued  the  following  regulations  to 
implement the Labour Law: 
- Government   Regulation   No.   35/2021,   dated   2   February   2021,   regarding   Fixed-Term 
Employment  Agreement,  Outsourcing,  Working  Hours  and Rest  Hours,  and  Termination  of 
Employment (GR 35/2021); and 
- Government Regulation No. 36/2021, dated 2 February 2021, regarding Wages (GR 36/2021) 
The other main statutes are: 
- Law  No.  2/2004,  dated  14  January  2004,  regarding  Industrial  Relations  Dispute  Settlement; 
and 
- Law No. 21/2000, dated 4 August 2000, regarding Labour Unions. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3

In addition to the above laws and regulations, employers and employees are also subject to the relevant 
company  regulations  (or  work  rules)  or  collective  labour  agreement  (CLA),  as  applicable,  as  well  as  the 
provisions of the employment agreement between the employer and employee. Workers will be informed 
about their rights during the recruitment and induction process. 
 
2.3.17 Occupational Safety Assessment (G3.12) 
Within the proposed project activities, there are certain risks associated with the activities that can generate 
a  negative  impact  on  the  health  and  safety  of  the  participants  due  to  the  project’s  nature  in  peat  and 
mangrove ecosystems. Potential risks include: 
• Accidents due to improper handling of tools 
• Insect and spider bites 
•
 
Snake bites 
•
 
Wildlife attacks (bears, wild boars) 
• Falls, which can lead to contusions, fractures, sprains, etc. 
• Overturning of boats 
• Drowning 
• Exposure to fire and smoke during fire management activities 
•
 
Getting lost in the field during patrolling activities 
To reduce the risks associated with each activity, the project will hold meetings and trainings prior to the 
implementation of the activity where, in addition to discussing technical issues, the risks and measures to 
mitigate them are discussed. The measures proposed to minimize risks are designed in such a way that 
they  are  aligned  with  the  cultural  practices  of  the  community,  so  that  these  safety  measures  are  easily 
accepted and complied with by the communities involved. For example, the project will provide navigation 
tools and markings on clothing, work in a buddy system, require the use of personal protective equipment 
(PPE), and provide anti-snake venom serum. A Manual on Safety and Health at Work including SOPs will 
be  provided with  the  aim to achieve zero  incidents. Incident  reporting  forms  and  an  emergency  plan  to 
ensure  occupational  safety  during  activities carried  out  within  the  framework  of  the  project  are  also 
presented, in accordance with legal requirements.

2.4.3 Management Team Experience (G4.2) 
Dr. Handoko Limaho is the Chief Executive Officer (CEO) of Hutan Kencana Group, the parent company 
of PT Pagatan Usaha Makmur (PT PUM). He is one of the main decision makers of the project proponent.  
In 2021, Dr. Limaho obtained his Research Doctorate in Management specialized in sustainability issues 
at Universitas Pelita Harapan. Prior to that, he had obtained a bachelor’s degree (BSc.) in Cell Biology from 
the University of Manchester and a master’s degree (MSc.) in Management from the University of Salford. 
In 2008, he began his career as an Account Manager at Carphone Warehouse, and later as a Director at 
PT  Wangijaya  Sejahtera,  a  marketing  company,  where  he  was  in  charge  of  the  marketing  strategy  of 
several Fast-Moving Consumer Goods (FMCG) companies. 
Subsequently,  he  worked  for  Agro  Investama  Group  (AIG)  for  nearly  ten  years.  His  last  position  before 
leaving the company was COO, where he led the operational team to drive and increase performance. He 
also conducted collaborative actions between stakeholders such as NGOs, government bodies, and others 
to create a more sustainable organization. In 2021, Dr. Limaho’s family decided to exit the oil palm business 
and  instead develop ecosystem  restoration projects.  In  his  current  position  as  CEO  of  Hutan  Kencana 
Group, he is leading a nature-based solutions company that specializes in ecosystem restoration from the 
ground up.

Panji Ahmad Fauzan, S.Hut is the project’s Biodiversity and Forest Protection Coordinator. In 2010, he

2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (G4.3) 
As  a  private  company  engaged  in  Ecosystem  Restoration  services, the project proponent  strives  to 
constantly  improve  its  performance  quality and carry  out  work  in  accordance  with  applicable  laws  and 
regulations. The project proponent established an anti-corruption policy
46
 and culture in the workplace by 
preventing, anticipating, and eliminating bribery or illegal levies and gratuities in all work operations when 
performing services to foster cooperation and long-term partnerships. The following are the specifics of the 
anti-bribery framework that has been put in place: 
1. Reducing the potential for bribes and extortion in all lines of work operations that affect them. 
2. Avoiding giving and receiving gratuities in all areas of work. 
3. Providing assurance  to  service  users  that  there  is  no  bribery,  extortion,  or  gratification  in  any 
construction work service provided. 
4. Increasing partners’ (goods/services providers) confidence that there is no bribery, extortion, and 
gratification practice in every procurement process. 
5. Ensuring the integrity of the project proponent’s employees.

2.5 Legal Status and Property Rights  
2.5.1 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (G5.1) 
There are no outside statutory claims in the project area as the project has officially received all statutory 
rights to manage the area under the license as described in Section 2.5.8. However as described in Section 
2.1.6, the villages  surrounding  the  project  area have  been  engaged  in  a  FPIC  process to  identify  any 
customary  rights  and  access  to the  project  area. As  described  previously, some  villagers hold  informal 
management rights over tatah/canals in the project area that are used for fishing and transportation. 
2.5.2 Recognition of Property Rights (G5.1) 
The  project  proponent  is  committed  to  recognizing,  respecting,  and  supporting  all  property  rights  of 
communities  and  other  stakeholders.  The  assessments undertaken during  the  FPIC  process,  village 
profiles, and livelihood assessment identified that there is no community property in the project area such 
as people living permanently in the area. 
2.5.3 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (G5.2) 
The FPIC process ensures that community rights are respected when activities of reducing emissions from 
deforestation and forest degradation and enhancing forest carbon stocks (REDD+) are implemented. The 
project proponent designed and conducted an FPIC process to obtain the consent of the communities living 
in the project zone for the project implementation. As described in the previous Section 2.5.2, there is no 
community property in the project area. Nonetheless, the project proponent conducted an FPIC process, 
as the project plan may affect communities’ practices, such as fishing, hunting, harvesting non-timber forest 
products,  or  timber  removal  for  house  construction,  and  may influence  the  communities’ income  or 
livelihood cycle. 
The FPIC process was conducted as the initial participatory process of community involvement in project 
implementation. The FPIC process is described below: 
1. Project introduction to 17 villages surrounding the project area. The project proponent introduced 
the project and the team to the villages. The meetings were conducted in each village and attended 
by the heads of the villages and representatives of communities. 
2. Village consultations. After the introduction, the project proponent conducted village consultations. 
In these meetings,  the  project  proponent  explained  the  project  objectives,  plan,  and  activities  in 
detail and showed the project area map. The consultation was a two-way communication, where 
the project proponent described the project in detail and listened to the responses, comments, and 
concerns  of  the  community. The  communities’ concerns  were  discussed  and  addressed (see 
Appendix 5: FPIC Consultation Report). The detailed agenda of consultation was as follows: 
a) Explanation of the project: objectives, plan, legal basis, and project area map in large size. 
b) Community’s activities or interactions with the project area. 
c) Community’s responses, comments, questions, and concerns.  
d) Agreed points as the discussion results and meeting notes. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
75 
 The final  project  zone  area and  engaged  villages were determined in  this  process based  on  the 
stakeholder inputs. 
3. Obtaining consent. After the consultation process, the project team documented the consultation 
note  which  was  signed  by  the  parties.  The  note  covered  information:  date,  day,  attendees, 
discussed concerns, agreed points, and signature of parties. 
4. Negotiation process. The consultation was conducted in 17 villages, and each village consultation 
had  a  different  obtained  consent  process. Based  on  the  consultation  process,  13  villages  were 
included  in  the  project  zone;  the  other  four  villages were  determined  to  have limited  interactions 
with the project area. The results and status of the FPIC process in the 13 engaged villages can be 
found in Appendix 5:  FPIC  Consultation  Report. Eight out  of the 13  villages  have  granted  their 
consent and have signed an agreement with the project. The FPIC process is still ongoing in the 
remaining five villages. The FPIC process is an iterative process that will be continued throughout 
the life of the project.  
5. Monitoring  plan  and  team.  The meeting  participants  discussed a participatory  monitoring  plan 
where parties – the project team and community – together will monitor the project implementation. 
For the joint monitoring activities, the project team and communities agreed to establish monitoring 
teams composed of the project team and representatives of community.  
6. The  signing  parties. The  meeting notes/results  were  signed  by  the  heads  of  villages,  community 
representatives, and a representative of the project. 
2.5.4 Property Rights Protection (G5.3) 
The project activities do not lead to involuntary removal or relocation of property rights holders from their 
lands  or  territories  and  does  not  force  rights  holders  to  relocate  activities  important  to  their  culture  or 
livelihood. For example, the river fishermen group uses the canals inside the forest in the project area as 
fishing ground. The project team will not prevent these fishermen from fishing in the forest after the start of 
the project activities but will educate them to apply environmentally friendly fishing techniques. 
 
2.5.5 Illegal Activity Identification (G5.4) 
Based on the biodiversity and livelihood assessment and discussion with the communities, there were some 
identified small-scale illegal activities as follows: 
- Illegal  logging.  Although  the volume of  removed  timber  is  small and  mostly  used for  house 
construction, it has the potential to cause harm to the environment in the project area.  
- Illegal hunting. The villagers hunt using traditional traps.  
- Destructive fishing methods including electrofishing and fish poisoning. 
The  project  team  has  identified  the  drivers  and developed  activities  to  mitigate them.  The  activities  are  as 
follows: 
- Conduct patrol and include illegal logging and hunting into the monitored activities list. 
- Provide  alternative  livelihoods for  loggers and  hunters.  The  project  includes  livelihood  training, 
revolving fund, and other supporting activities to empower these community groups. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
76 
 
2.5.6 Ongoing Disputes (G5.5) 
There are no ongoing or unresolved disputes over rights to lands, territories and resources in the project 
area. During  the  FPIC  process,  it  was  identified  that some small-scale illegal  logging,  fishing  and  rice 
farming activities are conducted within the project area. Also, some community members have customary 
management  rights  over  tatah  (trenches/canals  where  logs  were  transported  in  the  past)  in  PT  PUM 
concession. Before the project started, these tatah were still partially used for transportation of wood, but 
were mostly used for fishing. The project will not restrict the customary rights of local communities and will 
continue  to  allow access  for  fishing,  NTFP  collection,  and  access  to  tatah/canals. During  the  FPIC 
consultations, it was clarified that (i) PT PUM will not block these canals without the consent of the tatah 
holders, and the design will be discussed with them; (ii) fishing activities will continue to be allowed; (iii) as 
logging and wood collection will no longer be permissible (by law), PT PUM will work closely with affected 
individuals to provide alternatives. 
Some communities were concerned that the boundaries between their rice paddy fields and PT PUM were 
not  clear,  and  that  it  could  affect  their  rice  farming  activities.  To  address  these  concerns,  the  FPIC  team 
conducted participatory mapping exercises in each village to clarify the boundaries. It also followed up with 
related  parties  (including  the  SIMURP  program)  to  clarify  the  boundaries  and any future  plans  for 
expansion
47
. This helped address concerns in the affected villages and FPIC agreement was reached with 
them.  Any potential additional  concerns  will  be  addressed  through  regular  communication  with  the 
concerned villages. It is noted that 182 hectares of paddy fields (since 2014) are located inside PT PUM, 
to which an additional 286 hectares were added under SIMURP. These areas, located along the boundary 
of  the  concession, have  been  excluded  from  the  project  area,  and  farmers will  be  allowed  to  continue 
cultivating these fields. 
The project  aims  to  bring  exceptional  benefits  to  the  community  (refer  to Section 4.5)  and  biodiversity 
(Section 5.5), while restoring the land for reducing and removing emissions through forest protection and 
ecosystem rehabilitation.  
 
2.5.7 National and Local Laws (G5.6)

Indonesia,  especially  laws  regulating  forest  conservation  projects. Necessary  approvals  are  obtained  by 
the project in line with these laws and regulations. Relevant laws and regulations include

2.5.9 Project Ownership (G5.8) 
PT Pagatan Usaha Makmur is the legitimate owner of the project for the establishment of responsibility and

2.5.10 Management of Double Counting Risk (G5.9) 
The project generates benefits to the climate, communities and biodiversity. However, only net reductions 
and removals of greenhouse gases will be marketed. The project is not part of any other public or private 
sector programs that account for emission related benefits.

2.5.11 Emissions Trading Programs and Other Binding Limits  
This project does not participate or is included in any emission trading schemes other than VCS and other 
binding limits. However, approvals are obtained from Indonesia’s National Registry System (SRN)
49
 which 
is the Indonesian GHG emission registry. 
 
2.5.12 Other Forms of Environmental Credit  
The  project  is  not  intended  to  generate  any  other  form  of  environmental  credits  related  to  the  reductions 
and removals of GHG emissions claimed under the VCS Program. 
 
2.5.13 Participation under Other GHG Programs  
The project is not participating under other GHG Programs. In compliance with Indonesian regulations, the 
project has been listed under Indonesia’s SRN with identification number 005-V-2023-4005. 
 
2.5.14 Projects Rejected by Other GHG Programs  
The  project  has  not  undergone  validation/verification  of  any  other  GHG  program  and  is  therefore  not 
rejected by any other GHG program. 
 
2.5.15 Double Counting (G5.9) 
As referred to in Section 2.5.11 of this document, this VCS project is submitted for approval under the VCS 
Program and SRN in Indonesia. Hence, there will be no double counting.

3 CLIMATE 
3.1 Application of Methodology 
3.1.1 Title and Reference of Methodology  
The  methodology  title  and  reference are the  approved  VCS Methodology  VM0007  REDD+  Methodology 
Framework (REDD+ MF), v.1.6, 8 September 2020. 
The tools  applied  by  the  project are: 1) the VT0001  Tool  for  the Demonstration  and  Assessment  of 
Additionality in AFOLU Project Activities, v.3.0, 1 February 2012 (T-ADD); 2) the Agriculture, Forestry, and 
Other  Land  Use  (AFOLU)  Non-Permanence Risk Tool (NPRT), v.4.2, 12 October 2023 (T-BAR); 3) Tool 
for testing significance of GHG emissions in A/R CDM project activities (T-SIG) v.0.1. 
 
3.1.2 Applicability of Methodology 
The project applies the latest version of VCS Methodology VM0007 (Version 1.6 dated 08 September 2020) 
also called ‘REDD+ MF’ together with applicable pre-framed modules and tools. The modules were selected 
based on the PLUM Project activities covered by the VM0007 including (1) Avoiding Planned Deforestation 
(APD), (2) Afforestation, Reforestation and Revegetation (ARR), (3) Restoration of Wetlands Ecosystems 
(RWE)  on  Peatland  and  Tidal  Wetlands,  and  (4)  Avoiding  Planned  Wetland  Degradation  (APWD) on 
Peatland and  Tidal  Wetlands.  All  applicability  conditions  of  the  VM0007 (REDD+  MF)  and  its  associated 
modules/tools are met by the project. 
Table 3-1. Analysis of applicability conditions for the VM0007 methodology.

12 REDD+ MF 
Version 1.6, 
Section 4.5.2, 
RWE Project 
Activities 
General 
For RWE project activities, prior to the 
project start date, the project area 
must meet the following conditions: 
The area is free of any land use that 
could be displaced outside the project 
area, as demonstrated by at least one 
of the following, where relevant:
 
• The project area has been 
abandoned for two or more years 
prior to the project start date; or 
• Use of the project area for 
commercial purposes (i.e., trade) is 
not profitable as a result of salinity 
intrusion, market forces, or other 
factors. In addition, timber harvesting 
in the baseline scenario within the 
project area does not occur; or 
• Degradation of additional wetlands 
for new agricultural/aquacultural sites 
within the country will not occur or is 
prohibited by enforced law. 
OR 
b) The area is under a land use that 
could be displaced outside the project 
area, although in such case, baseline 
emissions from this land use must not 
be accounted for, and where 
degradation of additional wetlands for 
new agricultural/aquacultural sites 
within the country will not occur or is 
prohibited by enforced law. 
OR 
Condition met. The project will 
continue at a similar or greater level of 
service or production during the project 
crediting period as the concession is 
under a Forest Utilization Business 
License (PBPH). The license is valid 
until the end of the project crediting 
period. In line with the granted license, 
the project will develop a long-term 
and annual management plan 
(RKU/RKT) which will include 
restoration of wetland ecosystem in the 
project area.

14 REDD+ MF 
Version 1.6, 
Section 4.5.2, 
RWE Project 
Activities Tidal 
Wetland 
Restoration 
Project activities restoring tidal 
wetlands may include any of the 
following, or combinations of the 
following: 
> Creating, restoring and/or managing 
hydrological conditions (e.g., 
removing tidal barriers, improving 
hydrological connectivity, restoring 
tidal flow to wetlands or lowering 
water levels on impounded wetlands) 
> Altering sediment supply (e.g., 
beneficial use of dredge material or 
diverting river sediments to sediment-
starved areas) 
> Changing salinity characteristics 
(e.g., restoring tidal flow to tidally-
restricted areas) 
> Improving water quality (e.g., 
reducing nutrient loads leading to 
improved water clarity to expand 
seagrass meadows, recovering tidal 
and other hydrologic flushing and 
exchange or reducing nutrient 
residence time) 
> (Re-)introducing native plant 
communities (e.g., reseeding or 
replanting) 
> Improving management practice(s) 
(e.g., removing invasive species, 
reduced grazing) 
> In RWE-ARR project activities, the 
prescribed burning of herbaceous and 
shrub aboveground biomass (cover 
burns) may occur 
Condition met. The project will: 
i. (Re-)introduce native plant 
communities (e.g., reseeding or 
replanting) 
ii. Improve management practices(s)

85 
No Module Applicability Condition Comment 
 
Project activities conserving tidal 
wetlands may 
include: 
1. Protecting at risk wetlands (e.g., 
establishing conservation easements, 
establishing community supported 
management agreements, 
establishing protective government 
regulations, and preventing disruption 
of water and/ or sediment supply to 
wetland areas) 
2. Improving water management on 
drained wetlands 
3. Maintaining or improving water 
quality for seagrass meadows 
4. Recharging sediment to avoid 
drowning of coastal wetlands 
5. Creating accommodation space for 
wetlands migrating with sea level rise 
Furthermore, the project will conserve 
tidal wetlands by:  
1. Protecting at risk wetlands 
2. Improving water management on 
drained wetlands

in the project boundary because 
omission is conservative.

assumes that all-natural forest

and this will logically reduce baseline 
biomass including non-tree above 
ground biomass to zero and 
significantly less than in the project 
scenario.

Where selective logging is taking 
place in the project case: 
Emissions from logging may be 
omitted if it can be demonstrated the 
emissions are de minimis using Tool 
T SIG. 
If emissions from logging are not 
omitted as de minimis, logging may 
only take place within forest 
management areas that possess and 
maintain a Forest Stewardship 
Council (FSC) certificate for the years 
when the selective logging occurs. 
Logging operations may only conduct 
selective logging that maintains a land 
cover that meets the definition of 
forest within the project boundary. 
All trees cut for timber extraction 
during logging operations must have 
a DBH greater than 30 cm. 
During logging operations, only the 
bole/log of the felled tree may be 
removed. The top/crown of the tree 
must remain within the forested area. 
The logging practices cannot include 
the piling and/or burning of logging 
slash Volume of timber harvested 
must be measured and monitored. 
Condition not applicable. The project 
does not involve selective logging or 
any timber harvesting.

To  define the  project  boundaries,  the  project  team  conducted a land cover  assessment  using  the  VCS 
Module X-STR. The maps below show the project boundary with physical locations of the various project 
activities, as well as the leakage belt. The leakage belt boundary corresponds to the project zone boundary 
as shown in Map 3-1 and explained in Section 3.2.3. 
The  PLUM project area  is  identified  as  eligible  for  various  project  activities  under  the VM0007  REDD+ 
Methodology   Framework,   including   REDD   (Reducing   Emissions   from   Deforestation   and   Forest 
Degradation),  WRC  (Wetland  Restoration  and  Conservation),  and  ARR  (Afforestation,  Reforestation  and 
Revegetation). Specifically, the area eligible for REDD spans 5,523.14 hectares, constituting approximately 
23.85% of the total project area. To qualify for REDD, the designated area must meet the requirement that 
the  land within  the  project  area  has qualified as  a  forest  for  a  minimum  of  10  years  before  the  project's 
commencement. 
For Wetland Restoration and Conservation (WRC), the eligible area encompasses 21,009.91 hectares on 
Peat, representing 90.74% of the total project area, and an additional 2,143.84 hectares on Tidal Wetlands, 
accounting  for  9.25%  of  the  total  project  area.  Meanwhile,  for  the Afforestation,  Reforestation  and 
Revegetation (ARR)  activity,  the  eligible  area  is  15,087.59  hectares,  constituting  65.12%  of  the  overall 
project  area.  An  essential  criterion  for  an  area  to  be  considered  eligible  for  ARR  is  that  the  project  area 
should be non-forest land or land with degraded forest, and it must not have undergone clearance of native 
ecosystems  to  achieve  greenhouse  gas  emissions  reductions  or  removals. About  1,079  ha out of  the 
15,087.59 hectares of ARR area was still forest in 2012 but was burnt by forest fires in 2015. It is noted that 
PT  PUM  was  not  operating  as  an  oil  palm  plantation during  this  period as  it  was  still  in  the  process  of 
obtaining  all  the  necessary  approvals.  The fire was likely the  result  of small-scale  local  activities  (fires, 
fishing, illegal  logging) and  exacerbated  by  the  El Niño  event  in  2015. This  situation  demonstrates  that, 
prior to the project initiation, activities were not implemented to generate GHG emissions for the purpose 
of their subsequent reduction. 
Please refer to Map 3-1 for the location of eligible areas.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario 
This section provides justification for the project’s baseline and additionality using the “VT0001 Tool for the 
demonstration and assessment of additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forestry and Other Land Use (AFOLU) 
Project Activities (version 3.0)” which was adapted from the “combined tool to identify the baseline scenario 
and demonstrate additionality in A/R CDM project activities: Version 2”. 
This tool is applicable to the project as the project fulfils the following conditions: 
a)
 
AFOLU activities the same or similar to the proposed project activity on the land within the proposed 
project boundary performed with or without being registered as the VCS AFOLU project do not lead 
to violation of any applicable law even if the law is not enforced; 
b)
 
The  project  used  the  tool  to  determine  additionality  as  the  tool  provides a  stepwise  approach 
justifying the determination of the most plausible baseline scenario.  
Step 1.  Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
Sub-step 1a.  Identify credible alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
A list of realistic and credible alternative land use scenarios that would have occurred in the project area in 
the absence of the project is provided in Table 3-4. These scenarios were identified by analysing the pre-
project  land  use,  the  current  land  use  across similar  ecosystems  and  land  use  classifications  in Central 
Kalimantan,  the  land  use  trends in other  similar  regions  of  Indonesia  (i.e., Sumatra  Island),  and  the 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
97 
observed similar activities in the geographical area with similar socio-economic and ecological conditions 
to the proposed project activity. 
All identified land use scenarios are deemed realistic and credible, as the land-uses within the boundary of 
the proposed project are currently existing or existed at some time in the period beginning ten years prior 
to the project start date but no longer exist.  
 
Table 3-4. Identified land use scenarios in the absence of the project. 
Land Use Scenario Description 
Conservation project 
without being 
registered as a VCS 
AFOLU project 
The project concession could be acquired by a conservation organization with 
long-term philanthropic funding and converted into an ecosystem restoration 
project. 
Industrial oil palm 
plantation 
Oil palm is one of the most common non-forest commodity industrial land uses 
of lowland peatlands in Indonesia. The area of oil palm plantations in Indonesia 
has increased dramatically over the past decade. Oil palm cultivation requires 
maintaining the water table of the peat to below 70cm.  
 
In Central Kalimantan Province, the oil palm plantation area increased by 71% 
(1.5 million ha) between 2013 and 2018 alone (BPS, 2023) on non-state forest 
land (Areal Penggunaan Lain, APL) and convertible state forest land (Hutan 
Produksi Konversi, HPK). 
 
82.56% of the project area is on HPK, and before PT PUM applied for the 
Forest Utilization Business License (PBPH), the area was designated to 
become hybrid coconut oil plantation in 2009 (PT PUM, Katingan Regent 
Decree No. 335/2009) and oil palm plantation in 2011 (PT PUM, Katingan 
Regent Decree No. 525/229/KPTS/VIII/2011). The environmental permit for oil 
palm plantation granted to PT PUM in 2013
51
, followed by other administrative 
steps and approvals. 
 
Before 2021, PT PUM was part of the PT Agro Inti Semesta (PT AIS) group
52
 
who owns two other oil palm plantations in the area, namely PT Katingan 
Mujur Sejahtera (PT KMS) and PT Trubus Bumi Sejahtera (PT TBS). PT PUM 
was planned to be developed as an oil palm plantation along with PT KMS and 
PT TBS. PT KMS has already started conversion of the peat forest into oil 
palm plantation, made canals, and secured HGU
53
; PT TBS has obtained a 
Plantation Business Permit (Izin Usaha Perkebunan or IUP) from the Katingan 
Regent, and has secured funding. Without the PLUM Project, it is highly likely 
that PT PUM’s area would have continued to be developed into an oil palm 
plantation by PT AIS. 
 
 
51
 Katingan  Regent

(No.525.21/172/KPTS/IV/2013)

 Hak Guna Usaha (Right to Cultivate). 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3

98 
Land Use Scenario Description 
Furthermore, there is one more oil palm plantation (PT KSB – not in PT AIS 
group) that has been designated in the area surrounding the project and 
already secured the permit to convert the state-forest land into non state-forest 
land. This illustrates that the project zone was envisioned as an oil palm 
production area. 
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
Industrial acacia plantation is another common land use in lowland Indonesia, 
which also requires maintaining the water table of the peat to below 70cm. 
Acacia plantations have been expanding in Indonesia between 2011-2020, 
from 9.6 million ha in 2011 to 11.2 million ha in 2020, and more than 50% are 
located in peatland
54
. 
Logging concession Since 1970 until late 1990s, most businesses on Kalimantan Island were 
logging concessions. In the last decade, only few logging concessions 
operated in Central Kalimantan Province, and none operates on wetlands in 
Central Kalimantan today. 
 
However, logging concession remains a legal possibility in the project area 
despite the unlikely practice mainly due to the low commercial timber 
availability. Most logging concessions in Central Kalimantan Province are on 
mineral soil (non-wetland) in the northern part of the province, while the 
southern part of the province is predominantly being developed for oil palm 
plantations (Map 3-2). 
Smallholder 
agriculture/farming 
 
Smallholder agricultural land occupies around 15% of the planted oil palm area 
in Central Kalimantan Province
55
.  
 
In the direct vicinity of the project area, there are over 13,000 ha of paddy 
fields from 13 villages, providing the main income and livelihood to the local 
communities in the area. Under the SIMURP project, the government has 
supported the smallholder farmers by constructing an irrigation system. This 
involved digging of a 4m x 4m canal directly adjacent to the project area. 
 
Although smallholder agriculture/farming is not seen as a likely land use for the 
project area, it remains considered as a plausible scenario given that it is one 
of the primary land uses in Indonesia and in the surroundings of the project 
area. 
Unprotected Forest – 
Continuation of pre-
project land use 
In Indonesia, unprotected and unexploited forests are not uncommon, but they 
typically exist as a temporary state between phases of commercial or local 
exploitation. These neglected forest areas are often degraded, leading to a 
loss of their commercial value for timber. The vicinity of the project area has 
seen this pattern play out, with unprotected forests being replaced by oil palm 
plantations and smallholder agriculture. 
Protected Forest There are instances of logging concessions in Central Kalimantan Province 
that have been converted to protection forest, especially in areas with 
Production Forest (Hutan Produksi, HP) status on state-forest land. Some

 The State of Indonesia’s Forests 2020 (MoEF).

petani-kecil-kelapa-sawit-di-kalimantan-tengah 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3

examples were former logging concessions that became Sebangau National 
Park and a number of areas of Protection Forest (Hutan Lindung). Although 
only 17.44% of the project area was Production Forest, the scenario to 
become protected forest is plausible. 
Apart  from  the  six  major  land  use  scenarios  described  above,  several  smaller  land  uses  were  analyzed, 
such  as  the  development  of  fish  ponds,  infrastructure,  and  settlements. Nevertheless,  these  were  not 
retained as they were not deemed sufficiently significant and credible.

Sub-step 1b.  Consistency of credible land use scenarios with enforced mandatory applicable laws and 
regulations 
The consistency of the six major land use scenarios identified under Sub-step 1a with mandatory laws and 
regulations  in  Indonesia was  then  considered. In  particular,  the  analysis  took  into  account the  legal 
designation of the project area as 17.44% production forest (HP) and 82.56% convertible production forest 
(HPK). 
 
Land Use Scenario Legality 
Conservation project 
without being 
registered as a VCS

regulations (including the Forestry Law No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the 
Ministry of Forestry Regulation No. 31/2014 and supporting regulations 
including the latest MoEF Regulation No. 7/2021). A PBPH license for

Land Use Scenario Legality 
ecosystem restoration could potentially be obtained by a non-profit or for-profit 
organization that implements a conservation project without carbon finance. 
Industrial oil palm 
plantation 
This scenario is consistent with applicable laws and regulations. Oil palm can 
be legally established on land designated as HPK with a request to convert the 
area to become APL (as per the recent MoEF Regulation No. 7/2021). The 
application to convert HPK into APL had been submitted by the former license 
holder in the project area (before PT PUM) (Letter No.01/PUM/P.KS/VII/ 2013). 
In addition, there are 43 oil palm plantation permits granted on lands with 
Production Forest status within 100 km radius of PT PUM (Map 3-3). 
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
Acacia plantation is consistent with applicable laws and regulations, including 
the Forestry Law No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the Ministry of Forestry 
Regulation No. 31/2014 and supporting regulations including the latest MoEF 
Regulation No. 7/2021. 
Logging concession Logging concession is also legally a possibility in line with the Forestry Law 
No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the Ministry of Forestry Regulation No. 
31/2014 and relevant regulations including the latest MoEF Regulation No. 
7/2021. 
Smallholder 
agriculture/farming 
 
As production forest, the project area cannot be legally converted to 
smallholder agriculture. Nevertheless, smallholders often target forest land that 
is neglected and not subject to active concession licenses or commercial 
exploitation. In the absence of a commercial license, smallholders may seek to 
assert ownership of the occupied land. 
Unprotected Forest – 
Continuation of pre-
project land use 
In the absence of an application for a concession license, the project area 
could continue to be classified as production forest in line with applicable 
forestry laws and regulations and remain unprotected. 
Protected Forest Forest land in the project area could undergo a legal process to be converted 
to protection forest or conservation forest. However, this is a complex legal 
process that is subject to a number of laws and regulations (MoEF Regulation 
No. 7/2021). 
Sub-step 1c.  Selection of the baseline scenario 
In  conclusion,  all  the  plausible  scenarios are  retained  for  analysis  in  the  following  step. Although  the 
smallholder agriculture scenario could be rejected due to the illegality, it was retained as it remains common 
across Indonesia and in the surroundings of the project area. 
VT0001 provides the option to undertake either Step 2 (Investment analysis) or Step 3 (Barrier analysis). 
Step  3 was  selected to  be  used  to  determine  the  baseline  and  additionality. Nevertheless, the  project 
undertook an analysis  of the  Net  Present  Value  (NPV)  between  baseline  scenario  and  project  scenario, 
which showed  that  oil  palm  plantation is the  most  profitable  land  use  compared to the project  scenario 
(Appendix 4:  NPV  Calculation). The barriers  to  the alternative scenarios and  the  project  scenario are 
evaluated in the following section. As established in this section, the most likely and credible business-as-
usual scenario is the conversion to oil palm plantation. Oil palm plantation becomes the baseline scenario 
for the project.

3.1.5 Additionality 
Step 3.  Barrier analysis 
Sub-step 3a.  Identification of barriers that would prevent the implementation of the type of proposed 
project activity 
 
Land Use Scenario Barriers 
Conservation project 
without being 
registered as a VCS 
AFOLU project 
There are investment barriers that would prevent the implementation of the 
project if it were not registered as a VCS AFOLU project. In the absence of 
carbon finance revenues, there would not be any revenues from this project 
undertaken by a private entity. As shown in Appendix 4: NPV Calculation, the 
only revenues of the PLUM project are from the sale of VCUs. Without the 
registration as a VCS AFOLU project, alternative sources of financing would be 
needed such as philanthropic funding for a conservation project. These 
sources of financing are not widely available for the scale and duration of the 
PLUM Project. 
 
Sub-step 3b.  Demonstration that the identified barriers would not prevent the implementation of at least 
one of the alternative land use scenarios 
The investment barrier identified under Sub-step 3a above does not prevent the implementation of the four 
commercial alternative land  use scenarios  below,  given  that  these  projects  have  revenues from  the 
commercialization  of  commodities. The  same  barrier  would,  however,  prevent  the  implementation  of the 
Unprotected Forest and Protected Forest scenarios. A summary of other barriers faced by the alternative 
land use scenarios is provided below.  
 
Land Use Scenario Barriers 
Industrial oil palm 
plantation 
This land use scenario does not face any significant barriers. When the project 
was initiated in 2021, the area was legally permissible for plantation 
establishment, and PT PUM (which was formerly part of PT AIS) had been 
granted a permit for the same purpose. After the acquisition of PT PUM by 
Hutan Kencana Group in 2021, PT AIS group still has two oil palm plantations 
adjacent to the project area, which have already started the process of 
conversion of the peat forest into oil palm plantation. 
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
This land use scenario faces barrier in transporting logs to a processing 
factory. The nearest factory is situated in South Kalimantan Province
56
 (> 
250km from the project area) and is only accessible by sea since there is no 
land routes available. With the high transport costs and the relatively small 
area size, it may not be financially feasible to develop an industrial acacia 
plantation in the area. There is also an institutional barrier for the 
establishment of industrial acacia plantation since the area has already 
obtained a government permit (from the Regent) for oil palm plantation 
development since 2011 and has been in the process of land status 
 
56
 PT Hutan Rindang Banua (plywood factory) 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
102 
Land Use Scenario Barriers 
conversion from forest estate to non-forest estate. In addition, there are no 
existing acacia plantation concessions in the vicinity. The majority of acacia 
plantation concessions in Central Kalimantan Province are concentrated in the 
northern part of the province, which indicates that acacia plantation 
establishment is not a common practice in the area. 
Logging concession This scenario faces ecological and economic barriers. Logging activities took 
place in the project area since the early 1970s, which resulted in a reduction in 
commercially viable timber. Also, timber prices have decreased while costs 
and requirements for certification have increased. 
Smallholder 
agriculture/farming 
 
Barriers to the expansion of smallholder agriculture in the project area are both 
ecological and institutional. Peat soils are generally less suitable for 
agriculture, and smallholder agriculture is not legally permissible on production 
forest land. 
Unprotected Forest – 
Continuation of pre-
project land use 
There are economic barriers to maintaining the area as unprotected forest, 
since the project area would not generate any state revenues nor benefits to 
local communities. 
Protected Forest Political and economic barriers exist for this scenario, since as a protected 
forest the project area would not generate any state revenues nor benefits to 
local communities and local government. 
 
Based on the analysis above, oil palm plantation is the most credible land use scenario in the project area 
due to its financial, legal and technical viability and the significant barriers faced by the other options.

the project areas

103 
Step 4.  Common practice analysis 
In  the  absence  of  carbon  finance  revenues,  there  are  few  instances  of  this  practice  in  Indonesia  where 
intact forest on production forest land is protected. It is not common practice in the country to maintain intact 
forests  on  production forest  land. Projects  aiming  to  protect  these  forests  would  rely  heavily  on  donor  or 
corporate funding sources, which are often scarce. Other similar conservation and restoration projects in 
Central Kalimantan are either implemented by non-profit organizations and reliant on philanthropic funding 
or are VCS AFOLU projects that rely on carbon finance. 
Conclusion 
The  project  is  considered  additional, and the  most  likely  and credible business-as-usual  scenario is  the 
conversion  to  oil  palm  plantation. By  preventing  this  conversion,  the  project  significantly  contributes  to 
carbon  sequestration  and  biodiversity  preservation,  marking  a  vital  step  away  from  business-as-usual 
towards environmental sustainability. 
 
Additional background on PT PUM’s transformation to ecosystem restoration project 
In 2021, PT PUM separated from PT AIS group and transformed its business from oil palm plantation 
into Forest Utilization Business License (PBPH). In 2021, Dr. Handoko Limaho’s family, a shareholder 
of PT AIS, decided to exit the palm oil business and instead develop ecosystem restoration projects. The 
family had been in the palm oil business since 2009 and became increasingly aware of the urgency of 
the climate and biodiversity crisis and the importance of conservation. The decision was made in 2021 
to divest from PT AIS and to acquire PT PUM from PT AIS to stop the planned deforestation of this high-
biodiversity  area. The  family  established  Hutan  Kencana  Group  with  a  mission  to  support  ecosystem 
restoration efforts, protect biodiversity, and improve the wellbeing of communities around forests. Without 
the prospect of carbon revenues, the decision to convert PT PUM into an ecosystem restoration project 
would not have been viable. 
 
Additionality of tidal wetland restoration and conservation project activities 
The  project  also  applied VCS  module VMD0052 to  demonstrate additionality  of  tidal wetland  restoration 
and conservation project activities (ADD-AM). 
Step 1: Regulatory surplus 
The project  activities related  to  tidal  wetland  restoration  and  conservation are not  mandated  by  any  law, 
statute, or other regulatory framework of Indonesia. The western part of the project area (where the tidal 
wetland  activities  take  place) is  on Production  Forest (Hutan Produksi) land  which mandates  production 
rather than conservation use. As shown in Map 3-3 above, there are 43 oil palm plantation permits granted 
on lands with Production Forest status within 100 km radius of PT PUM. 
Step 2: Positive list 
As explained in Section 3.1.2, the project meets all applicability conditions of Section 4.5 of the VM0007 
REDD+  Methodology  Framework,  and  in  so doing,  it  is  deemed  as  complying  with  the  positive  list 
established for VMD0052. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
104 
3.1.6 Methodology Deviations 
The  project  does  not  involve  deviations  from  the VM0007 methodology or  any  of  the related  modules 
relating to the criteria and procedures for monitoring or measurement. 
 
3.2 Quantification of GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions 
3.2.1.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
The project  has  used  VM0007  (REDD+  MF),  VMD0006  (BL-PL), VMD0042  (BL-PEAT),  VMD0050  (BL-
TW), and VMD0041 (BL-ARR) to assess the project’s baseline emissions. 
VMD0006 (BL-PL) 
Part 1: Calculating annual area of land deforested 
Under the VMD0006 (BL-PL), four parts of procedures with several steps were analysed. As explained in 
Sections 3.1.4 and 3.1.5, the deforestation agent is the oil palm company group PT AIS, the former parent 
company of PT PUM, who received the permit to convert the land into oil palm plantation in 2011 (Katingan 
Regent Decree  No.525/229/KPTS/  VIII/2011). However,  in  order  to  determine  a  more realistic  and 
conservative  deforestation  rate compared  to  the  plan indicated  in  PT  PUM’s  Environmental  Impact 
Assessment Report (AMDAL), the project conducted a deforestation rate analysis in proxy areas following 
the criteria for applicability of proxy areas laid out in VMD0006. 
The project identified six companies for proxy areas deforested by the same ‘class of deforestation agent’ 
at  least  ten years  previously. There  is  an  immediate  site-specific  threat  of  deforestation  (refer  to Section 
2.2) at a rate that was established by examining the proxy areas. VMD0006 BL-PL guides the reference or 
proxy area selection process. A sufficient number of parcels are required to be reflective of usual practice 
in  both  the  proxy  area  and  the  project  region  since  the  proxy  area  will  be  used  to  estimate  the  average 
proportion  of  land  that  is  cleared  each  year.  Below  are  the  overall  project  area  criteria  and  the  list  of 
reference region requirements. All of the criteria have been fulfilled.

Moreover, VM0007 REDD+ MF Version 1.6 also describes the historical reference period criteria for each 
activity.  The  historical  reference  period  is  the  temporal  domain  from  which  information  on  historical 
deforestation is extracted, analyzed and projected into the future. For REDD activities (Non-Wetland Area)

the starting date of the historical reference period must be between 9 and 12 years in the past and the end

date of the historical reference must be at least 10 years in the past.

Part 2: Baseline carbon stock change 
A  total  of  36  sampling  plots  (0.25  ha  each)  and  97  sampling  plots  were  sampled  for  the  above  ground 
biomass and below ground biomass respectively
58
. The number of sampling plots were deemed sufficient 
to  fulfil  10%  error  and  95%  confidence  interval.  From  the  sampling  plots, it  was  determined  that around 
1,822,430.64  tC  is  stored  in  the  above  ground  biomass,  around  20,302,995.97  tC  in  the  peatland  soil, 
around 1,002,137.80 tC in the tidal wetlands soil, and around 117,562.38 tC in the below ground biomass 
(mangrove roots) of the tidal wetlands, totalling 23,245,126.79 tC of carbon stock within the project area.

Part 3: Greenhouse gas emissions 
The GHG emissions in the baseline within the project boundary were estimated as Equation 15 below. 
1.
 
According  to  VMD0006,  the  N
2
Odirect-N,i,t  must  be  calculated  using  the  latest  AA/R  CDM  tool 
“Estimation  of  direct  nitrous  oxide  emission  from  nitrogen  fertilization” (Module E-NA).  However,  the 
tool is not applicable when A/R CDM project activities are implemented on wetlands which is the case 
in this project. Hence, the GHG emissions from fertilizer application is excluded in the baseline. 
2. According to VMD0006, the eBiomassBurn,i,t must be calculated using Module E-BPB. Where used in 
the baseline, accounting must occur under both the baseline and project scenarios in both the project 
area and in the leakage belt. As this project assumes that the fire prevention program is successfully 
implemented by the project, it is assumed that no fires will occur after the project initiation in the project 
scenario. Hence, the  GHG  emissions from biomass burning  (not  the peat burn) in  the baseline were 
conservatively omitted. 
3. According to VMD0006, the EFC,I,t must be calculated using Module E-FFC. All fossil fuel combustion 
associated with a project may be accounted, including fossil fuel combustion of subcontractors that are 
conducting (parts of the)  work  to  implement  the  project.  Fossil  fuel  combustion  in  all situations  is  an 
optional emission source. This project assumes that the use of fossil fuel will be de minimis under the 
project  scenario.  Hence,  the  GHG  emissions  from  combustion  of  fossil  fuels  in  the  baseline  were 
conservatively omitted.

VMD0042 (BL-PEAT) 
Step 5.2: Assessing GHG emissions in the baseline scenario of the CUPP Project Activity 
The VMD0042 (BL-PEAT) indicates  the steps  that  need  to be followed for  estimating  the  baseline 
emissions. The project area meets the VCS definition for peatland. The total eligible area for WRC Peat is 
21,009.91 ha (90.74% of the project area). The project area is drained and partially drained (please refer 
to the map of legacy canals in Map 3-5). 
 
Map 3-5. Legacy canals distribution

CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3

Map 3-6. Peat Depth Map 
The estimation of carbon loss and GHG emissions from peat fires is based on the mass of peat lost by fire 
and  emission  factors  from  IPCC.  The  project will implement  peatland  rewetting  and  best-practice  fire 
management as determined by relevant authorities (i.e. MoEF, BRGM
59
). Hence, peat fires in the project 
scenario are assumed to be catastrophic (unavoidable) events. Under CP-AB, the belowground biomass is 
already included as peat component and is not used separately including for shallow peat (where the entire 
peat layer is above the water table depth-as defined in VMD0016 X-STR). 
GHG emissions in the baseline scenario are estimated using Equation 1 VMD0042 as follows:

Step 5.3: Assessing GHG emissions due to peat drainage (GHG
peatsoil,BSL,i,t
) 
GHG emissions from the peat soil per stratum as a result of drainage in the baseline scenario are estimated 
using Equation 6 VMD0042 as follows: 
 
 
 
In case of abandonment of pre-project land use in the baseline scenario, the baseline scenario must also 
consider  non-human  induced  rewetting  brought  about  by  collapsing  dikes  or  ditches  that  would  have 
naturally  closed  over  time,  and  progressive  subsidence,  leading  to  raising  relative  water  table  depths, 
increasingly thinner aerobic layers and reduced CO
2
 emission rates. However, prior to project initiation, the 
pre-project  canals  were  maintained  by  actors  to  have  access  to  the  remnant  peat  forest  inside  the 
concessions.  This  demonstrates that  non-human  induced  rewetting  will  not  happen.  Hence,  the  annual 
subsidence is assumed to result in a 1:1 proportional rise of the water table relative to the surface in the 
area between ditches. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
120

Step 5.5: Assessing GHG emissions due to peat fire (E
peatburn,BSL,I,t
) 
Procedures for quantification of GHG emissions from peat fires are provided in module E-BPB. The baseline 
scenario sets out the frequency of peat fires and the volume (or mass) of peat burnt. Procedures to assess 
the area and depth of peat burnt from field observations or remote sensing data are provided in module M-
PEAT. Module M-PEAT also provides a simplified fire reduction premium approach to acknowledge peat 
fire emissions based on historic burnt area alone. The project used the VMD0046 M-PEAT and VMD0013 
E-BPB to assess the GHG emission. 
The PLUM Project is a rewetting project with historical incidents of uncontrolled (unplanned) burning. The 
baseline fire frequency and impact are assessed using annual historic data in the project area (in case of 
rewetting projects) over a period of minimum 10 to maximum 15 years ending 2 years before the project 
start date. The map below shows fire hotspot from 2012 until 2022 within and around project areas (Map 
3-7).

VMD0050 (BL-TW) 
Step 5.3: Assessing soil GHG emissions in the baseline scenario 
Part of the PLUM Project is implemented on tidal wetland where the wetting and drying cycles of the tides 
are influencing the ecosystem. The project follows VMD0050 BL-TW module to provide procedures for the 
establishment of a WRC baseline scenario for tidal wetlands. Emissions in the baseline scenario of WRC 
project activities in tidal wetlands are attributed to carbon stock changes in biomass carbon pools and soil 
processes. The SOC data on tidal wetlands is provided in Appendix 6: SOC Tidal Wetland Calculation. 
The  project  also  used  the VMD0006 BL-PL, VMD0013 E-BPB,  and VMD0001 CP-AB  to  estimate  the 
emissions   for   REDD-CIW   project   as   guided   by   VMD0050. The   non-tree   biomass,   however,   is 
conservatively omitted. For ARR-RWE project activities, procedures for biomass and biomass burning are 
provided in Module BL-ARR (see the following sub-chapter). In this project, the fossil fuel emissions were 
conservatively omitted.

VMD0041 (BL-ARR) 
The  net  GHG  removals  under  the  ARR  baseline  scenario  are  estimated using  Equation  1  VMD0041 as 
follows: 
 
The  annual  planting  rate  is  established  to  be  equivalent  to  the  rate  of  deforestation  determined  through 
analyses in the reference areas. In the case of oil palm, it was assumed based on common practice that 
the plantation would follow a 25-year cycle, meaning it would be harvested and replanted every 25 years. 
For calculating oil palm (Elaeis guineensis Jacq) carbon sequestration per year, carbon sequestration was 
assessed from two carbon pools, namely aboveground biomass (AGB) and belowground biomass (BGB) 
using certain allometric equations. The AGB estimation utilizes the formula 0.0706 + 0.0976H
64
. Meanwhile, 
the BGB estimation employs the equation EXP(-1.0587 + 0.8836 * LN(AGB))
65
, referred to formula on the 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) document
66
. These calculations are applied within a 
planting plot of dimensions 9 meters by 9 meters. With a maximum height potential of 18 meters
67
.  
Based on satellite image data from the last 10 years in the PLUM project area, there has been a natural 
growth  of  0.13%  per  year,  transforming  shrubland  into  secondary  swamp  forest.  In  a  conservative 
approach, we are adopting the figure of 0.13% per year for the conversion of shrubland to secondary swamp 
forest, which equates to an estimated addition of approximately 17.37 hectares per year.

3.2.2 Project Emissions 
3.2.2.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
The project used VM0007 (REDD+ MF), VMD0015 (M-REDD), VMD0046 (M-PEAT), VMD0051 (M-TW), 
and VMD0045 (M-ARR) to assess the project scenario emissions. 
Calculations for the estimation of changes in carbon sinks and GHG emissions under the project scenario 
followed the same methodological guidelines presented in Section 3.2.1. CH
4
 and N
2
O emissions were not 
considered  as  they  were  negligible  according  to  the  results  of  the  “Tool  for  testing  significance  of  GHG 
emissions  in  A/R  CDM  project  activities” (T-SIG).  Both  gases  had  a  contribution  of  0.02%  to  the  GHG 
emissions of the project scenario. 
VMD0015 (M-REDD) 
An  efficiency  index  of  a 85%  decrease  in  deforestation (100%  decrease  starting  from  Year  10) was 
considered for the project scenario and applied to the historical annual deforestation rate from the project 
zone for the period of 2012 – 2022. Hence, the annual rate of deforestation under project scenario is 0.36% 
(Table 3-26) and the annual rate of deforestation under the project scenario is 19.67 ha/year (Table 3-27).

The calculation of carbon stock in the project stratum is estimated by using annual C increment of the strata 
at  project  area.  As  required  by  M-REDD,  the  areas  projected  to  experience  C  enhancement  from  forest 
growth  are  limited  to  those  that  would  be  deforested  in  the  baseline.  Carbon  stock  enhancement  is  not 
accounted for in areas not deforested in the baseline. The calculation of carbon stock in the project stratum 
is  estimated  by  using  annual  C increment of  peat  swamp  forest and  mangrove  forest. The  mean  annual 
increment of carbon stock in peat swamp forests was reported as 3.27 tC ha

. Additionally, the annual 
increment for mangrove forests was documented at 3.18 tC ha

VMD0046 (M-PEAT) 
Regarding the peatland rewetting aspect of the project scenario, the calculation only considers emissions 
from  legacy  canals.  The project scenario does  not include peat  drainage  and  peat  ditches  from  oil  palm 
canals. The assumption in the project scenario is that the opening of canals due to oil palm cultivation can 
be  avoided. Meanwhile,  peat  burning  is  assumed  to  be  zero  in  the  project  scenario, based  on  the 
assumption that  with  the  implementation  of  the  project, there  will  be  no  more  instances  of  fires. The

VMD0045 (M-ARR) 
Under the project scenario for ARR activities, the project will rehabilitate a total of 15,088 ha degraded land, 
composed of 424 ha of ARR in mangrove, 1,306 ha of ARR on peatland, and 13,358 ha of Assisted Natural 
Regeneration on peat (Map 3-9). The carbon stock Assisted Natural Regeneration (ANR) increment, which 
is 6.68 tCO
2
e/ha/yr, is estimated from the carbon stock changes between wet shrub to secondary swamp 
forest stratum and assumes the natural regeneration will take 30 years. Areas replanted with high diversity 
and  density  took  approximately  10  years  to  establish  a  tree  canopy  as  dense  as  that  found  in  mature 
remnant  rainforests.  In  comparison,  natural  regrowth  sites  typically  require  an  average  of  30-40  years  to 
achieve a similar level of canopy density. Areas subjected to reforestation activities can thus transition back 
into  a  forested  state  within  a  decade,  whereas  naturally  regenerating  areas  take  a  longer  period.
70
 With 
project interventions, the aim is for areas to undergo regrowth within 30 years, developing into a secondary 
forest.  
In  the  project  scenario,  the  selection  of  plant  species  for  planting  includes  endemics  such  as  Jelutung 
(Dyera costulata), Balangeran (Shorea balangeran), Ramin (Gonystilus bancanus), etc., specifically chosen 
for  planting  activities  on  peat  soil.  Meanwhile,  for  mangrove  areas,  the  selected  species are Rhizophora 
apiculata and Rhizophora  stylosa.  The  approach  in  calculating  the Afforestation,  Reforestation,  and  
Revegetation (ARR) involves using allometric equations as follows: 
• Shorea  Balangeran: Aboveground   Biomass   (AGB)   is   calculated   using   the   formula   0.11   * 
Diameter^2.62
71
,  and  Belowground  Biomass  (BGB)  is  determined  by  EXP(-1.0587  +  0.8836  * 
LN(AGB))
67
.
 The growth rate is 2 cm/year, with a maximum diameter of 30 cm. Planting spacing is 
5 meters x 5 meters, with a survival rate of 80% per year in the first 3 years.. 
•
 
Rhizophora apiculata:  Aboveground  Biomass  (AGB)  is  determined  by  the  equation  0.1709  * 
DBH^2.516
72
,  and  Belowground  Biomass  (BGB)  is calculated  using  EXP(-1.0587  +  0.8836  * 
LN(AGB))
67
. The growth rate is 0.52 cm/year, with planting spacing of 3 meters x 3 meters and a 
60% survival rate per year in the first 3 years. 
Hence,  the  estimated  GHG  removals  in  the  project  scenario  for  60  years  from  ARR  project  activities  are 
(33,190,608) tCO
2
e (Table 3-31).

3.2.3 Leakage 
Applicable  leakage  modules  were  determined  according  to the requirements set in  the  VM0007  REDD+ 
Methodology  Framework.  As  demonstrated  in Section 3.1.5,  the  baseline scenario is  determined  as 
planned deforestation and peatland drainage as a result of conversion to oil palm plantation. The project is 
therefore categorized as a combination of Avoiding Planned Deforestation (APD), Reforestation (ARR), in 
combination with Restoration of Wetlands Ecosystem (RWE) and Conservation of Intact Wetlands (CIW). 
As a result, potential sources of leakage emissions include: 
(a)
 
Displacement of planned deforestation activities, 
(b)
 
Displacement of pre-project agricultural activities on non-forest land, and 
(c)
 
Ecological leakage due to possible alterations of mean annual water table depth in adjacent areas. 
These potential sources are covered in the VCS Methodology VM0007 Modules LK-ASP, LK-ARR, and LK-
ECO respectively. The applicability of modules for the quantification of leakage emissions is shown in Table 
3-33. 
 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
149 
Table 3-33. Applicability of leakage modules. 
Module Applicability 
Estimation of emissions from activity 
shifting for avoiding planned deforestation 
and planned degradation (LK-ASP) 
Applicable. The project may cause activity shifting of 
avoided planned deforestation.  
Estimation of emissions from activity 
shifting for avoiding unplanned 
deforestation (LK-ASU) 
Not applicable. The project is not categorized as 
avoiding unplanned deforestation. 
Estimation of emissions from displacement 
of fuelwood extraction (LK-DFW) 
Not applicable. The project is not categorized as 
avoiding unsustainable fuelwood extraction. 
Estimation of emissions from displacement 
of pre-project agricultural activities (LK-
ARR) 
Not applicable. There are no pre-project agricultural 
activities in the ARR areas.
