effects (LK-ME) 
Not applicable. The project does not reduce the 
production of timber, fuelwood, or charcoal.  
Estimation of emissions from ecological 
leakage (LK-ECO) 
Applicable. The project is categorized as WRC and may 
cause ecological leakage.  
 
 
3.2.3.1 Estimation of emissions from activity shifting for avoiding planned deforestation and planned 
degradation (LK-ASP) 
This module is applicable for estimating the leakage emissions due to activity shifting from forest lands that 
are  legally  authorized  and  documented  to  be  converted  to  non-forest  land,  including  activity  shifting  to 
forested  peatland  that  is  drained  as  a  consequence  of  project  implementation.  Under  these  situations, 
displacement  of  baseline  activities  can  be  controlled  and  measured  directly  by  monitoring  the  baseline 
deforestation agents or class of agents. 
In the context of the VMD0009 LK-ASP  module,  leakage  monitoring  is  refined  by  the  understanding  that 
leakage does not need to be considered where a specific deforestation agent has been identified and there 
is  verifiable  information  demonstrating  that  the  management  plans  and/or  land-use  designations  of  the 
deforestation agents’ other lands remain unchanged by the project activities. As described in Sections 2.2.1, 
2.2.2 and 3.1.5, the specific deforestation agent has been established as PT AIS who formerly owned PT 
PUM. After the acquisition of PT PUM by Hutan Kencana Group in 2021, PT AIS group still has two other 
oil palm plantation concessions (PT KMS and PT TBS) adjacent to PT PUM, which have already started 
the process of conversion of the peat forest into oil palm plantation. PT AIS did not change the management 
plan or land use of these other two concessions as a result of the PLUM Project, since these concessions 
had  already  been  granted  oil  palm  plantation  licenses  and  started the approval  and conversion process 
prior  to  the  project  start (Map 3-2). Additionally,  the  project  furnishes  robust  information  and  evidence  to 
the effect that PT AIS operates exclusively within a delineated area. Through the operations and activities 
of the PLUM Project, any threat of potential leakage is deemed effectively mitigated. 
Consequently,  this  document  proceeds  under  the  assumption  that  project-induced  leakage  is  unlikely. 
Nonetheless, vigilance against actual leakage remains a cornerstone of the project’s strategy, with ongoing 
monitoring throughout the crediting period and reporting at each monitoring event to ensure transparency 
and accountability. Hence, the project will be closely monitoring PT AIS and any of its subsidiaries for new 
developments  related  to  oil  palm  plantations  within  the  same  bioregional  landscape  of  Kalimantan.  This 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
150 
approach underscores a proactive stance against the risk of leakage, ensuring that any shifts in land use 
or management by PT AIS or its subsidiaries, indicative of moving deforestation activities to new areas as 
a response to the project, are monitored and identified. 
Adding to this framework, it is imperative to monitor all activities that result in landcover changes within the 
project zone, irrespective of the deforestation agent. Therefore, the project will monitor and report any new 
oil palm development in the project zone, irrespective of the deforestation agent, at each monitoring event. 
By integrating these measures, the project ensures a comprehensive surveillance over the project’s direct 
and indirect impacts on deforestation rates, to identify any displacement of such activities to other forested 
areas  under  the  management  or  influence  of  PT  AIS  within  Kalimantan,  as  well  as  any  displacement  of 
such activities within the project zone by any other deforestation agent. 
3.2.3.2 Estimation of emissions from displacement of pre-project agricultural activities (LK-ARR) 
No agricultural activities currently take place in the areas where the project will implement ARR and Assisted 
Natural Regeneration (ANR). Therefore, this module is not applicable. 
3.2.3.3 Estimation of emissions from ecological leakage (LK-ECO) 
Ecological  leakage  under  the VMD0044  module  occurs  when  the  hydrological  connectivity  of  the  project 
area with adjacent areas leads to the drainage of peatlands in the project area. The project was designed 
to  ensure  that  its  hydrological  connectivity  does  not  lead  to  a  significant  increase  in  greenhouse  gas 
emissions outside and in the project area.  
The PLUM project area consists of peatland and mangroves, and some of the baseline conditions of this 
project are:  
1. The construction of canals in the project area has occurred since several decades ago. The canals 
are used as a source of fisheries for villagers as they help villagers find fish. In the 2000s, these 
canals  changed  their  function  to  become  transportation  routes  for  illegal  timber  collection.  Since 
the  prohibition  of  illegal logging  by  the  Government  of  Indonesia,  these  canals  have  been 
abandoned  and  are  no  longer  used for  transportation  of  timber. However,  they  are  still  used  for 
fishing by some villagers. 
2.
 
The  Indonesian  government  has  built  irrigation  channels  for  rice fields  in  rice-producing  villages 
around the project area as part of the Strategic Irrigation Modernization and Urgent Rehabilitation 
Project  (SIMURP) project
73
. A 10-km  stretch  of the  irrigation  canal crosses the  boundary  of  PT 
PUM concession. The SIMURP irrigation canal area in PT PUM has been excluded from the project 
area. 
All upstream areas of the project are conservation areas, with Sebangau National Park located upstream 
of the eastern part and the PT Rimba Makmur Utama (PT RMU) concession and village forest upstream of 
the western part. Rice paddy areas are downstream of the project area. Hence, despite the hydrological 
connectivity  of  the  project  area  with  adjacent  areas,  it  is  unlikely  that  these  would  lead  to  drainage  of 
 
73
 The SIMURP project was implemented through collaboration of four Ministries/Institutions (K/L), namely the Ministry 
of National Development Planning of the Republic of Indonesia (Bappenas), Ministry of Public Works and Housing 
(PUPR), Ministry of Home Affairs (Kemendagri), and Ministry of Agriculture (Kementan). It was funded with loans from 
the World Bank and Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
151 
peatlands in the project area. Furthermore, the project scenario is expected to prevent ecological leakage, 
with  the  canal  blocking  and  revegetation  activities  designed  to  restore  the  peat  ecosystem  in  the  project 
area to prevent leakage from occurring. The PLUM Project meets the requirement of the VMD0044 module, 
hence the project assumes that the emission from ecological leakage is zero. 
3.2.4 Net GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.4.1 Estimation of the GHG emission reductions and removals 
Uncertainty 
Uncertainties  were  calculated  for  both  REDD  and  WRC  components  following  the  X-UNC  module 
(VMD0017 v2.2). The detailed uncertainty calculation is available in Appendix 11: Uncertainty Analysis. 
REDD: Since  the  baseline  deforestation  rate  was  derived  from  measurements  of  proxy  areas,  the 
uncertainty  must be  equal  to  the  95%  confidence  interval  as  a  percentage  of  the  mean  of  the  area 
deforested in each proxy (D%
pn
) divided by the number of years over which deforestation occurred in each 
proxy  (Yrs
pn
).  The  uncertainty  of  baseline  deforestation  rate was calculated to  be 0.0178% or  can  be 
assumed zero. The total uncertainty of combined carbon stocks and greenhouse gas sources in the REDD 
baseline was calculated to be 5.48%. Therefore, the cumulative uncertainty in the REDD baseline scenario 
is 5.48%. No ex-post (re-)measurements of carbon pools or GHG sources have been made at this stage, 
therefore the uncertainty in the REDD project scenario is set equal to zero as per the X-UNC module. 
WRC: To  calculate  the  uncertainties  in  WRC-Peat emissions  in  the  baseline  and  project  scenario, the 
project used the standard errors of default factors provided by the IPCC (IPCC Wetlands Supplement 2013). 
The uncertainty of CH
4
 emissions was set to zero since CH
4
 emissions are conservatively omitted in both 
the baseline and project scenarios calculation. The uncertainty of uncontrolled peat burning in the project 
scenario was  also set to zero  since it is assumed that no  fires  will  occur  after  the  project  initiation. The 
cumulative uncertainty of WRC-Peat in the baseline and project scenarios were calculated to be 6.63% and 
13.25%  respectively. For  the WRC-TW,  the project used  field-collected data (soil  bulk  density) when 
estimating the CO
2
 emissions. To calculate the uncertainty, standard errors from the field data was used. 
The cumulative uncertainty  of WRC-TW was calculated  to  be 0.66%  for  the  baseline  and  0.64%  for  the 
project  scenario.  The  total  cumulative  of  all  WRC emissions  (peat  and  tidal  wetlands)  was 6.67%  for 
baseline and 13.27% for project scenario. 
Total  error  in  the  REDD+  project  activity is  equal  to  the  combined  uncertainty  in  baseline  and  project 
estimates  for  the  REDD  and  WRC  activities  and  was  calculated  to  be 5.51%. The value  is below  the 
allowable threshold of 15% as stated in the VM0007 REDD+MF methodology, therefore no VCU deduction 
is necessary.

3.2.4.2 Non-Permanence Risk Buffer Withholding 
The  annual  buffer  withheld  for  all  activities  under  the  VM0007  REDD+  Methodology  Framework  was 
calculated as a percentage of the total carbon stock benefits, excluding any emissions due to leakage. As 
the project does not account for emissions from fossil fuel combustion, direct N2O emissions and emissions 
from  biomass  burning  were  also  omitted  from the calculations.  The  project  used  the  AFOLU  Non-
Permanence Risk Report Excel Calculation Tool, v4.2 (Appendix 7: AFOLU Non-permanence Risk Report) 
where the overall risk rating of 17% was determined (Table 3-38). The total estimated buffer pool from the 
project is 14,337,337 tCO
2
e for 60 years.

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan 
Climate impacts will be monitored in accordance with Appendix 13: Monitoring Plan, which includes land 
cover change monitoring,  fire monitoring  (e.g., hotspots, burnt  areas),  peat  water  table  and  subsidence 
monitoring following the requirements under VCS VM0007 methodology. The project will use both remote 
sensing approach and field data measurement for monitoring climate impacts. The monitoring plan outlines 
the indicators to be monitored, monitoring frequency, and data collection method. 
3.3.3.1 Remote Sensing 
Remote sensing approach will be used to monitor land cover change and burnt areas (if any fire incidences 
occur  within  the  project  area)  and  will  be  conducted  annually.  Satellite  imageries  with  resolution  of ≤ 30 
meters (e.g., PlanetLab, Sentinel-1, Sentinel-2, ALOS/PALSAR, etc.) with cloud cover <10% will be used. 
Land cover change analysis will follow the procedures set out in Appendix 1: Remote Sensing Approach 
and Land Cover Classification Reports. Any deforested areas within the project area and project zone will 
be  groundtruthed  by  the  field  team  by  directly  visiting  the  location  and/or  using  drones  if  the  location  is 
inaccessible. Overall classification accuracy is targeted at >90%. 
Leakage  monitoring  in  this  project  is undertaken to address  the  risk  that deforestation  activities are 
displaced  elsewhere. By  focusing on  a  specific identified actor, namely PT  AIS,  the  project  applies  a 
targeted approach  that draws  on historical information and  present  circumstances  and  practices to 
demonstrate low potential for project-induced leakage. 
Additionally, a robust and diversified monitoring strategy is implemented by the project as a commitment to 
transparency and environmental integrity, as follows: 
• Remote Sensing: Satellite imagery tracking land cover changes across the project area and project 
zone to provide a general view of possible changes in land use patterns indicative of leakage. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
179 
• GLAD Alerts: The incorporation of the Global Land Analysis & Discovery system to provide near-
real-time deforestation alert data, a tool that helps in rapid identification of unexpected changes to 
forest cover and enables prompt investigation and response. 
• Ground Truthing: Regular field visits shall be undertaken to verify remote sensing and alert data to 
ensure accuracy for detection and attribution of leakage to actual activities or developments. 
These sources of information are gathered and analyzed each time a new verification event occurs, with a 
special focus on the production, changes in the water table, and new oil palm developments. This integrated 
approach not only meets the specifications of the VMD0009 LK-ASP module but also serves to further build 
the project’s capacity in monitoring any potential leakage and responding appropriately, hence delivering 
on its commitment to reducing deforestation and the related climate impact. 
The project’s approach  to  monitoring  tidal  wetlands adopts  a  comprehensive  strategy  to  effectively  curb 
GHG emissions. By combining direct measurements of GHG flux through both chamber methods with water 
quality evaluations for nutrients and dissolved carbon, this method offers a robust analysis of environmental 
health.  The  use  of  remote  sensing  technology, such  as satellite  imagery  and  drone  surveys, provides 
detailed insights into the dynamics of the wetland, including changes in vegetation and shoreline changes. 
Ground  evaluations  further  contribute  by  assessing  the  vegetation  and  soil’s  carbon  storage  potential. 
Monitoring the hydrological aspects, such as water table levels and the impact of tides, is fundamental for 
understanding how water movements influence emission trends. This integrated system ensures that the 
project can enhance the wetland’s capacity for carbon capture while keeping GHG releases to a minimum. 
 
3.3.3.2 Field Data Measurement 
3.3.3.2.1 AGB C Stock 
To monitor the C stock within the project area, the project will establish 20 permanent vegetation/biomass 
plots in the same locations that were used for the baseline assessment. The plots will be monitored annually 
for tree growth (DBH). To estimate the C stock from DBH, we will use the same allometric equations that 
were used when determining the baseline AGB C stock
74
. 
For ARR activities, the project will establish new plots that will represent the areas of ARR (including ANR) 
activities for  both  mangrove  and  peat  ARR. DBH will  be  the  main  parameter  measured  in  the  field  and 
species specific allometric equations will be used to estimate the C stock (in accordance with the species 
that  will  be  planted).  Field  measurement  will  be  conducted  annually. In  addition,  the  project  will create a 
logbook that records the number of seedlings planted by species, survival rate, DBH, and height for active 
planting activities. 
 
74
 PT PUM’s Biomass Carbon Stock Assessment Report. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
180 
 
Map 3-10. Planned location of permanent vegetation plots 
3.3.3.2.2 Fire Hotspots and Burnt Areas 
The project will develop a Fire Danger Rating System (FDRS) to serve as an early warning system of fire 
risk in the project area. Automatic Weather Stations (AWS) will be installed on the ground to collect climate 
data  that  correlate  with  fire  risk (e.g.,  temperature,  precipitation,  soil  moisture,  and  peat  water  level)  and 
these data will be supplied into the FDRS to calculate the Peat Fire Vulnerability Index (PFVI). The FDRS 
will then provide a 7-day forecast of fire risk in the project area. The FDRS will categorize the fire risk based 
on PFVI’s value as Safe / Alert / Dangerous / Extreme. 
In  the  field,  the  project will monitor  fire  hotspots through  daily  monitoring  from  fire  watch  tower and  fire 
prevention patrols during the dry season when the FDRS rates the fire risk as Alert / Dangerous / Extreme. 
In addition, the project also monitors fire hotspots from NASA’s Fire Information for Resource Management 
System (FIRMS). FIRMS provides near real-time active fire data within three hours of satellite observation 
from  the  MODIS  and  VIIRS  instruments. The  fire  patrol  team  will  verify  fire  hotspots  on  the  ground by 
directly visiting the location and/or using drone if the location is inaccessible. 
Burnt  areas  within  the  project  area  will  be mapped  and  calculated using  remote  sensing  approach  with 
high-resolution satellite  imagery within  one  month  of  fire  incidences with  detailed  procedure  listed in the 
Standard  Operational  Procedure document for Prevention  and Management  of Forest  and  Land  Fire 
(please refer to Appendix 12: List of SOPs). Areas with repeated burning will be analyzed using historical 
burning records. GHG emissions from biomass and peat burning will be calculated annually following the 
E-BPB module. 
 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
181 
3.3.3.2.3 Peat Water Table and Subsidence 
The  project plans  to install 352 dipwells  and 13 subsidence  poles along  the  planned  canal  blocks in  the 
project area to monitor peat water level and peat subsidence. However, the numbers of monitoring stations 
may be adjusted depending on field conditions. Peat water level will be measured automatically (daily) or 
manually (weekly), while peat subsidence will be monitored manually every three months. 
The  project  implements  a  detailed  approach  to  monitor  peat  water  levels  and  subsidence,  crucial  for 
evaluating its impact on peatland conservation and carbon sequestration. Through both automatic (daily) 
and manual (weekly) measurements of water levels, as well as quarterly manual checks of peat subsidence, 
it captures essential data on hydrology and land stability. This is followed by data analysis on peat water 
levels and subsidence to provide further understanding of this influential factor on greenhouse gas (GHG) 
emissions.  Water-level  fluctuations  in  peat,  in  fact,  determine  the  rate  of  decomposition.  Generally, low 
water levels imply more decomposition, hence higher emissions of GHGs. Equally, peat subsidence data 
provide  information  on  the long-term  stability  in  the  peat  and  also  the  storage  of  carbon  in  peatland. 
Summed  with  other  data  points,  the  project  can  then  very  accurately  infer  GHG  emissions  from  peat 
decomposition in tropical peatlands. 
 
3.3.3.2.4 GHG Emissions 
The  project  is  exploring  future  installation  of  greenhouse  gas  (GHG)  monitoring  equipment  aimed  at 
providing long-term data on atmospheric GHG flux in peatlands, including carbon dioxide (CO
2
), methane 
(CH
4
),   among   others.   Atmospheric   monitoring   of   GHG   flux enhances   the understanding   on   the 
sequestration of carbon from the atmosphere by vegetation and its subsequent integration into the soils. 
The  project  may  leverage atmospheric-based  monitoring,  potentially  employing  GHG  flux  towers,  in 
collaboration with Hyphen Global AG to further these objectives.
75
 
 
 
75
 Hyphen Global AG is a Switzerland-based climate tech company pioneering science driven digital MRV by way of 
precision emissions validation through real-time atmospheric greenhouse gas (GHG) monitoring. Atmospheric-based 
methodologies for monitoring these processes receive international endorsement from authoritative bodies such as the 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change 
(UNFCCC), and the World Meteorological Organization (WMO). These endorsements underscore the peer-reviewed 
accuracy of measured sequestration data obtained through atmospheric monitoring.

The procedures  for data  management, internal  auditing  and  QA/QC are  described  in Figure 3-7 below.

The project will also adopt the Spatial Monitoring and Reporting Tool (SMART) Patrol System
76
 to digitize 
the process of field data collection (including data for Community and Biodiversity) and therefore minimizing 
the  risk  of  human  error  in  manual  data  entry.  The  SMART  Patrol  Mobile  application  will  be  installed on 
tablets or smartphones that will be used by the field staff for data collection. The SMART Patrol Desktop 
application will serve as the main database of all field data. 
To avoid non-conformances with the monitoring plan, the project has developed and will develop SOPs at 
each level (e.g., field data collection, data management, data analysis, etc.). A list of available SOPs and 
planned SOPs  that  will  be  developed  is  available in Appendix 12: List  of  SOPs. A  copy  of  SOPs  can  be 
provided to the validator upon request. All project staff will be trained to follow procedures in accordance 
with the  SOPs and will continuously be supervised. The  project  will  also  implement  multiple  QA/QC  at 
various levels. All of the data collected, entered, uploaded, and analyzed are subject to review and approval 
by the Coordinator or Manager or Expert or Director (depending on the level) to ensure that the SOPs are 
correctly followed (Figure 3-7). In addition, the project also adopts adaptive management principle (please 
refer to Section 2.3.8) and will regularly evaluate the implementation of monitoring procedures and make 
improvements when required.

3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (CL4.2) 
There are three types of stakeholders involved in the project, namely government, local communities, and 
business entities. For all stakeholders, the summary of project documents, including monitoring plan and 
results, will be shared and given access to through regular coordination, printed documents at the project 
offices  and  village  information  boards,  and  online  resources  through  Verra  and  the  project  website 
(www.plumproject.id). 
Specifically  for  communities,  the  project  documents  will  be  shared  through  village  meetings  and  village 
information boards. Regular communication will occur during the project lifetime, as the project will closely 
involve the community in the project activities and monitoring. Formally, at least two village meetings will 
be conducted annually to disseminate project activities updates and reports. 
 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
185 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
The project area and adjacent communities in Central Kalimantan are vulnerable to climate change impacts. 
The  project  will  implement  strategies that help  local  communities,  in  particular  vulnerable  groups, and 
biodiversity  adapt  to  climate  change. Overall,  the project  interventions  are  expected  to  increase  the 
resilience of local communities and biodiversity to climate change. The project seeks to be validated to the 
Gold Level for climate change adaptation benefits. 
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (GL1.1) 
1) Climate change and climate variability scenarios and impacts 
Exposure 
Historical and Future Projections 
- Temperature 
Regionally,   Indonesia   has seen a   positive   trend   in   changes   in   minimum,   average   and   maximum 
temperatures.  Based  on Indonesian  Meteorological,  Climatological,  and  Geophysical  Agency  (BMKG) 
observation  data  historically  observed  from  1981  to  2018,  it  shows  a  trend  of  increasing  temperature  of 
0.03°C  every  year.
77
 Future  projections  in  the Indonesia  Climate  Change  Sectoral  Roadmap  (ICCSR) 
2010
78
 were obtained from GCM (global climate model) output analysis. The projected upward trend of the 
Indonesian region’s temperature in Kalimantan, following the global trend, is 1°C for 2020-2050 period and 
1.5°C - 3°C for 2070-2100 relative to the baseline period of 1961-1990.
79
 According to this analysis, there 
are higher probabilities for higher temperature increase in Kalimantan compared to the national average. 
Therefore, it is likely that the project zone will experience warming temperatures due to climate change in 
the order of an estimated 1°C for 2020-2050 and 1.5°C - 3°C for 2070-2100. 
- Precipitation 
Based on BMKG observation data from 1981 to 2018, in general in Indonesia rainfall has seen a positive 
trend at all levels of rain intensity. The trend equation has a slope value of 0.1149 (intensity of 20 mm/day) 
which means that rainy days with an intensity of 20 mm/day tend to increase by 0.1149 days every year, or 
the equivalent of 1.149 more rainy days every decade.
80
 The anticipated changes in rainfall patterns across 
Borneo  are  strongly  influenced  by  alterations  in  the  monsoon  seasons  and  the  region’s  geographical 
characteristics. Most parts of Borneo are expected to experience an overall increase in rainfall. Projections 
suggest that the Southwest monsoon rainfall will be more evenly distributed, while the Northeast monsoon 
 
77
 [BMKG] Meteorological, Climatological, and Geophysical Agency. temperature trend.

 https://perpustakaan.bappenas.go.id/e-library/file_upload/koleksi/migrasi-data-
publikasi/file/Policy_Paper/synthesis-roadmap__20110217190358__0.pdf 
79
 [ICCSR]  Indonesia  Climate  Change  Sectoral  Roadmap.  2010.  Scientific  Basis:  Analysis  and  Projection  of 
Temperature and Rainfall

will be shorter and more intense in the majority of regions.
81
 Thus, it is anticipated that the project zone will, 
overall, experience an increase in mean annual precipitation.  
- Extreme Weather Events (droughts, forest fires) 
Climate  change  can  alter  the  natural  patterns  and  processes  that  drive  climate  variability.  In  Central 
Kalimantan, climate variability is influenced by factors such as El Niño/La Niña events, which can lead to 
changes  in  precipitation  patterns.  Climate  change  can  exacerbate  these  natural  fluctuations,  leading  to 
more  extreme  weather  events  and  longer-lasting  impacts  on  the  region.
82
 Extreme  weather  events  that 
occur in Indonesia, especially in the Kalimantan region, relate to drought and forest fires. Historical extreme 
weather that has occurred (1997 and 2015) has been exacerbated by the very strong ENSO and positive 
IOD  phenomena  showing  an  increase  in  forest  fire  events  in  Kalimantan.
83
 Increase  in  drought  risk  is 
characterised by an anomalous increase in temperature reaching 0.4°C by September 2023
84
 considered 
as the highest anomalous temperature throughout the observation period since 1991, and occurring longer 
predicted until early 2024 which is influenced by the El Niño-Southern Oscillation (ENSO) phenomenon and 
exacerbated by the Indian Ocean Dipole (IOD) phenomenon.
85
 It is, therefore, expected that episodes of 
drought  caused  by  El  Niño  will  increase in  the  project  zone,  leading  to  an  increased  risk  of  fires  in  the 
degraded peat areas. 
- Sea Level Rise 
Previous research on the analysis of sea level rise in Indonesia used available altimetry satellites to analyse 
historical, and projected sea level rise in Indonesia. From 1992 to 2022, the Indonesian region experienced 
an average sea level rise of 4.6 +- 0.4 mm/year.

(i.e., not in the area where mangroves will be planted/restored by the project).

- Location specific differences 
While detailed downscaled information is not available for each of the project villages, it is understood that 
there will be some differences in climate change scenarios for each village. For examples, information from 
the Climate  Information  Portal
89
 suggests  that in Pulau  Hanaut Sub-district (western  part  of  the  project 
zone) annual   mean   temperature will   not increase   for   the   period 2011-2040 under Representative 
Concentration Pathway (RCP) 4.5 scenario. Annual mean precipitation would decrease by 2%. In turn, for 
Pagatan in Katingan Kuala Sub-district (eastern part), Climate Information Portal data suggests that annual 
mean temperature would increase by 1% and annual mean precipitation by 2% in the same period, while 
actual aridity
90
 would also increase.

In  conclusion, overall, the  project  zone  is  expected  to  experience  rising  temperatures,  increased  rainfall, 
and  greater  variability  in  rainfall  patterns  due  to  changes  in  the  monsoon  seasons  and  geography. 
Additionally, the region is likely to face further sea level rise and an increase in extreme weather events, 
particularly droughts leading to an increased risk of fires. 
2)
 
Potential changes in the local land use scenario due to climate change 
Climate change can affect the local land use scenario in the project area and adjacent local communities 
in  various  ways.  The  expansion  of  oil  palm  plantations  in  the  region  is  one  of  the  major  drivers  of 
deforestation  and  land use  change,  resulting  in  the  loss  of  forest  cover,  biodiversity,  and  ecosystem 
services. Climate change can affect the productivity and yield of oil palm plantations, as well as other crops 
grown  in  the  region,  due  to  changes  in  temperature,  rainfall,  and  other  climatic  factors. Changes  in 
precipitation patterns, such as higher precipitation or floods, can cause stress and decrease the production 
and quality of crude palm oil (CPO).
93
 The impacts of climate change can also affect the livelihoods of local 
communities,  such  as  rice  farmers  and  fishermen,  who  depend  on  natural  resources  and  ecosystem 
services that are affected by climate change and land use change. Therefore, the impacts of climate change 
may, in the long term, lead to changes in land use such as adopting alternative crops or adding additional 
planting areas. However, in the short to medium term, it is unlikely that the land use scenarios would change 
because of the impacts of climate change. 
 
92
 SMHI Climate Information Portal. Available at: https://climateinformation.org (accessed Jan 21, 2024). 
93
 Paterson RRM, Lima N. Climate change affecting oil palm agronomy, and oil palm cultivation increasing climate 
change, require amelioration. Ecol Evol. 2017 Nov 30;8(1):452-461. https://doi.org/10.1002/ece3.3610

3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (GL1.2) 
The projected climate changes described above are likely to have an impact on both the conservation status 
of biodiversity in the project zone and surrounding regions and on the well-being of communities. 
Sensitivity 
- Ecosystems and biodiversity 
Habitation loss and fragmentation. Ecosystems and biodiversity in the project region are vulnerable to the 
impacts of climate change. PT PUM concession is part of the remaining lowland forest of Kalimantan which 
is a habitat for many threatened species such as orangutan, proboscis monkey and white-bearded gibbon. 
Forest loss and high levels of habitat fragmentation, exacerbated by the impacts of climate change, threaten 
the survival of  these  key  species.  A  study  has  shown  that the Bornean orangutan  lost  36% of its habitat 
between  1973  and  2015 due  to  deforestation  and  conversion  to oil  palm,  rubber, timber,  and  other 
plantations. Extensive deforestation has resulted in the loss of not only important forest habitat for species, 
but  also  the  availability  of  functionally  connected  habitats.
94
 Habitat loss  and  fragmentation impacts the 
connectivity  of  forest  species and  renders  them  more  vulnerable  to  climate  change  due  to  smaller 
population sizes, increased isolation, and reduced genetic diversity.
95
 
The  decline  in  ecosystem  functions and  connectivity compromises species’ adaptability  and  resilience  in 
future landscapes under climate change.
96
 In turn, the preservation of ecosystem functions and connectivity 
can contribute to the adaptability and resilience of threatened species, such as orangutans in Kalimantan. 
Ecosystem functions provide essential resources and services that support the survival and well-being of 
species.  By  preserving  these  functions,  species  are  better  able  to  adapt  to  changing  environmental 
conditions  and  maintain  their  populations.  Additionally,  connectivity  allows  species  to  move  and  interact 
with other populations, which can help them adapt to new conditions and maintain genetic diversity. Overall, 
the  preservation  of  ecosystem  functions  and  connectivity  is  an  important  component  of  conservation 
strategies for threatened species, as it can help them adapt to the impacts of climate change and maintain 
their populations. The eastern part of PT PUM concession area is adjacent to the larger forested area of 
Sebangau National Park to the north. Therefore, protecting and restoring PT PUM’s area will increase the 
connectivity of the forest fragments in the Sebangau landscape (see Figure 3-10). 
 
94
 Ocampo-Peñuela N., et al. (2020). Impacts of Four Decades of Forest Loss on Vertebrate Functional Habitat on 
Borneo. Front. For. Glob. Change 3:53. https://doi.org/10.3389/ffgc.2020.00053  
95
 Püttker T., et al. (2020). Indirect effects of habitat loss via habitat fragmentation: A cross-taxa analysis of forest-
dependent species, Biological Conservation, Volume 241, 2020. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.biocon.2019.108368  
96
 Ocampo-Peñuela N., et al. (2020).

Figure 3-10. Priority Attention Areas (PAA) for critical connectivity linkages between PT PUM concession 
area and Sebangau National Park.
97
 
Changes in phenology. Changes  in  precipitation  patterns  and  increasing  temperatures  are  expected  to 
impact the phenology and fruiting season in tropical forests. This is likely to lead to reduced fruit availability, 
affecting  orangutan  behavior  and  potentially  reducing  their  reproduction  rates  due  to  food  scarcity. 
Furthermore, this phenomenon will also impact other species that depend on tropical forest fruits for food.
98
 
Increased risk of fires threatening habitat and species survival. The increase in temperatures and periods 
of prolonged drought (e.g., due to El Niño) lead to an increased risk of fires in the peatlands of Kalimantan. 
A  study  on  peatland  fire  weather  conditions  in  Central  Kalimantan,  Indonesia,  found  that  high  air 
temperature is one of several fire weather conditions that contribute to the risk of fires
99
. Strong winds that 
result from the difference in sea and land temperatures further contribute to the spread of fires. For example, 
during El Niño in 2015, there was an increased record of hotspots in the project zone and project area of 
PT  PUM  compared  to  a  non-El  Niño  year. Additionally,  deforestation  and  climate  change  have  been 
identified  as  significant  factors  in  increasing  fire  risk  in  Borneo,  including  Kalimantan,  amid  a  warming 
 
97
 Borneo Nature Foundation (2023). Orangutan Population Survey: Baseline Survey of the Orangutan Population 
within the PT. Pagatan Usaha Makmur (PUM) Concession. 
98
 Numata,  S., et  al. Impacts  of  climate  change  on  reproductive  phenology  in  tropical  rainforests  of  Southeast 
Asia. Commun Biol 5, 311 (2022). https://doi.org/10.1038/s42003-022-03245-8  
99
 Usup,  A., et  al. Peatland  Fire  Weather  Conditions  in  Central  Kalimantan,  Indonesia. Fire 2023,6,182. 
https://doi.org/10.3390/fire6050182 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
192 
climate.
100
 The  vulnerability  of  deforested  tropical  peatlands  to  fire  emissions  is  exacerbated  by  the 
drainage and desiccation of the surface peat, which makes it highly susceptible to ignition and sustained 
underground  smoldering  combustion,  particularly  in  the  context  of  increased  air  temperature  and  drier 
microclimate  in  deforested  landscapes
101
. This  directly  threatens  the  habitat  of  threatened  species  in  the 
project area, including the three trigger species of the project, the Bornean orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus 
wurmbii),   Proboscis   monkey   (Nasalis  larvatus),   and   the   Bornean   white-bearded   gibbon   (Hylobates 
albibarbis). Thus, rewetting of peatlands and avoiding further drainage are important measures to mitigate 
the impacts of climate change on these species by preserving ecosystem integrity and resilience. 
Sea level rise. Sea level rise may pose a long-term threat to the mangrove habitat specifically in the western 
part of PUM concession area. Sea level rise is known to increase coastal erosion, salinity level, and cause 
inundation which can exceed the salinity tolerance level of mangroves.
102
 Although mangroves are known 
to have the ability to adapt with inundation through the accretion of sediments, a study on the vulnerability 
of Indo-Pacific mangrove  forests  to  SLR  shows  that the  rate  of  SLR  exceeds  the  rate  of  soil surface 
elevation gain in 69% of the study areas.
103
 In addition, coastal abrasion may also be exacerbated by SLR. 
The historical annual average rate of coastal abrasion in the western part of PUM concession was 10.64 
meters inland. The  potential  negative  impact  of SLR on coastal habitat will  likely  affect  the  species that 
inhabit the  areas,  such  as  the  endangered  proboscis  monkey and the far  eastern  curlew  (Numenius 
madagascariensis), a migratory bird that was found to forage on the mudflat areas of the western part of 
PUM concession area. Therefore, protecting the remaining mangrove habitat and restoring the degraded 
areas are critical for the long-term integrity of the habitat and the survival of the species that depend on it. 
- Local communities and livelihoods 
Communities  in  the  project  zone,  including  women  and  vulnerable  groups,  rely  on  natural  resources 
including water for agriculture, non-timber forest products, etc. PT PUM’s project zone, which is dominated 
by rice farming, will suffer from prolonged droughts due to El Niño and high precipitation levels due to La 
Niña. Crop failure will also potentially increase which leads to increasing levels of poverty in communities 
who live in the project zone. 
Based  on  Indonesia  Statistic  Bureau  (BPS  2020), the  main  livelihoods  of  the  communities  in  the  project 
zone  include  rice  farmers  (69.1%),  vegetable  farmers  (12.1%),  fishermen  (10.1%),  fish  farmers  (3.5%), 
loggers (1.8%), and NTFPs collectors (3.3%) (Yayasan Puter, 2022). These communities relying on farming 
and  fishing  livelihoods  are  considered  more  vulnerable  to  climate  change  and  are  more  likely  to  live  in 
poverty. Six villages in the project  zone are categorized  as  underdeveloped  villages  and over 50%  of 
villagers in the project zone are below the national poverty line of IDR 2,120,371/month per household
104
. 
 
100
 Taraka  D.-B., et  al. (2023).  Future  fire  risk  under  climate  change  and  deforestation  scenarios  in  tropical 
Borneo. Environ. Res. Lett. 18 024015. https://doi.org/10.1088/1748-9326/acb225  
101
 Nikonovas,  T., et  al. Near-complete  loss  of  fire-resistant  primary  tropical  forest  cover  in  Sumatra  and

Farmers’ average income in Katingan Kuala Sub-district is around IDR 1,800,000/month

. Rice farmers 
income in the project villages is around IDR 446,854/month (Yayasan Puter, 2022). 
Agriculture is considered a sector that is vulnerable to climate change. The sector is vulnerable to increases 
in  temperature,  increased  variability  in  rainfall,  and  periods  of  drought.  It  is  often  the  poor  who  are  most 
vulnerable to climate change given a lack of diversification and lack of access to financial resources that 
would help them recover from shocks and stresses. Climate change impacts can have a disproportionate 
effect on women, as they often bear the brunt of the consequences of climate change, such as increased 
workloads, food insecurity, and reduced access to resources.
106,107 
Climate change is likely to affect rice growing areas in Central Kalimantan through long-term changes in 
sea level rise, alterations in rainfall patterns, and increases in temperature. Rice production is vulnerable 
to changes in the onset and duration of the wet season, with delays in monsoon and increased floods and 
droughts  impacting  production.  Climate  change  can  lead  to  a  decline  in  rice  yield  due  to  temperature 
increments and changes in rainfall patterns, potentially increasing the risk of food insecurity.
108
 
Furthermore, coastal communities are threatened by abrasion, inundation, and high waves driven by sea 
level  rise,  which  can  lead  to  flooding. High waves can  threaten small  boats  and  decrease  production of 
local capture fisheries.
109
 Additionally, long-term changes in sea levels, alterations in rainfall patterns, and 
increases in temperature can affect inland fisheries, potentially leading to changes in water quality and fish 
habitats, ultimately impacting fish production.
110
 
Adaptive Capacity 
Overall, adaptive capacity of local communities in the project zone is still limited. Factors contributing to the 
adaptive capacity of the communities are summarized below. 
- National and sub-national climate change policies and strategies 
Since  2011,  Indonesia  has  set  up  several  measures  for  mitigating  the  impacts  of  climate  change  at  the 
national  level. The  country  has  integrated  climate  change  adaptation  into  its  development  planning,  as 
evidenced by the National Adaptation Plan process and the incorporation of CCA into the fourth Medium 
Term  Development  Plan  (RPJMN)  from  2020  to  2024. At  the  provincial  level,  the  Governor  of  Central 
Kalimantan has issued several regulations regarding climate change adaptation and mitigation.

194 
- Community-level adaptation plans 
At the community level, the GoI promotes a national approach to mitigate climate change namely Kampung 
Iklim. The objectives of this approach are to promote mitigation and adaptation to climate change by the 
community at the village level, increase the capacity and participation of the community to mitigate climate 
change;  and  to  support  national  programs  on  climate  change  adaptation  and  mitigation.  However,  this 
approach  is  still  limited  in  terms  of  participation  since  not  all  the  villages  in  Indonesia  participate  in  this 
program. The villages in the project zone currently do not participate in the program. 
- Access to weather/climate information/early warning 
BMKG has established a system for weather forecast, early warning, and fire monitoring
111
. However, this 
system is not yet widely disseminated to the grassroots level. The information is not yet commonly used for 
agronomy decision making such as about planting start date and pest management. 
- Farmer and community organization 
Agriculture in Katingan Kuala Sub-district is one of the biggest economic sectors that provides the largest 
livelihood opportunity for around 65.2% of the population
112
. There are several existing farmer groups and 
farmer  organizations  in  the  project  zone that  have  been  promoted  by government  agencies  and  other 
entities. The presence of these farmer groups and organizations can contribute to the adaptive capacity of 
the  communities.  By  leveraging  the  knowledge  and  expertise  of  these  groups,  the  community  can  better 
respond to the challenges posed by climate change and other environmental pressures. 
- Existing adaptive practices 
The  Government  already  established  a  group  of  volunteers  called Masyarakat  Peduli  Api (Fire  Care 
Community)  in  each  village. However,  due  to  limited  funds,  these  groups  are  often  inactive.  The  main 
objective of these groups is to assist the Government when fires occur in villages that are close to the forest 
area. Other existing adaptive practices at the community level include home gardens and intercropping in 
rice fields, which helps diversify community livelihoods and food and income sources. 
- Access to finance 
Access  to  finance  remains  a  significant  barrier  for  communities  to  become  resilient  to  climate  change, 
particularly for farmers who often rely on high-interest loans from middlemen. This lack of financial support 
limits livelihood options, especially for poor families whose businesses are not bankable. 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (GL1.3) 
As described in the Theory of Change in Section 2.1.11, the project aims to conserve the remaining forest, 
restore  ecosystem  function,  protect  biodiversity,  and  improve  the  well-being  of  communities  to  become 
 
111
 BMKH 3-Days Weather Early Warning https://www.bmkg.go.id/cuaca/prakiraan-cuaca-tigaharian.bmkg?lang=EN  
Fire  Danger  System  on  Forest  Fire  and  Other  Land  Use  [SPARTAN]  by  BMKG https://spartan.bmkg.go.id/peta-
interactive/

 Yayasan Puter (2022). Preparation of Empowerment Program in Affected Livelihood Groups Ecosystem Restoration

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
195 
resilient to climate change. Among others, it aims to achieve improved ecosystem quality and resilience, 
biodiversity conservation and protection, and sustainable, resilient livelihoods. 
To  achieve  these benefits of  increased  resilience and  adaptation of  ecosystems,  biodiversity,  and 
livelihoods to climate change, the project will implement the following measures that will assist communities 
and  biodiversity  to  adapt  to  the projected climate  change impacts. These  activities  have  been  integrated 
into the project’s causal model as reflected in its Theory of Change. 
Ecosystems and Biodiversity 
- Conservation and restoration of habitats of threatened species and their connectivity 
The project will enhance the resilience of ecosystems and biodiversity through conservation and restoration 
of peatlands and mangrove forests. These ecosystems naturally regulate hydrological flows, buffer against 
coastal flooding, filter freshwater lenses, and reduce fire risk – functions that will be increasingly important 
under climate change. In particular, the project will contribute to the adaptive capacity of the three trigger 
species  (orangutan,  proboscis  monkey  and white-bearded  gibbon) by  preserving  and increasing  habitat 
connectivity. The project will avoid the conversion of the project area to oil palm plantation and will restore 
the degraded ecosystems and habitat. 
Specifically, under Output 1.4.2, the project will implement mangrove restoration and will establish sediment 
traps to prevent coastal abrasion in the mangrove area. These activities will contribute to the preservation 
of habitat for the endangered Proboscis monkey. Under Output  1.4.4, the project will implement planting 
and assisted natural regeneration of degraded peatland to restore some of the degraded peat forest. This 
will contribute to the preservation and increase of habitat for the endangered Bornean orangutan and other 
threatened species present in the project area and surrounding region. In its restoration and reforestation 
efforts, the project will only use native tree species and will prioritize species that are adapted to climate 
change.  For  example,  the  Balangeran  tree  (Shorea balangeran)  has  been  found  to  have  relatively  high 
survival  rates  and  tolerance  to  saturated  conditions,  indicating  a  degree  of  resilience  to  environmental 
stressors such as flooding.
113
 
- Fire prevention 
Under Outcome 1.2, the project will implement various fire prevention and management activities that will 
contribute  to  the  preservation  of  the  mangrove  and  peat  ecosystems  that  provide  habitat  for threatened 
species. As  part  of the forest  fire  mitigation, PT  PUM will  install an  Automatic  Weather  Station  (AWS)  to 
measure and monitor weather variability such as; precipitation, temperature, soil moisture, and water level 
and will establish a fire danger rating system (FDRS) for the project’s early warning system. Furthermore, 
the  project  will  establish  a  fire  patrol  team,  which will  collaborate  closely  with  the  community  firefighting 
team  under  Output  3.2.1 (Masyarakat Peduli Api). The  project will  reactivate  these  groups  of  volunteers 
and will provide them with the necessary skills for fire suppression. The project will also provide firefighting 
equipment  and  fire  watch  tower  infrastructure. Finally,  the  project  will  plant trees  species along  project 
boundary of the concession that serve as firebreak trees in peatland areas across Kalimantan. 
 
113
 Tata HL, et al. (2022). Flooding tolerance of four tropical peatland tree species in a nursery trial. PLOS ONE 17(4): 
e0262375. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0262375 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
196 
- Peat rewetting 
Under Output  1.3.1, to reduce vulnerability  of the  peat  ecosystem  to  climate  change  and  in  particular 
episodes  of  droughts  under  El  Niño while  also  reducing  greenhouse  gas  emissions,  the  project  will 
implement canal  blocking  and  peat  rewetting activities.  Under Output  1.3.2 the peat  water  level will  be 
monitored. This will support adaptive capacity by maintaining peatland ecosystem services and ecological 
structure and functions. 
- Biodiversity conservation and monitoring 
Under Outcome  2.1,  the  project  will implement  biodiversity  conservation  and  monitoring  activities. In 
particular, it will establish patrol teams and will adopt the SMART Patrol System to collect and monitor data 
on the three trigger species and biodiversity in general in the project area. This will ensure that the project 
identifies any threats to biodiversity, monitors the effectiveness of its conservation strategies, and is able 
to make adjustments as needed and be adaptive to change. 
Local Communities 
- Livelihood diversification and other adaptive practices 
The  project will  implement  several  strategies  to diversify livelihoods  and income  sources and implement 
other  adaptive  practices  related  to  community  livelihoods.  Under Output  3.1.1 the  project  will  develop 
alternative, resilient livelihoods for affected communities, in particular women and vulnerable groups such 
as  the  poor.  It  is expected that  this  will  increase the adaptive  capacity  of  local  communities  to  climate 
change. Initial options for sustainable livelihood development have been identified in consultation with local 
communities and include agroforestry, mangrove honey, local coffee, duck and chicken rearing, and small-
scale  fishponds.  The  activities  cover  training,  start-up  capital,  and  technical  assistance. When  selecting 
agroforestry crops in consultation with local communities, the project will take into account climate change 
considerations building on both scientific and local knowledge. For example, tree species will be selected 
that  are  resistant  to  flood  and  drought. The  project  will  also  build  on existing  adaptive  practices  at  the 
community level such as home gardens and intercropping in rice fields, which helps diversify community 
livelihoods and food and income sources.  
Under Output 3.1.3, the project will help increase access to marketing of agricultural products. The project 
will  support  enhanced  marketing  and  market  access  for  agricultural  products  such  as  honey  and  coffee. 
This will enable local communities to increase and diversify their income sources.  
Under Output 3.2.2 the project will promote sustainable, resilient agricultural practices. Best practices will 
be introduced such as crop rotation, integrated pest management, agroforestry, and tillage management. 
This  will  support  the ability  of the agricultural system to adapt to accelerating environmental change and 
will enhance resilience to droughts, pests, and other climate change impacts.  
Finally,  as  part  of  the  adaptive  management  approach,  the  project  will  regularly  discuss  climate  change 
impacts  with  local  communities  and  identify  additional  measures  that  can  be  integrated  into  the  project 
activities to ensure that they are adaptive to climate change. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
197 
- Farmer and community organization 
The project will also contribute to the adaptive capacity of local communities by strengthening community 
organizations such as farmers groups and women’s groups. PT PUM will assist communities in establishing 
micro,  small,  and  medium  enterprises  (MSMEs) and  women’s  groups  as  an  additional  source  of  family 
income. It  is  expected  that  this  increased community  organization  will  enhance the  ability  of  local 
communities  to  generate,  receive,  and  disseminate  knowledge  and  information  in  support  of  appropriate 
adaptation options and influence their capacity to adapt to changing shocks, stresses and trends. 
- Access to weather/climate information/early warning 
The information provided by the AWS and FDRS systems will be published for the community to increase 
their awareness of climate change issues. 
- Access to finance 
Under Output  3.1.2 the project  will  enhance  access  to  financial  resources  through  a  revolving  fund 
mechanism   that   helps   households,   especially   vulnerable   groups,   develop   sustainable   alternative 
livelihoods and income sources. A revolving fund will be made available to local livelihood groups, including 
women’s  groups,  and/or  MSMEs.  The  targeted  groups  of  the  program  are  low-income  households, 
women’s groups, and villagers in general. It is expected that these activities will contribute to strengthening 
the resilience of local communities to climate change. 
Indicators for Adaptation Benefits 
The  following  indicators for  adaptation benefits  for  biodiversity and  communities have  been  incorporated 
into the  monitoring  plan (Table 3-49). As  described  in Sections 4.2.1 and 4.4.1,  these  benefits  will  be 
monitored  annually  as  part  of  the  project’s  monitoring  plan.  The project’s  annual community  impact 
assessment will  include an  evaluation  of  the  impacts  by  the  affected  communities through  community 
meetings  and  interviews. In  this  way,  community  feedback  will  be  incorporated  through  participatory 
monitoring to evaluate the effectiveness of adaptation measures.

4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CM1.1) 
Administratively,   the   project   area   lies in two regencies,   East   Kotawaringin   and   Katingan.   In   East 
Kotawaringan Regency there is only one village located in the project zone, namely Satiruk Village, part of 
Pulau Hanaut Sub-district. In Katingan Regency, 12 villages of Katingan Kuala Sub-district are included in 
the  project  zone.  Historically,  the  project  zone  area  was  the  territory  of  the  Banjar  Sultanate  before  the 
Dutch East Indies Colonial Period. During the Indonesian independence period, this area was included in 
East  Kotawaringin  Regency,  until 2002,  when  the  region  was  divided into  three regencies,  namely  East 
Kotawaringin Regency, Katingan Regency, and Seruyan Regency.  
The  formation  of  settlements  in  the  project  zone  area occurred  in two  phases. The  first  phase was the 
formation of settlements by Banjarese people who migrated from South Kalimantan Province to seek new 
locations for fishing and timber. In the second phase, transmigration villages were formed in the 1980s by 
the  Indonesian  government. The  majority  of  the  transmigrants  came  from  Central  Java,  East  Java, and 
West Java, although there was also a small number of local transmigrants who came from areas around 
Central Kalimantan Province. The following villages are classified as transmigrant and non-transmigrant or 
local villages (Table 4-1). 
Table 4-1. Villages around the project areas distributed by village history 
Transmigrant Villages Local Villages 
Bangun Jaya Selat Baning 
Singam Raya Kampung Keramat 
Bumi Subur Kampung Tengah 
Subur Indah Satiruk  
Jaya Makmur Pagatan Hilir 
Makmur Utama Pagatan Hulu 
 Sungai Kaki 
4.1.1.1 Well-Being Information 
Livelihood 
The  livelihoods  in  the  villages  can  be  categorized  into  two main  groups: transmigrant  villages  are 
characterized by wet-rice farmers, while local villages have diverse livelihoods with the primary livelihood 
being fishermen. The main paddy producing transmigrant villages are Makmur Utama, Subur Indah, and 
Jaya  Makmur. Paddy  rice  in  transmigrant  villages  became  the  main  livelihood  in  accordance  with  the 
development  planning  of  the  Indonesian  government  during  the  early  development  of  transmigration 
villages  in  the  1980s.  The  success  of  paddy  field  development  has  made  these  villages  a  source  of  rice 
supply in Katingan Regency and beyond; these villages are known as the rice barns of Katingan Regency.  
Livelihoods  in  the  local  villages  are  dominated  by  fishermen,  although  there  are  some  villages  with  rice 
paddies  such  as  Kampung  Tengah  and  Kampung  Keramat.  Other  occupations  found  in  project  zone 
villages  are  field  farmers,  garden  farmers,  craftsmen,  traders,  laborers,  teachers,  farmers  (goats,  cows, 
chickens and ducks), loggers, and honey seekers. Some occupations affected by the project are loggers, 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3

Income 
The majority of the villagers in the project zone are engaged in multiple livelihoods, meaning that they are 
not  dependent  on  one  livelihood.  This  provides  additional  income  for  each  household,  increasing  the 
average    monthly    household    income.    The    average    monthly    household    income    reached    IDR 
1,886,397/month, with the lowest income in Satiruk Village averaging IDR 266,667/month and the highest 
income in Subur Indah Village averaging IDR 2,900,000/month. This income calculation is based on several 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION:

Access to clean water and sanitation is a major life support factor in the villages in the project zone, which 
is  dominated  by  river  and  peat  ecosystems.  Access  to  clean  water  and  good  sanitation  is  a  challenge 
because the characteristics of rivers and peat make it difficult for residents to get good quality water. Most 
villagers utilize refill water, rainwater and river water for drinking water. However, villagers face difficulties 
in obtaining refill water because the villages are only accessible by river, which makes refill water expensive. 
Rainwater is the main source for cooking and bathing. 
Regarding bathrooms and toilets, the majority of villagers use their own bathrooms in their homes. However, 
there  are  still  many  residents  who use  the  river for  bathing  and as  a  toilet.  Based on  the project survey, 
22.7% of  the  population still  use the  river  as a toilet.  This  practice  is  an  example  of  unhealthy  sanitation 
practices and can have an impact on public health and damage the water ecosystem. 
Education 
The following table shows the total educational facilities in the project zone between 2010 and 2020. In the 
last 10 years, there has been an increase in the number of schools. Namely, six elementary schools, two 
junior high schools, and three senior high schools have been added. The ease of reaching public education 
facilities is the main factor determining educational attainment in the villages. There are two villages with 
limited access to basic and mid-level education, namely Sungai Kaki and Makmur Utama village. All villages 
have limited access to higher education.

Health 
The  table  below  details  the  number  of  healthcare  facilities  and  personnel  in  the  project  area. Healthcare

service posts (Posyandu). If a village has a public health center, there will not be an auxiliary health center, 
and vice versa. An integrated service post is a healthcare community basis run by volunteers called village 
health  workers  or  cadres.  The  services  of  integrated  service  posts include  mother  and  child  health 
monitoring, socialization of birth control, child immunization, and other simple community health monitoring. 
Each  village  has  at  least  one  healthcare  facility  and  each  village’s  health  facilities  are  supported  by 
healthcare professionals. The nearest hospitals are in Sampit, Kasongan and Banjarmasin, and it takes 5-
7 hours to reach them by boat.

4.1.1.2 Community Characteristics 
Culture and Traditions 
There are two main ethnic groups in the project zone: the Banjarese and the Javanese. The Banjar ethnic 
group  originates from  southern  Kalimantan;  its history  comes  from  the  Banjar  Kingdom  in  the  Martapura 
and Barito River areas. The Banjarese are known for their hardworking and diligent nature. They are also 
widely known as successful traders in the regions of southern and central Kalimantan. The language used 
in daily conversation is Banjar, while to communicate with others they use Indonesian.  
The Banjarese in certain locations still apply traditional customs and culture in their daily lives. One of the 
traditional ceremonies that is still carried out is Ngaju, a traditional ceremony in Banjar society that involves 
activities  such  as  cleaning  the  village,  purifying  river  water  and  honoring  ancestors.  In weddings, the 
Banjarese still  use  traditional  clothing  called  Baju  Bodo  for  women  and  Baju  Kurung  for  men. Another 
cultural treasure of the Banjar is a record book of the Banjar people of the past called Hikayat Banjar. This 
book contains heroic, mythological and historical stories of the Banjar Kingdom.  
The Javanese are one of the most dominant ethnic groups in Indonesia, originating from the island of Java. 
The transmigration program of the Indonesian government has contributed to the broad distribution of the 
Javanese  population  in  the  country.  In  daily  activities  the  language  used  is  Javanese  while  to  relate  to 
others they use Indonesian. The richness of Javanese culture is reflected in traditional arts such as wayang 
kulit, wayang orang, and gamelan music. The Javanese batik motif which is Indonesia’s national dress has 
been designated as a world cultural heritage by UNESCO.  
History and Mythology  
The Banjarese have a written cultural heritage in the form of the Banjar Hikayat. One of the contents of this 
hikayat is the history of the regions around South Kalimantan and Central Kalimantan. In the Banjar Hikayat, 
it is told that Katingan Regency was included in the Banjar Kingdom’s territory in the 17
th
 century. At this 
time,  the  Banjar  Kingdom  was  still  influenced  by  Hindu-Buddhist  religions  from  kingdoms  in  Java  and 
Sumatra.  The  area  of  Katingan  Regency  at  that  time  consisted  of  two  regions,  Mendawai  and  Katingan, 
each led by a Sakai Minister. At that time, Mendawai was a transit point for Banjar traders on their way to 
sail to the Mataram Kingdom in Java.  
The Banjarese mythology  and  folklore closely relate to nature. One of the mythological stories related to 
rivers is the story of a Naga-shaped creature. Naga is a mythological creature shaped like a giant snake 
that  in  Banjar  culture  is  considered  the  protector  of  water,  especially  rivers  and  lakes.  It  is  said  that  this 
creature has a role in maintaining the balance of nature and protecting water sources that are important for 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
207 
the lives of the Banjar people. The appearance of the Sasamba Dragon is believed to be a sign of impending 
disaster in the region. 
The  Javanese  have  a  long  history  in  the  history  of  Indonesia,  starting  from  the  Tarumanegara  Kingdom 
located in West Java and Banten to the remaining kingdoms on Java Island today in the Sultanates of Jogja 
and  Solo.  This  long  history  began  with  the  influence  of  Hinduism  and  Buddhism  from  kingdoms  in  India, 
followed by the arrival of Arab traders who brought Islamization. The Javanese in the project zone are a 
group  of  Javanese  people  who  moved  from  Java  Island  through  the  transmigration  program  of  the 
Indonesian government between 1982 and 1985.  
Javanese mythological stories are found in the shadow puppet art that adopts the stories of Mahabarata 
and Ramayana. One of the most famous characters in wayang kulit is Semar who is an original Javanese 
puppet character. Semar is a combination of small people and gods because he is a god who transforms 
himself into a human being in the world. One of the most famous gods in Java in relation to nature is Dewi 
Sri, the goddess of fertility who is said to sacrifice herself to become rice for human life. In some areas of 
Java, traditional ceremonies are still performed at harvest time to honor Dewi Sri’s sacrifice. 
Government Structure and Customs  
In  the  administration  of  government  at  the  village  level,  there  are  three  main  organizations:  the  Village 
Government,  the  Village  Council  (Badan  Permusyawaratan  Desa, BPD), and  the  Mantir  Adat  Village 
Institution  (Mantir).  The  Village  Government  as  the  executor  of  development  programs  in  the  village 
functions  as  an  executive  agency; the  BPD  carries  out  supervision  of  the  Village  Government  as  a 
legislative function; and the Mantir carries out the function of implementing customary rules governing the 
life  of  villagers.  The  Village  Government  has  a  dominant  role  as  an  institution  that  has  the  mandate  to 
implement village development programs. The Village Government has community institution partners such 
as Rukun Tetangga (RT) or neighborhood, Rukun Warga (RW) or hamlet, Pemberdayaan Kesejahteraan 
Keluarga (PKK) or  family  welfare empowerment, Karang  Taruna (Youth  Organization), Pos  Pelayanan 
Terpadu (Posyandu) or Integrated Service Post, and Lembaga Pemberdayaan Masyarakat Desa (LPMD) 
or Village Community Empowerment Institution.  
The Mantir as the implementer of customary rules assists the village government as a partner in maintaining 
harmonious  relations  between  residents.  A  Mantir  is  a  person  who  has  in-depth  knowledge  of  customs, 
traditions, and customary rules that regulate social norms among villagers. Mantirs come from the customs 
of the Dayak people, but Mantirs are also found in transmigration villages inhabited by the Javanese and 
Sundanese since this apparatus has become part of formal bureaucracy in Central Kalimantan Province. 
Problems of social conflict between villagers are dealt with by the mantir as a conflict mediator before being 
referred to the police, such as land ownership disputes, infidelity, and fights between villagers. The mantir 
also  functions  as  an  institution  that  provides  consultation  for  the  village  government  in  organizing 
development and planning.

CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3

Ethnicity and Religion 
The formation of settlements, which occurred in two phases, resulted in the establishment of two prevalent 
ethnic groups in the project villages. Phase 1 villages are inhabited by the Banjarese, while transmigration 
villages are inhabited by the Javanese and Sundanese. The Banjarese are an ethnic group who originated 
from South Kalimantan, especially the Martapura and Barito River areas. The Javanese and Sundanese 
are from the island of Java, with the Javanese originating from central and eastern Java and the Sundanese 
from western Java. Apart from these two main ethnic groups, there are other minority groups, namely the 
Dayak, Bugis, Lombok, Batak, and others. Indonesian is a unifying language used for inter-ethnic relations 
and in public spaces. Meanwhile, among themselves, ethnic groups usually use their respective languages.  
 
130
 Central Bureau of Statistics.

The predominant religion of the villagers in the project zone is Islam. Another religion that is quite widely 
practiced is Protestant Christianity. In addition, some people practice Kaharingan Hinduism, which is  the 
religion of the Dayak on the island of Kalimantan, especially in Central Kalimantan and East Kalimantan.

Lingkungan, IKL),  economic  resilience  index  (Indeks Kualitas Ekonomi, IKE),  and  social  resilience  index 
(Indeks Kualitas Sosial, IKS)  for  each  village.  There  are  five  categories  within  VDI;  however,  in  villages 
within the project area, there are only two groups of villages: the developing villages with a VDI score within 
a range of 0.5989 to 0.707 and the underdeveloped villages with an average VDI score of 0.4907 to 0.5989.

4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (CM1.1) 
The social groups described in Section 4.1.1 consist of groups based on ethnicity, religion, and livelihood. 
The biggest differences in inter-group relations are based on ethnic groups and livelihoods, as well as the 
geographical distance between villages. Javanese and Sundanese, who primarily rely on rice farming, are 
located in transmigration villages, while the Banjarese, who depend on fishing and trading, are located in 
local  villages.  The  relationship  between  these  groups  runs  harmoniously  and  there  is  mutual  economic 
cooperation.  No inter-ethnic  discrimination or  conflict  has occurred  because  of  the  relationship  that  has 
blended in daily life. The Banjar people need rice from the Javanese, and the Javanese people need fish 
and  goods  from  the Banjar  people.  Despite  the  ethnic  and  livelihood  differences,  these two predominant 
groups share a common religion.  
Other  groups  not  mentioned  in 4.1.1 are  women’s  groups,  youth  groups,  coffee  farmer  groups,  honey 
seeker groups,  cattle  breeder  groups,  trader  groups,  and  religious  groups.  The  women’s  groups  in  the 
villages are  Family  Welfare  Empowerment  (Pemberdayaan Kesejahteraan Keluarga, PKK)  and  Women 
Farmers  Group  (Kelompok Wanita Tani, KWT). PKK  is  a  group  of  housewives  who  are  partners  of  the 
village  government  and  are  engaged  in  community  empowerment, while  KWT  is  a  women  farmer  group 
engaged  in  agricultural  development  by  women  in  the  village.  KWT  is  under  the  guidance  of  the  village 
government and the Ministry of Agriculture.  
The youth group in the villages is called Karang Taruna, a group that partners with the village government, 
assisting  in  youth  activities  organized  by  the  village  government  and  in  village  ceremonial  activities. 
Religious groups include the recitation group and the mosque board, both of which play a role in religious 
activities  in  the  village.  Some  of  these  groups  were  formed  formally  and  some  informally.  Relationships 
between groups are mutually cooperative for several joint activities such as major religious activities held 
in the village. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
211 
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CM1.2) 
The  project  aims  to  protect  and  improve  the  quality  of  high  conservation  values  (HCVs)  that  support 
community  well-being in the  project zone. To  this  effect,  the project  identified  areas  that  provide  critical 
ecosystem services (HCV 4) and areas that are fundamental for the livelihoods of communities (HCV 5) as 
follows. Sites that are critical for the traditional cultural identity of communities (HCV 6) were not identified 
in PT PUM concession and the project zone based on the HCV assessment and consultations with local 
stakeholders. 
4.1.3.1 HCV 4: Ecosystem Services 
The project team identified areas that provide basic ecosystem services in critical situations. The detailed 
description is provided below: 
High Conservation 
Value 
HCV 4: Rare and endangered ecosystems in the form of secondary forest and 
shrubs remaining in the project area (Peat Swamp, Freshwater Swamp, Beach 
Forest, and Mangrove and Intertidal Swamp) 
Qualifying Attribute There are two components of HCV 4 that are found in the project area: 
 
HCV 4.1: Protection of Water Catchments 
The mixture of freshwater swamps and riparian areas within the project area 
resulted in the uniqueness of peat swamps. In addition to storing water and 
preventing floods, peat swamps play an essential role in the environment as 
water reservoirs. The peat areas within the project concession area are the 
result of deposits of organic matter and stagnant water with impeded drainage 
on depressed interior soils. The nutrients in this form of peat are derived from 
mineral soil layers at the bottom of the basin, river water, plant remains, and 
rainwater. These areas are important to the communities for providing water 
supply for daily household activities and agriculture. 
 
HCV 4.3: Areas that function as natural barriers to the spread of forest or 
ground fire 
In addition to serving as a water catchment, water supply, and flood 
prevention, the peat swamp area also serves as a natural firebreak, and 
therefore preventing the negative impacts of peat fire to the communities (e.g., 
negative health impacts from pollution of the fire haze, spread of fire to 
communities’ agricultural fields). The secondary forests, rivers, and their 
borders serve as a barrier against the spread of ground and forest fires, thus 
meeting the requirements of HCV 4.3. 
Focal Area The area containing HCV 4 is shown in Map 4-1; each component of HCV 4 is 
represented in the legend of the map. 
 
Table 4-8. The area of HCV 4 (HCVA 4) within the project area. 
HCV 4 Eastern Part (ha) Western Part (ha) Total (ha) 
HCV 4.1 12,399.30 2,191.37 14,590.66 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION:

4.1.3.2 HCV 5: Basic Needs of Community 
Humans in living their lives need various types of goods and services to meet their needs. Some of these 
needs are basic needs and some are complementary. Areas that have an important function as a source 
of livelihood for local communities, especially in meeting basic needs, are areas that have HCV 5. 
High Conservation Value HCV 5: Forests, shrubs, rivers, and their borders, as well as fishing 
ground.  
Qualifying Attribute An HCV 5 study examines how a local community group relies on 
nature for fundamental necessities. If the services they provide cannot 
be replaced, resources are essential to meeting basic needs. Losing 
or damaging them will greatly impact the affected stakeholders. There 
are several HCV 5 attributes identified within the project area that are 
crucial for the community. They include:  
1. A fishing ground (ditches) where people go fishing within the 
project area.
