4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CM1.3) 
The  without-project  scenario  involves  the conversion  of  forest  and  land  into  oil  palm  plantation. In  this 
scenario, the communities around the project areas: 
1.
 
May have sold their land to the oil palm company for conversion to oil palm plantation. They would 
gain a one-time payment for selling their lands (including GRTT, Ganti Rugi Tanam Tumbuh). The 
amount of GRTT ranges from IDR 600,000 to IDR 1 million per hectare. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
214 
2.
 
May have become smallholder-partners with the oil palm company to convert their land into oil palm 
plantation. It is expected that they would become the company’s smallholder (plasma) suppliers, 
and they would receive revenue from the partnership. Usually, a farmer that has two hectares of 
land would receive an average of IDR 1.5 – 2 million per month after the fourth year of planting.  
3.
 
Villagers would benefit  from employment  opportunities  in  the oil palm company in  sectors  that 
match  their  educational  background.  The  majority  of  villagers  usually  work  in  harvesting  and 
maintenance  positions.  Work  status  can  be  as  casual  laborers  (Buruh  Harian  Lepas,  BHL)  or 
contract workers. BHL get a salary based on working days and daily targets with a salary range of 
IDR 125,000 per  day  and  an  average  of  20  working  days  a  month.  Monthly  income  can  be 
estimated at IDR 2.5 million. 
Although oil palm plantations provide economic income opportunities, there are several negative impacts: 
1.
 
Unequal financial capital. Land ownership is not equal among villagers. Some community groups 
own  large  tracts  of  land  above two hectares while others own only two hectares and some even 
less. Large landowners will benefit more from GRTT and plasma; other villagers may lose land and 
not get plasma. 
2. Increased immigration and competition with migrants. The large number of migrants as company 
workers may provide unhealthy competition for villagers. In many cases companies prefer migrant 
workers because they may work more with lower salaries. In the management sector, villagers will 
face challenges competing with migrants who possess higher levels of education and qualifications.  
3. Low  quality  of education  services.  The level  of  education  quality  will  not  increase  significantly 
because the company is not primarily concerned with the education of villagers. The low quality of 
education  is  due  to  the  difficult  road  access  from  the  city  and the  district  governments’  limited 
resources for education in the project zone. Without intervention from a third party, it will be difficult 
to improve education in the project zone.  
4.
 
Low access to health services. The low level of health services is due to difficult road access and 
limited government resources. The nearest community health center is located in the sub-district’s 
capital (Pagatan Hilir) which takes two hours to reach by river from the villages. Without third-party 
intervention, it will be difficult to improve access to health services. 
5.
 
Environmental degradation in the form of forest fires, illegal logging, reduced water sources, and 
coastal abrasion.  
6.
 
The without-project scenario would reduce the community’s ability to develop their own livelihood 
sources. At present, the community relies on paddy rice production. With the establishment of an 
oil palm plantation directly bordering their land, the community would experience an “edge effect” 
from the plantation, meaning that rice production areas would likely decrease. This situation would 
reduce the community’s resilience and self-sufficiency. 
4.2 Net Positive Community Impacts  
4.2.1 Expected Community Impacts (CM2.1) 
In the villages within the project zone, there are several community groups based on various factors such 
as livelihood, gender, age, ethnicity, and religion. This section describes the expected impacts of changes 
in  land  use  scenarios  on local communities, focusing  on four  community  groups as  well  as village 
communities in general.  

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
215 
To  assess the benefits, costs, and risks  of  the  project for community  groups, Focus  Group  Discussions 
(FGDs) were conducted by Remark Asia during the FPIC consultations and by Yayasan Puter during the 
livelihood assessment/Social  Impact  Assessment (SIA).  The  impacted  community groups  were gathered 
and consulted regarding the issues that might arise from the project. The existence of this project will have 
a  direct  impact  on  livelihood  groups  that  depend  on  income derived  from the  project  area. Impacts are 
anticipated to be mostly positive, as explained below. 
As explained elsewhere, fishermen and farmers will be allowed to continue their livelihood activities in PT 
PUM; thus, only logging and hunting

 will be prohibited.

2.

assistance to develop agriculture and non-timber forest products

relations and community and development staff. 
4. Wood  loggers  will  be  provided  with  training  assistance  and/or

and other businesses.

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (CM2.2) 
The project aims to provide net benefits for local populations. Nevertheless, potential negative community 
impacts could result from the project. Thus, it is necessary to design a plan to mitigate any negative impacts 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
218 
on the well-being of community groups. The potential negative impacts as well as their mitigation include 
the following: 
● Illegal Loggers: As a result of the project, the local people will no longer be able to log illegally. 
Hence, their income will be reduced. Community  members  who  were  conducting  these  activities 
for  their subsistence or  main  livelihood  will  be  offered  opportunities  to  participate  in  the project’s 
livelihood activities. They will also be considered for direct employment when opportunities emerge. 
Finally,  they  will  benefit  from  the project’s  other community  benefits  such  as  increased  health 
access, and training to help them transition away from illegal activity. 
● Local Community: During the project implementation, conflicts may occur owing to envy from local 
communities toward people coming from outside of the project zone. To mitigate this, the project 
proponent will ensure   that   the   employment   of   local   people   is   prioritized. Furthermore,  the 
compensation given to the local workers should not differ significantly from that given to a worker 
from outside the project zone. In addition, potential conflicts may arise between the community and 
the project proponent regarding land use. To address this, all activities and decisions involving the 
land and  resources  in the  project  area will involve affected  groups  and the  local  and  traditional 
leaders. 
● Farmers: Farmers in Sungai Rangkang from Satiruk Village have been identified as growing paddy 
rice in the western part  of  the project area, covering  an  area  of approximately 10  ha. They grow 
rice for  their  subsistence and for additional  income. In  the  eastern  part, there  are  approximately 
468 ha of rice paddy fields along the boundary of the concession, including the newly added areas 
under  SIMURP. Based  on  the  consultations  with  these  farmers,  it  was  decided  to  exclude  these 
rice paddy areas from the project area so that the farmers can continue to cultivate their land.

4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CM2.4)

the  future,  the project  will  ensure  that  these  values  are  not  harmed.  The  following  table  summarizes  the

4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
The project proponent will put in place a community monitoring plan to identify how the project affects the 
surrounding communities and other stakeholders. The community monitoring plan will help the project team 
understand how well each activity contributes to the project reaching its goals. Please refer to Section 4.2.1 
regarding  community  impacts  that  will  be monitored,  including well-being  impacts, risks  for  community 
members and impacts on women. These impacts are reflected in the monitoring indicators in Appendix 13: 
Monitoring  Plan. The  monitoring  plan  outlines the  indicators to  be  monitored, monitoring frequency, and 
data  collection  method. It  also  includes  indicators  on climate  change  adaptation  benefits  as  described  in 
Section 3.4.3. 
The  preservation  of  HCV  values  as  described  in Sections 4.1.3 and 4.2.4 will  be  measured  through  the 
following  indicators  that  are  included  in  the  monitoring  plan: Output 1.3.2 Indicator 1) Peat water level, 
Output  1.2.2  Indicator  4) Number of fire hotspots in the project area,  and Impact  Indicator  2) Estimated 
number of hectares of reduced forest loss in the project area measured against the without-project scenario. 
The community monitoring will apply both qualitative and quantitative approaches and will involve survey 
activities  and Participatory  Rural  Appraisal  (PRA) in Focus  Group  Discussions  (FGDs) with  community 
groups: 
a. The qualitative method is carried out with Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) activities. 
b. Quantitative  method  is  carried  out  with  survey  activities,  with  the  sampling  technique  chosen  in  this 
study being purposive sampling. 
In  addition,  during FPIC  a  community  monitoring  team  was  established  in  each  village  consisting  of 
community representatives that will monitor achievement of agreed project benefits in their village. 
4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CM4.3) 
The  community  monitoring  plan  will  be  disseminated  to  the  communities  and  other  stakeholders.  The 
project-related plans will be available in Indonesian along with the other project documents as mentioned 
in Section 2.3.2. In addition, the project team will also provide project progress information and results of 
the  monitoring which  will  be  displayed  on  the  project  website  and  disseminated  in  village  meetings  and 
information boards.  
4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (GL2.1) 
The  project  meets  the  CCB  criteria  for Exceptional Community  Benefits  as over  50%  of  villagers  in  the 
project zone are below the national poverty line of IDR 2,120,371/month per household

. Based on the

activities. 
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (GL2.2) 
In the short term, the community benefits include: 
1. Improved access to health services through the village health service improvement program. 
2. Improved access to higher education (for their children) through scholarship assistance programs 
and cooperation with universities.  
3. Increased access to high quality education through education assistance programs in the village.  
4.
 
Increased access to clean water through village water supply programs. 
5. Employment opportunities in the project through the company’s recruitment program. 
6. Hydrology  system  improvement  will help prevent  fires  and  floods  in  plantations  and  residential 
areas. 
In the long-term, the identified benefits are listed below: 
1. Protection of air and water quality: The project restores degraded wetlands and maintains forests 
that  play  an  important  role  in  maintaining  water  and  air  quality.  They  can  act  as  natural  filters, 
maintain  the  sustainability  of  the  water  cycle, prevent/suppress  land  and  forest  fires  which will 
reduce the negative health impacts of fire smoke/haze to local communities. 
2. Improving  the  quality  of  life and  resilience of  the  community  through  empowerment  programs  in 
education, health, and economy. 
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (GL2.3) 
The  project implementation  started with  an  FPIC  process  that  emphasized  transparency,  openness,  and 
community  participation. The  FPIC  process  openly  discussed risks  for  communities to  participate  in  the 
project including  potential  impacts  on  livelihoods (see Appendix 5:  FPIC  Consultation  Report). Village 
meetings  will  be  held regularly as  a  medium  for  socialization  and  evaluation  of  the  project  with  the 
community by promoting several principles such as: 
1.
 
Open dialog that provides opportunities for everyone to be actively involved. 
2.
 
Ensuring that all community groups are represented in each meeting, so that all groups have equal 
opportunities and involvement. 
3.
 
Ensuring that the decision-making process is carried out transparently and openly.  
Furthermore, a grievance redress procedure was put in place to provide a formal process for community 
members to raise concerns and complaints related to the project. 
4.5.4 Marginalized and/or Vulnerable Community Groups (GL2.4) 
The following marginalized and vulnerable groups were identified in the project zone:

Community Group 1 Women 
Net positive impacts The project  provides  benefits  of  strengthening  women’s  role

provision    of    training,    capital    and    mentoring, alternative

with   women. Socialization   will   be   conducted   prior   to   the 
implementation  of  the  program  to  explain  the  importance  of 
gender equality in households and villages in decision-making. 
Negative impacts There  is  no expected negative  impact  on  women  from  the 
project. The     project     will     conduct     regular     community 
consultations to ensure that no negative impacts result from the 
project activities. 
 
Community Group 2 Poor community members 
Net positive impacts Poor   people   in   the   villages will benefit   from employment 
opportunities in the project. In addition, business development 
assistance  will  be  provided  through  an  alternative  livelihood 
program that provides training, capital assistance and business 
mentoring.  
Benefit access Lack of accurate data and information may prevent the project 
from  identifying  vulnerable  people,  especially  the  poor. The 
project will closely involve  the  village  government,  community 
leaders  and  all community  groups to identify  the  poor  in  the 
villages and provide targeted assistance. 
Negative impacts Poor  people  who  previously  worked  in  logging  in  the  project 
area  may  be  affected  by  the project.  These groups  will  be 
identified in  the  community  consultations and  provided  with 
alternative livelihood programs.

Net positive impacts Smallholder groups will receive assistance through agricultural 
capacity   building   training   programs   and   financial   capital 
assistance for agricultural business development. 
Benefit access The project may face challenges in ensuring that poorer or more 
vulnerable  smallholders  equally  benefit  from  the  project.  To 
prevent  this, project  planning and  monitoring  is  carried  out 
through the involvement of all village groups to ensure that the

beneficiaries  are selected  fairly and  that  all  smallholders  have 
an equal opportunity to participate in the project. 
Negative impacts Smallholders are not negatively impacted by the project. The 
project will conduct regular community consultations to ensure 
that no negative impacts result from the project activities. 
 
4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (GL2.5) 
One of the objectives of the project is to strengthen the role of women in the community. To achieve this, 
several activities will be dedicated specifically to women’s groups in the community: 
1. Alternative livelihood   program that   provides   training   and   financial   assistance   for   business 
development of women’s groups.  
2. Training  program   on   the  involvement   of  women   in   decision-making.  This training  provides 
socialization  on  the  importance  of  involving  women  in  decision-making  that  often  ignores  issues 
concerning women.  
3.
 
Women’s  involvement  in all village  meetings,  such  as  in  FPIC  meetings,  Village  Forums,  and 
others.  
These activities are  designed  to  promote  the  role  of  women  in  the  villages.  Based on  social  studies 
conducted at the village level, participation of women in decision-making is still low. Women-related issues 
such as domestic violence (DV) remain inadequately addressed in discussions at the village level. On the 
other  hand, for some  livelihoods,  women are extensively involved  in  household  income  through  trade, 
fisheries and wet-rice farming. 
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (GL2.6) 
During the FPIC consultations, the project discussed project benefits and potential costs/impacts with the 
local  communities, and  identified  community  expectations  related  to the benefits  from  the  project. In-
principle agreements were reached on activities that will be implemented in each village to provide benefits 
to  local  communities. All  community  groups  have  been  involved  in  the  decision-making  process  as 
described  in Appendix 5:  FPIC  Consultation  Report. The  project  will  continue  to  transparently  discuss 
benefit sharing of the project with the local communities and plan and implement the project activities in a 
participatory manner. 
The use and distribution of benefits agreed with the communities will be regulated through village meetings 
agreed upon by all community groups, in order to ensure transparency and fairness in the benefits provided. 
This process will be led and monitored by the project team and local community representatives.  
4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (GL2.7) 
During  the  FPIC  process, the  benefits,  costs  and  risks  of  the  project were openly  and  transparently 
discussed  with all  affected community  groups,  as  described  in Appendix 5:  FPIC  Consultation  Report. 
Project  information  was delivered  through  methods  that  ensure that all  community  members  know  and 
understand the project.  

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
227 
To ensure that the information provided is understood by community members, several methods were used, 
namely: 
1. Agreement through FPIC about the project and the benefits, costs and risks that will be faced by 
the communities. Ensure that FPIC is agreed by all community members through representatives 
of all groups in the villages.  
2. Collaborative  implementation  of  project  monitoring  and  evaluation  with  community  members 
through  regular  village  meetings,  evaluations,  and  implementation  of the  grievance  redress 
procedure. Ensure that the community provides feedback on the implementation of the project and 
that this feedback is followed up to build a high level of community trust in the project.  
3. Implementation of  annual  reporting  on  project achievements  and  benefit  sharing  as  agreed. The 
meeting will be an annual communication event between the community and the project proponent. 
Reports will be submitted and received by all community members through group representatives 
throughout the community. 
 
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (GL2.8) 
The project will be implemented at the village level in collaboration with the village government and related 
institutions. Local community  involvement  in  the  project  is  developed  through participatory  planning, 
monitoring  and  evaluation  and  a  grievance  mechanism.  By  prioritizing  the  principles  of  FPIC,  the project 
will ensure information  disclosure  and  the  involvement  of  all  community  groups  in project  planning  and 
implementation.  
Community members can also be fully involved in project implementation through the recruitment of local 
staff, so that project planning and implementation initiatives can be monitored in depth. Where possible and 
in line with their work capacity and capability, local staff can fill middle and senior management positions, 
thereby further increasing the involvement of local staff in project planning and implementation. In the future, 
the  number  of  community  members  employed  in  the  project should increase  in  line  with  the  project’s 
development  of  local  human  resources. Please  also  refer  to  Section 2.4.1 regarding  the  project’s 
governance structure. 
4.5.9 Smallholders/Community Members Capacity Development (GL2.9) 
The project will develop a training and capacity building program for the local communities through training 
of local staff and community members. The training program for local staff employed in the project will seek 
to develop local staff to become more skilled so that they can advance their careers by occupying project 
management level positions.  
Community members  are  provided  with  training  for  economic  development  through  agricultural  capacity 
building   and   alternative   livelihood   development.   This   training   is   provided   specifically   to   affected 
communities in particular and community members in general. Women’s groups will be trained on gender 
equality  issues  at  the  village  level  with  a  focus  on  increasing  women’s  participation  in  village  decision-
making. Other training includes business development through alternative livelihood programs.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (B1.1) 
The eastern part of the PLUM project area is situated approximately 1.3 km south of Sebangau National 
Park (NP), a 568,700 ha
135
 intact tropical peat swamp forest. The Sebangau NP is considered one of the 
best  representatives of  wetlands  ecosystem  and  biodiversity  richness  in  Borneo.  A  biodiversity  survey 
conducted by Husson et al. (2018)
136
 identified at least 65 mammals, 172 birds, 46 reptiles, 11 amphibians, 
and  307  flora  species, of  which  44  species  are  classified  as  threatened  (Critically  Endangered/CR, 
Endangered/EN,  and Vulnerable/VU)  by  the International  Union  for  Conservation  of  Nature (IUCN) Red 
List. The national park is also known to be home to the largest population of Bornean orangutan (Pongo 
pygmaeus) (5,826  individuals)  in  Central Kalimantan  Province
137
. Approximately  one km  north  of the 
western  part  of the project  area  is  PT  Rimba Makmur Utama  (PT  RMU)’s  concession,  a  149,800 ha 
ecosystem  restoration  concession  which  is  a  habitat  for  67  mammals,  157  birds,  41  reptiles,  eight 
amphibians, and 314 flora species
138
. Located within the same landscape with Sebangau NP and PT RMU, 
the PLUM project area possesses similarities in its ecological and biodiversity characteristics. 
A biodiversity assessment conducted in 2022
139
 identified two main ecosystem types in the project area, 
namely the peat swamp forest ecosystem that dominates the eastern part and mangrove forest ecosystem 
that is found primarily in the western part. These ecosystems are important habitat for at least 367 species 
identified during  the  biodiversity  assessment, including 112 flora species  and  255 fauna species  (17 
mammal species, 119 bird species, 28 herpetofauna species, and 91 fish species). The project area is also 
home to at least five Bornean endemic species, including the Bornean skink (Dasia vittata). The number of 
species found in the project area is high considering only 34% of the project area is still forested, while the 
rest of the area is already deforested and degraded due to past logging activities and historical forest fires. 
Out of the 367 species recorded, 31 species are considered as threatened by the IUCN Red List, including 
the critically endangered Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus) and Sunda Pangolin (Manis javanica) and 
the  endangered  Proboscis  Monkey (Nasalis larvatus). 24  species  are listed in  Appendix  I  and  II  of  the 
Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES), indicating that 
these species are prohibited or restricted from international trade. A total of 33 species are protected by the 
Government of Indonesia (MoEF Regulation No. P106/2018). 
The following table shows  the  list  of  threatened  species  found  in  the  project  area,  along  with  the  CITES 
status and national protection status of each species. For a full list of species, please refer to the separate 
biodiversity survey report.

5.1.1.1 Ecosystem Description 
The  PLUM  project  zone covers  an  area of 75,280.54 ha that  includes  the two  parts  of the PT  PUM 
concession totalling 23,153 ha. At the start of the project, two ecosystem types were identified within the 
project zone, namely the mangrove ecosystem in the western part and the peat ecosystem in the eastern 
part  of PT PUM’s  concession  which is part  of  the  Borneo  Peat  Swamp  Forest  ecoregion (Olson, et al., 
2001). The  project  zone  is  predominantly  in  a  degraded  condition  due  to  historical  logging,  peatland 
drainage, and forest fires. Based on the land cover classification, the degraded condition was described as 
non-forest areas that consist of bare ground, open swamp, open water, paddy field, and wet shrub. 
Non-forest area 
In the non-forest  areas,  the plant  species  observed  on-site  were  dominated  by  Kelakai  (Stenochlaena 
palustris), Spikemoss (Selaginella sp.), Sword ferns (Nephrolepis sp.), Pteridium sp., and Glechnium spp. 
Species of grass (Scleria purpurascens, Hymenachne acutiguma) and elephant ear plants (Alocasia spp.) 
are also frequently found in non-forest areas. Several woody species such as Gelam (Melaleuca sp.), Tumih 
(Combretocarpus rotundatus), Pelantan (Alstonia sp.) and Jelutung (Dyera costulata) can also be found. 
Gelam is known as a pioneer species that is able to grow on disturbed land. A number of Gelam aggregate 
stands can be found in the previously burnt areas, some of which reach a height of more than 10 meters. 
This condition describes the ongoing rate of natural ecosystem succession. In addition to improving peat 
deposits, with shade from the gelam community, woody species that require shade, such as those from the 
Meranti species (Dipterocarpaceae), may grow in the future.

Peat swamp ecosystem 
The peat swamp forest includes primary and secondary forest, part of which has been exploited but is still 
in good condition and has a dense canopy cover. Fallen trees can be found at a number of points therein 
and a fairly easy-to-pass trail which is suspected to be an earlier exploitation.  
Plant species that dominate the peat swamp forest are Malam-malam (Diospyros areolata), Pisangpisang 
(Mezzetia parviflora), Bintangur (Calophyllum inophyllum), Katiau (Madhuca motleyana), and Jambu-jambu 
(Syzygium sp.). These species are generally found in the secondary forest and in abundance. In general, 
the forest is still in good condition with a dense canopy cover and trees of large diameters. Non-tree species 
that dominate the peat swamp forest include Snake fruits (Salacca sp.), Rotan-rotanan (Calamus spp. and 
Korthalsia spp.),  and  Talas-talasan  (Raphidophora spp.  and Homalomena spp.).  Woody  lianas  such  as 
Akar  kuning  (Fibraurea  tinctoria),  Bajakah  (Spatholobus sp.)  and  Kelawit  (Uncaria  gambir)  are  also 
frequently found in the peat swamp forest.

Mangrove ecosystem  
In the western part of the project area, primary mangrove forest, beach forest and peatlands can be found. 
Cemeti mangrove forest landscape has reached a climax succession; the mangrove stands are dominated 
by Rhizopora  stylosa which  is  even  and  dense,  with  trees  up  to  15  meters  high  and  more  than  20 
centimeters in diameter. Other types of mangroves that can be found include Sonneratia alba, Avicennia 
alba, and Avicennia marina. A large population of Nipa palm (Nypa fruticans) can be found 500 meters from 
the estuary. Meanwhile, other mangrove associates such as Cerbera odollam and Barringtonia asiatica can 
also be found.

Coastal ecosystem 
The Cemeti beach forest area is dominated by Coastal She-oak (Casuarina equisetifolia). Some individual 
pine trees can reach two meters in diameter. Other coastal plant species such as Coconut (Cocos nucifera), 
Butun (Barringtonia asiatica) and Bintaro (Cerbera odollam) also contribute to the beach forest community 
in  Cemeti.  There  is  a peat  ecosystem  100  meters  from  the  shoreline  of  Cemeti,  which  is  dominated  by 
Gelam (Melaleuca sp.) and Nipa palm (Nypa fruticans), some of which are 50 centimeters in diameter and 
more than 10 meters high.

5.1.1.2 Biodiversity Description 
5.1.1.2.1 Flora 
Information related to the flora species is carried out based on field assessment in the project area (Remark 
Asia, 2022). Sampling locations were determined using stratified purposive sampling based on ecosystem 
types in the project area. The ecosystem types in the PT PUM area consist of Forest Areas and Non-Forest 
Areas. Forest Areas are divided into Peat Swamp Forests and Mangrove Forests. A total of 19 plots were 
sampled which consisted of 14 plots in forest areas (seven plots in peat swamp forest and seven plots in 
mangrove forest) and five plots in non-forest areas. The survey method used was nested plot sampling and 
the size of  each plot was 400  m
2
 (20  m  x  20  m).  The sub-plot  size was 20 m  x  20 m  for  the  trees.  The 
nested sub-plot of 10 m x 10 m was created for the poles, 5 m x 5 m for the saplings, and 2 m x 2 m for the 
seedlings. 
The survey identified  at  least  112 plant species  in  the  project  area. Six of  these species  are  listed  as 
threatened by  the  IUCN  Red  List. Four species are  categorized as Vulnerable,  including  Balangeran 
(Shorea  balangeran),   Jelutung   (Dyera  polyphylla),   Meranti   Batu   (Shorea  uliginosa),   and   Punak 
(Tetramerista  glabra).  Meanwhile,  Meranti  Bunga  (Shorea  teysmaniana)  is  listed  as Endangered.  The 
Ramin  species  (Gonystylus bancanus)  has  been  designated  as Critically Endangered by  the  IUCN Red 
List. Ramin,  Meranti  Bunga,  and  Punak  trees  were  found  in  the  eastern  part  of the project  area  in  peat 
swamp forest. Jelutung trees were found in non-forest area of the eastern part of the project area. 
5.1.1.2.2 Fauna 
The fauna information in the project area is based on the field assessment in the project area. The sampling 
locations were determined using stratified purposive sampling based on the ecosystem types in the project 
area. Sampling locations were dominantly placed in the remaining natural habitats, namely forests and wet 
shrubs, since  these land  cover  classes were  the  main  habitats for  fauna  and  hence  would  increase  the 
probability  of animal  encounter/sighting.  However, observations were also  made  on  other  land  cover 
classes such as open swamps, bare grounds, and wetlands or aquatic habitats. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
235 
Standardized survey methods were used for each fauna taxon (e.g., line transect and reconnaissance walk 
for  mammals,  point  count  for  birds,  Virtual  Encounter  Survey  for  herpetofauna,  and trapping for  fish 
supplemented with fishermen interviews and fish market surveys). The survey identified at least 255 fauna 
species in the project area consisting of 17 species of mammals from 12 families, 119 species of birds from 
45 families, 28 species of herpetofauna from 14 families, and 91 species of fish from 57 families. From the 
255 fauna species identified in the project area, 25 species are classified as threatened by the IUCN Red 
List, seven species are categorized as CITES Appendix I, seven as CITES Appendix II, and 15 species are 
protected under Indonesian law (MoEF Regulation No. P106/2018). 
Birds 
Based on observations and identification of bird species in the project area, at least 119 bird species from 
45 families have been identified. The Ardeidae (11 species), Scolopacidae (10 species), Nectariniidae (7 
species),   Alcedinidae,   Cuculidae,   Timaliidae   families   each   had six species,   and   the   Accipitridae, 
Campephagidae,  and  Columbidae  families  each  had five species. The  highest  number  of  species  was 
found in the peat swamp habitat with 32 species, followed by 23 species in beach forest habitat, and 21 
species in the open swamp habitat. 
Observations  in  the  eastern part  of  the  project  area indicate  that  the  mangrove  forest  is  still  in  relatively 
good condition and is located adjacent to the Cemeti River estuary, where a stretch of mudflat is visible at 
low tide. There are at least 17 migratory species belonging to three distinct families: Scolopacidae, Laridae, 
and  Charadriidae.  These  migratory  bird  species  use  the  wetland  habitat  of  Cemeti River  estuary as  a 
temporary  stopover  to  recover  and forage before  continuing  their  migration  journey  or  returning  to  their 
original breeding grounds.  
Regarding  global  population  status,  seven  bird  species  identified  in  the  project  zone are categorized  as 
threatened on the  IUCN  Red  List including  the  Endangered  migratory  Far  Eastern  Curlew  (Numenius 
madagascariencis),  Greater  Green  Leafbird  (Chloropsis  sonnerati),  and  Javan  White-eye  (Zosterops 
flavus) (please  refer  to Table 5-1). There are nine species  categorized  as  Near  Threatened while the 
remaining are Least Concern. In addition, 10 species are included in Appendix II of CITES which regulates 
international trade, and 24 species are protected under Indonesian law (MoEF Regulation No. P.106/2018).

Mammals 
At  least  17 mammal  species  from  12  families were identified in  the  project  area.  The  Cercopithecidae 
(monkey)  family  had  the  most  encounters,  including  Long-tailed  Monkeys  (Macaca fascicularis),  Red 
Langurs (Presbytis rubicunda), Silver Langurs (Trachypithecus cristatus), and Proboscis Monkeys (Nasalis 
larvatus).  Apart  from  that,  the  Sciuridae  family  (squirrels)  is  the  second  most  common  mammal  family, 
including Plantain Squirrels (Callosciurus notatus) and Horse-tailed Squirrels (Sundasciurus hippurus). 
Under the global conservation status,  two  mammal  species  are categorized as Critically Endangered on 
the IUCN Red List of Threatened Species, namely the Southwest Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus 
wurmbii) and the Sunda Pangolin (Manis javanica); three as Endangered, namely the Proboscis Monkey 
(Nasalis  larvatus),   Bornean   White-bearded   Gibbon (Hylobates  albibarbis), and   Flat-headed   Cat 
(Prionailurus  planiceps); and  eight  as Vulnerable. Furthermore, a  total  of  seven  different  species  of 
mammals are categorized under CITES Appendix I and II and a total of nine mammal species are protected 
under Indonesian law (MoEF Regulation No. P106/2018). 
Four species were identified as endemic to Borneo, namely the Bornean White-bearded Gibbon (Hylobates 
albibarbis), Red  Langur (Presbytis  rubicunda), Proboscis Monkey  (Nasalis  larvatus),  and Southwest 
Bornean  Orangutan  (Pongo pygmaeus wurmbii). A  preliminary nest-count survey  was conducted during 
the  biodiversity  assessment to  estimate  Orangutan population density  in  the eastern  part  of  the project 
area. It was estimated that the Orangutan population density in the eastern part was 0.89 individual/km
2
.

individual/km

Herpetofauna 
Kalimantan  is  an  island  with  a  very  distinctive  biodiversity  and  is  known  to  have  high  endemic  species 
including herpetofauna. At least 28 species of herpetofauna from 14 families were recorded in the project 
area. The amphibians were from the order Anura (frogs and toads), while the reptiles discovered are from 
the order Squamata  (lizards  and  snakes). One  of  the  endemic  species  found  in  the  project  area  is the 
Borneo Skink (Dasia vittata). The project also recorded several species that are commonly found in peat 
ecosystem including Suck   Toad   (Pseudobufo  subasper), Baram   River   Frog   (Pulchrana  baramica), 
Blackwater  Mud  Snake  (Phytolopsis punctata), Reticulated Python (Malayopython reticulatus)  and King 
Cobra  (Ophiophagus hannah). King  Cobra  is  categorized as Vulnerable under  the  IUCN Red List. Two 
species  are  also  listed  in  CITES  Appendix  II,  namely  King  Cobra  and Common  Water Monitor  (Varanus 
salvator).

Figure 5-7. Photo of Reticulated Phyton (Malayopython reticulatus) recorded in the project area. 
Fish 
From observations within and around the study area, 91 fish species from 57 families were identified. 31 
fish species from  12  families  were  recorded  in  the  eastern  part  of  the  Katingan  River  System,  which  is 
dominated  by  peat  swamp,  freshwater  swamp,  and  tributaries.  Meanwhile,  46  species  of  fish  from  40 
families  were  discovered  in  the  western  part  of  the  project  area  (southwest  side  of  the  Katingan  River 
System), which contains estuary microhabitat types, mangrove swamps, creeks, and freshwater swamps. 
Out of 91 fish species, four stingray species are categorized as Endangered and Vulnerable species under 
the  IUCN  Red List. Whitespotted  Whipray  (Maculabatis gerrardi) was  categorized  as  Endangered,  while 
three other species Spotted Eagle Ray (Aetobatus ocellatus), Bengal Whipray (Brevitrygon imbricata), and 
Longtail Butterfly Ray (Gymnura poecilura) were categorized as Vulnerable. These stingray species were 
usually found  in  the  estuary  or  the river downstream  during storms  or  high  tides. Furthermore,  six  peat-
swamp  affiliated  species  were  all  categorized  as  Near  Threatened  including Channa  pleurophthalma, 
Sphaerichthys selatanensis, Paedocypris carbunculus, Kryptopterus macrocephalus, Ompok fumidus, and 
Silurichthys cf. phaiosoma. 
5.1.1.3 Threats to biodiversity 
The biggest threat to biodiversity in the project area is the threat of conversion to oil palm plantations. Based 
on the history of land management, the project area has received an oil palm plantation permit. This can 
also  be  seen  from  the  current  conditions in the  project  zone  where  there  are  already  operating  oil  palm 
plantations. If land use changes occur, the habitat for most of the existing biodiversity will be lost. 
Other threats to biodiversity in the project area originate from the livelihoods of communities in the project 
zone. The livelihoods of the people living in the project zone are still based on the use of land and natural 
resources. The main livelihoods of the community include rice farmers (69.1%), vegetable farmers (12.1%), 
fishermen  (10.1%),  fish  farmers  (3.5%),  loggers  (1.8%),  and  NTFPs collectors (3.3%)
141
. Community 
livelihoods that are still land-based can have an impact on the condition of existing biodiversity. With farming 
representing the largest proportion of livelihoods, the expansion of agricultural areas could pose a threat to 
the project area which is a biodiversity habitat. 
 
141
 Report for Preparation of an Empowerment Program for Livelihood Groups Affected by the Ecosystem Restoration 
Project PT PUM (Yayasan Puter, 2022) 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
239 
Regarding fish  populations in  the  project  area  and  project  zone,  the  application  of  unsustainable  fishing 
methods has potential negative impacts on fish populations. In the project zone, at least four species of fish 
were found that were included in the HCV category, including the Spotter Eagle Ray (Aetobatus ocellatus), 
Whitespotted   Whipray   (Maculabatis  gerrardi),   Bengal   Whipray   (Brevitrygon  imbricata), and Longtail 
Butterfly  Ray  (Gymnura  poecilura). Project  interviews indicated the  use  of  electric  generators  and  fish 
poison in several areas of the Katingan River System. This has the potential to reduce population fitness in 
the  affected  area.  Fish  larvae  and  microscopic  fish are  likely to  be  disturbed  because  they  are  more 
vulnerable  to  the  use  of  destructive  fishing  gear.  Overfishing may reduce  the  population  of  a  species. 
However, there is no data available on the fishing methods used by fishermen in the area. A precautionary 
approach is taken to determine overfishing as a threat. 
Another livelihood activity in the project zone that may affect biodiversity is logging. Based on the results of 
a study by Yayasan Puter (2022) in the project zone, loggers’ activities are driven by demand for wood at 
the community level. Loggers look for wood in the peat swamp forest area of the project zone. This logging 
activity in peat swamp forests disturbs the habitat of protected animals that are highly dependent on forest 
canopy  covers,  such  as Bornean Orangutans  (Pongo  pygmaeus),  Bornean  white-bearded Gibbons 
(Hylobates albibarbis), and Proboscis Monkeys (Nasalis larvatus). 
The types of wood sought by the loggers include wood from the Meranti group (Dipterocarpaceae family), 
Balangeran  (Shorea  balangeran),  Punak (Tetramerista  glabra), Gelam (Melaleuca sp.),  and  Ramin 
(Gonystyulus bancanus).  From  the  list  of  types  of  wood  sought,  only Gelam is  not  included  in  the rare, 
threatened  and  endangered (RTE) category.  Balangeran  and  Punak are  included  in  the Vulnerable 
category according to the IUCN Red List while Ramin is Critically Endangered. Several Meranti species in 
the  project  area  are  also considered  Endangered,  such  as  Meranti  bunga  (Shorea  tesymaniana), and 
several others are in the Vulnerable category, such as Meranti batu (Shorea uliginosa).

5.1.2.2 HCV 2: Ecosystems and Mosaic Ecosystems at the Landscape Level 
The  area  containing  HCV 2,  which relates to  the  ecosystems  and  mosaic  ecosystems  at  the  landscape 
level, has been identified in the project area. The following is a summary of HCV 2 found in the area: 
 
High Conservation Value HCV 2: Large natural landscapes with capacity to maintain natural 
ecological processes and dynamics. 
Qualifying Attribute The  peat  swamp  forests  in  the  project  area  and  the  project  zone  are 
ecologically  connected  with the  Sebangau  National  Park  landscape 
which covers  a  total  area  of  568,700  hectares.  In  addition  to  its  vast 
integrity, preservation of this area is secure because it is a conservation 
area whose forest cover will be preserved, which is important to support 
the movement of wild animals. 
Focal Area The peat swamp forest in the project area and the project zone.

saltwater  swamps.  There  are  regions  bordering  the  two  remaining 
forested and sustainable ecosystems, namely the peat and freshwater

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (B1.3) 
The without-project scenario would have an impact on both the flora and fauna and the communities living 
around the project area. The project area is a supporting area for the Sebangau National Park that still has 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
245 
rich biodiversity, being home to 17 mammal species, 119 bird species, 28 herpetofauna species, and 112 
plant species. A number of these species are classified as vulnerable, endangered, or critically endangered. 
If the without-project scenario is put in place, both the eastern and western parts of the project area would 
be turned into oil palm plantations. Land clearing would occur as the initial stage of the plantation activities. 
As a result, the value of the biodiversity of the forest would be lost, and the habitat of different animals and 
plants  would  be  turned  into  monoculture  plants  or  oil  palm  plantations.  This  would  cause  the  value  of 
biodiversity to decline or be completely lost.  
5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (B2.1) 
Under  the  project  scenario,  the  forest  in  the  project  area  will  be  protected  from  the  threat  of  planned 
deforestation   and   forest   degradation.   The   expected   biodiversity   changes   as   a   result   of   project 
implementation are summarized below: 
 
Biodiversity Element Natural Ecosystem 
Estimated Change Reduced factors that contribute to species extinction and biodiversity loss 
Justification of Change 
As a part of the project, there will be forest protection initiatives, which include 
several programs as listed in Section 2.1.11. These programs are expected 
to  have  a  significant  positive  impact  on  the  ecosystem  within  the  nearby 
project area, which will create a positive domino effect for the biodiversity in 
the area due to improved habitat conditions and connectivity. 
 
Biodiversity Element Flora and Fauna 
Estimated Change Gradual dispersal and increase of flora and fauna species in the project area. 
Justification of Change 
It is anticipated that the abundance of flora and fauna in the project area will 
increase due to the project activities. The positive impact of the project can 
be  determined  by  comparing  the abundance of  threatened  plants  and 
animals  in  the  project  area  to  the  number  previously  identified.  This  will  be 
accomplished  by  a  regular  patrol  team  that  will  patrol  the  project  area.  In 
addition, monitoring of fauna will be conducted to monitor the populations of 
threatened species. 
 
Biodiversity Element Forest Cover 
Estimated Change Reduced deforestation and forest degradation 
Justification of Change 
The project prevents the land use change that will lead to deforestation and 
forest degradation in the project area. This is accomplished by avoiding the 
conversion  of  the  forest  area  to oil  palm plantation,  by  restoring  mangrove 
forests, by planting degraded peat forests and other activities to protect the 
forests. Over time, the restoration efforts will result in greater forest cover. 
 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
246 
5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (B2.3) 
Based on the assessment conducted by the project team, there are no expected negative impacts of the 
project on biodiversity and ecosystems. Therefore, no mitigation measures are required. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (B2.2, GL1.4) 
Compared with the conditions under the without-project land-use scenario, the project scenario will provide 
high net positive biodiversity impacts. There are three main components of biodiversity in the project area 
that will be secured and sustained during the project implementation, namely (i) ecosystems, (ii) the species 
of  flora  and  fauna  especially  those that  are critically  endangered,  endangered,  and  vulnerable,  and (iii) 
forest cover. 
The remaining forest in both parts of the project area are critical to ensuring the viability of flora and fauna 
diversity, particularly protected species. Furthermore, some ecosystems serve multiple functions, such as 
the peat  forest,  which  not  only  provides habitat  for  protected  species  but  also  serves as  a  water  cycle 
regulator  and  carbon  store.  Mangrove  forests  also  provide  ecosystem  services  by  filtering  sediment  into 
the sea while preserving the island’s physical integrity. Even though the existing forest in the project area 
is degraded and fragmented, it has the potential to be restored, providing more habitat for flora and fauna. 
Restoration efforts will focus on protecting, restoring, and connecting fragmented forest patches, as well as 
biodiversity quality as measured by population density/nest of Orangutans and Proboscis monkeys within 
60 years of the project’s inception. Proboscis monkeys, unlike orangutans, prefer specific habitats, such as 
large  rivers  in  coastal  areas  that  have  been  extensively  converted  into  cultivated  and  residential  areas. 
Severe fires in 1997-1998 destroyed much of the habitat and hunting for this species is also a contributing 
factor to its population decline (IUCN, 2021). 
The net benefits and impacts on biodiversity are expected to be positive as a result of restoration activities 
that  will  be  implemented  in  the  project  area. The project’s  biodiversity  impact  goal  includes  activities  to 
significantly increase forest cover area (expressed in ha) by planting local (native) tree species combined 
with natural regeneration in shrubs and bushes. Planned protection efforts are another important activity, 
with  regular  patrols  to  minimize  or,  if  possible,  eliminate  disturbances  (habitat  encroachment,  fires,  and 
hunting) to the existence of threatened species. Furthermore, the project will implement peatland rewetting 
activities to keep the average groundwater level in a year at about 0.4 meters below the peat land surface. 
One of the activities is the construction of canal blocks (dams) to slow the rate of subsidence and maintain 
the  peat  water  level.  This rewetting  program  will  be  critical  in  maintaining  the  physical  stability  and 
productivity of peatlands, allowing restoration efforts to proceed. All of these efforts will provide a habitat 
extension and enhance connectivity of the project area to the larger forest areas in the project zone (e.g., 
Sebangau  National  Park  to  the  north) which  will  be  beneficial  for  the  long-term  viability  of threatened 
species population. 
 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (B2.4) 
Four categories of biodiversity-related HCV areas were identified in the project area, as described in Section 
5.1.2. They are namely: 
- HCV1: Species Diversity 
o Rare, threatened, and endangered species  

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
247 
o Endemic species 
o Areas that contain habitat for viable populations of endangered, restricted-range or 
protected species. 
o Areas that contain habitat of temporary use by species or congregations of species 
- HCV2: Ecosystems and mosaic ecosystems at the landscape level 
o Large natural landscapes with capacity to maintain natural ecological processes and 
dynamics. 
o Areas that contain two or more contiguous ecosystems  
o Areas that contain representative populations of most naturally occurring species  
- HCV3: Ecosystem and habitat 
o Rare and endangered ecosystem 
- HCV4: Ecosystem services 
o Protection of Water Catchments 
o Areas that function as natural barriers to the spread of forest or ground fire 
Protecting  biodiversity  is a  key focus  in  the  project  activities.  The  project contributes  to preserving  and 
improving the area and quality of forests and natural ecosystems, as well as connecting each HCV area. 
This  emphasis  on  preserving  and  improving  forests  and  natural  ecosystems  to  protect  them  will  have  a 
significant positive impact on HCVs 1-4. Infrastructure such as basecamps, guard posts, fire towers, and 
nurseries will be built outside of the critical ecosystems.  
5.2.5 Species Used (B2.5) 
The project’s restoration activities, which include planting and reforestation in the project area, will utilize 
native species. No non-native species will be used in the project area. The species used in these activities 
are mentioned in Section 2.1.11. 
5.2.6 Invasive Species (B2.5) 
Project activities will not result in the introduction of invasive species. 
5.2.7 Impacts of Non-native Species (B2.6) 
No  non-native  species  will  be  used  in  the  project  area. Therefore,  there  will  be  no  impact  of  non-native 
species on the project areas. 
5.2.8 GMO Exclusion (B2.7) 
No genetically modified organisms (GMOs) will be used in the project. In addition, planting activities do not 
make use of seeds derived from GMOs. 
5.2.9 Inputs Justification (B2.8) 
No  fertilizers,  chemical  pesticides,  biological  control  agents  nor  other  similar  inputs  will  be  used  in  the 
project’s restoration efforts. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
248 
5.2.10 Waste Products (B2.9) 
There  are  some  potential  waste  products  from  the  project activities. Thus,  it  is  necessary  to  reduce  the 
waste products that are generated. Waste generated by human activities (domestic waste) in field offices, 
basecamps, and guard posts is one example of potential waste generated by project activities. Domestic 
waste includes solid waste such as plastic waste, used cans, used plastic packaging, Styrofoam, paper, i 
and food waste, as well as liquid waste such as soapy water, detergent water, oil, and faeces. Moreover, 
there is the possibility of waste from operational activities such as used generator oil, used vehicle or engine 
oil, gasoline or diesel spills, and used batteries. In line with the company’s Environmental Management and 
Monitoring  Plan  (UKL/UPL),  the  project  will develop a Standard  Operating  Procedure  (SOP)  for  waste 
management.  The  project  will  apply  the  3R  concept  (Reduce,  Reuse,  Recycle),  which  involves  reusing 
waste  that  can  still  be  used  or  has  other  functions,  reducing  everything  that  causes  or  generates  waste, 
and recycling waste into a product or item that can be useful. 
 
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (B3.2) 
The project is designed to have no negative impacts on biodiversity outside the project zone as described 
under Section 5.2.1.  
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (B3.3) 
Net  offsite  biodiversity  benefits  are  expected  to  be  positive  due  to  the  connectivity  with  the  surrounding 
landscape, in particular Sebangau National Park. The project focuses on the protection of biodiversity and 
improvement  of  biodiversity  habitat,  especially  habitat  for  key-species  such  as  Orangutan,  Proboscis 
monkey, and Bornean white-bearded gibbon.  
Compared to the without-project scenario, the project scenario is deemed to provide positive biodiversity 
impacts in the project area and its surroundings. Considering the project activities in patrol and monitoring 
species   and   habitat,   the   project   will   provide   more   favourable   conditions for   species   and   habitat 
conservation. 
 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
The project intends to meet the Gold Level for exceptional biodiversity benefits (GL3), hence the indicators 
of the population threats of each trigger species are monitored through the Spatial Monitoring and Reporting 
Tool  (SMART)
142
.  Furthermore,  the  relative  density  of  the  species  population  will  be  monitored  through 
permanent transects and regular monitoring.  
The project has developed a monitoring plan with aims to provide biodiversity net benefits. The coverage 
of  monitoring  activities  includes  protection  of  remaining  forests through  regular  patrols and biodiversity

5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (B4.3) 
The results of biodiversity monitoring are disseminated in two ways. First, by distributing annual reports to 
relevant government stakeholders (MoEF, BKSDA
143
, the Provincial Forestry Agency, and Environmental 
Agency). Second, by consistently sharing information via social media and the project’s website. In addition, 
to  reach  the  village  community,  the  project  proponent  team  will regularly publish  information  sheets and 
display them on the village information boards. Information will also be disseminated orally in community 
meetings and  in  local  schools.  Sharing  information  with  stakeholders  is  one  of  the  mitigation  efforts;  the 
data  will  be  useful  for  the  stakeholders  to  be  able  to  guide  the  implementation  of  appropriate  mitigation 
measures. 
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
The project aims to achieve exceptional biodiversity benefits and be validated to the Gold Level based on 
the presence of ecologically important IUCN Red List species (GL3) in the project area. 
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (GL3.1) 
There are 31 species categorized as threatened by the IUCN Red List in the project area. Overall, there 
are three species with the status of Critically Endangered (CR), eight species listed as Endangered (EN), 
and 20 species with Vulnerable (VU) status. Besides, seven species are included in CITES Appendix I, and 
seven in  CITES  Appendix  II.  In  addition,  15  protected  species  according  to  Ministry  of  Environment  and 
Forestry Regulation No. 106/2018, four endemic mammal species and one endemic bird species to Borneo 
inhabit the area. A list of priority species can be seen in Table 5-1. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (GL3.2, GL3.3) 
As defined in the CCB Standards, trigger species must meet the criteria of “vulnerability” or “irreplaceability”. 
Vulnerability  is  defined  as  regular  occurrence  of  IUCN  Red  List  of  threatened  species  (species  listed  as 
CR, EN, or VU) in the project area, while irreplaceability is defined as the presence of 1-5% of a species’ 
global population (at any stage of the species’ life cycle) in the project area. The project’s initial biodiversity 
survey identified at least 31 species (13 mammals, seven birds, one reptile, four fish, and six plants) that 
are classified as threatened by the IUCN Red List in the project area and therefore meet the vulnerability 
criteria and are considered as trigger species (Table 5-1). The project selected three trigger species that 
are endemic to Borneo to be monitored more intensively (to population density level), namely the Southwest 
Bornean  orangutan  (Pongo pygmaeus wurmbii), Proboscis monkey  (Nasalis larvatus),  and  the  Bornean 
white-bearded  gibbon  (Hylobates albibarbis).  The  remaining  28  species  will  also  be  monitored  regularly 
using  standardized  protocols  (e.g.,  point  transect  surveys  for  birds,  camera  trap  surveys  for  mammals, 
permanent vegetation plots for plants, etc.) to determine species occurrence and abundance in the project 
area. 
 
143
 Central Kalimantan Natural Resources Conservation Agency. 

   CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
251 
Southwest Bornean orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus wurmbii) 
The Bornean orangutan is classified as CR on the IUCN Red List. Bornean orangutans have a large home 
range  (>560  ha  for  adult  males  and  250-300  ha  for  adult  females  in  peat  swamp  forest
144
 and  require 
relatively intact forest for survival, hence they are considered as umbrella species for other smaller-ranged 
species in peat swamp forest ecosystem. Initial population density estimate from orangutan nest survey in 
the eastern part of the project area is 0.89 individual/km
2
.  
Proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus) 
The Proboscis  monkey  is  classified  as  EN on  the IUCN  Red  List.  Proboscis  monkeys  primarily  reside  in 
mangrove   forest   and   riverine/riparian   forest   ecosystems
145
,   therefore   monitoring   the   species   could 
represent other species that inhabit the same ecosystems. The initial survey identified at least 16 individuals 
in  the  western part of  the  project area. However,  the  baseline  population  assessment  has  not  been 
conducted, therefore the project will assess the population of the species at the beginning of the project. 
Bornean white-bearded gibbon (Hylobates albibarbis) 
The Bornean  white-bearded  gibbon  is  classified  as  EN on  the IUCN  Red  List.  This  species  has  similar 
habitat requirements as the Bornean orangutan but has smaller home ranges (39-52 ha in Sebangau peat 
swamp forest
146
), which makes them more vulnerable to habitat disturbances. Gibbons also have a faster 
life history compared to orangutans
147
, enabling faster increase in population size. Consequently, gibbons 
would show faster positive or negative responses to habitat disturbances. The initial biodiversity survey has 
not estimated the population or population density of this species, but they were encountered in peat swamp 
forest ecosystem. 
All three species face threats of habitat loss from deforestation and forest fires. Management of these three 
species will represent other trigger species that inhabit similar ecosystems or face similar threats.

Without-project Scenario 
It is probable that the population will decrease without the 
project as existing threats still remain. In the baseline scenario, 
the area will be converted to an oil palm plantation which will 
reduce the natural habitat of the species. 
With-project Scenario 
The eastern part of the project area is situated adjacent to the 
Sebangau NP where the 2016 PHVA
148
 estimated a population 
of 5,683-5,826 orangutan individuals. Therefore, protecting the 
remaining forest from conversion to oil palm plantation and 
restoring deforested areas in the project area will provide a 
habitat extension and enhance connectivity to the NP which will 
be beneficial for the long-term viability of the population. It is 
expected that with the project, the population will remain stable 
or increase. 
Monitoring Plan 
Once every two years, the orangutan population in the project 
area will be monitored using the orangutan nest count survey 
method. Permanent monitoring transects will be established in 
the beginning of the project. Any direct or indirect encounter with 
the species during regular patrols will also be recorded. 
 
Trigger Species Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus) 
Population Trend at Start of 
Project 
The species is endemic to Borneo and classified as Endangered 
by the IUCN Red List. The initial survey recorded at least 16 
individuals in the mangrove forest in the western part of the 
project area. According to IUCN Red List, the population trend of 
the proboscis monkey is decreasing due to deforestation (e.g., 
agricultural areas, settlements, roads and infrastructure) and 
forest fires. 
Without-project Scenario 
It is probable that the population will decrease without the 
project as existing threats still remain. In the baseline scenario, 
the area will be converted to an oil palm plantation which will 
reduce the natural habitat of the species. 
With-project Scenario 
Proboscis monkeys inhabit the mangrove and riverine forests in 
the project area. Protection of the remaining mangrove forest 
and mangrove restoration in the project area will benefit the 
species from protected and improved habitat conditions 
(increased forest cover and food sources). Therefore, we expect 
that the population will remain stable or increase with the 
project. 
Monitoring Plan 
Annually, the population of proboscis monkey in the project area 
will be monitored using the river survey method along the river 
and tributaries. Permanent monitoring routes will be established 
at the beginning of the project. Any direct or indirect encounter 
with the species during regular patrols will also be recorded.
